The Red Page
Anzalian
Though it has been decided to cease pub-
lication of Historical Records of Australia,
published by the Commonwealth Parliament
and edited by Fredk. Watson, apparently one
more volume is to be issued. The ninth
volume is now published, dealing with de-
spatches to and from Governor Brisbane,
January, 1823-November, 1825. Sir Thomas
Brisbane’s administration of the colony was
probably the most momentous period in our
history. During the- four years of his
government the foundations of the modern
fabric of civil life were laid, and many
important principles received consideration.
This period saw the partial separation of
Tasmania; the formation of a deliberative
and advisory council to consider legislation;
the beginning of trial by jury; the removal
of the censorship of the press; the sale of
crown lands ; the establishment of the first
monopoly in the colony—the Australian
Agricultural Company, which subsequently
obtained a monopoly of coal-mining; while
many changes were under trial or considera-
tion, such as taxation by representation;
the separation of the two branches of the
legal profession; the reduction of licensed
houses; prohibition at Newcastle and Port
Macquarie; the f damming of the convict
stream; the open-air treatment of prisoners:
wages and piece-work for prisoners; and the
establishment of agricultural colleges.
Fredk. Watson puts many of these Changes
down to the reports of John Thomas Bigge.
But these momentous four years were but
a small part of Brisbane’s busy life. A
soldier who rose to the rank of general, a
personal friend of the Dube of Wellington,
present in 14 battles, 22 minor actions and
six sieges, and with a wide lighting experi-
ence in many parts of the alobe, Brisbane
was also a great astronomer and an impar-
tial and far-sighted administrator. It was pro-
bably his passion for astronomy that induced
Brisbane to apply for the N. S. Wales joD.
The Governor himself endowed an observa-
tory for the colony, and was the most active
worker in the congenial task of observing
the stars of this hemisphere. His work at
Parramatta received world-wide scientific
recognition. On his return to England he
became president of the Royal Society ; and
he just missed neing mane a field-marshal
before his death. But as a eovernor he
depended too much upon his subordinates;
and he allowed his far-sighted ideals to be
thwarted, opposed, or ignored by his officials.
The tnwarter-in-chief was Frederick Goul-
burn, the Colonial Secretary, who withheld
correspondence from the Governor, modified
his instructions, and issued orders on his
own responsibility. Though Brisbane was
aware of ail this, he made no strong effort
to counteract Goulburn’s influence; and ulti-
mately the disputes between them led to
their joint recall from the colony. Brisbane
was clad to go, leaving behind him a record
which ‘“just missed being ideal from his
failure to enforce the loyalty and obedience
of his principal executive officers.”
Incidentally, a daughter and a son born in
the colony were christened respectively
Australia and Australius.

By the death of Geoffrey Wall, of the
Royal Flying Corps, in his 21st year, Aus-
tralian literature suffered a severe loss. It
is true that it is promise, not performance,
that justifies this assertion ; but the promise
was extraordinarily fine. The English boy
came to Australia when nine years old, was
educated at Wesley College, Melb., and at
Melb. University. After his first year there
he ‘went to England, entered the Flying
Corps, and obtained his wings in June, 1917.
Two months later he was killed in an aero-
plane accident. Son,gs of an Airman, with a
memoir by the headmaster of Wesley College
(Aust. Authors’ Agency, Melb.; 2s. 6d.),
indicates an interesting personality and a
considerable aptitude for literature. Of the
26 verses selected, 20 are the product of his
17th and 18th years, and the remaining six
were written during his year at the Univer-
sity. He wrote no verses after leaving Aus-
tralia. Thus these are practically schoolboy
efforts. Quotations from Geoffrey Wall’s
letters from England show more promise
than can be gleaned from his verse; this boy
had an original mind and showed a remark-
able feeling for style and quality in his
prose. Yet though most of his schoolboy
verse is negligible—as what schoolboy verse
isn't? —in “After the Boat Race” he touches
finely upon our sporting attitude to defeat
and victory which is so incomprehensible to
the Hun
The race is rowed. Along the banks
The frenzied shouting dies away.
Slowly disperse the thronging ranks,
The azure blue has won the day.
Though not.to us—past conquerors—
The Fates decreed the hard-fought race,
We cheer the blue —the better crew,
And -own defeat, but not disgrace.
But “The Secret of the Leaves” shows a
feeling for beauty that might have led to
higher things. Anyhow, it is pretty good
stuff for a boy of 18: —
The tree-tops wave against an azure sky,
The zephyrs sway the branches to and fro,
And now and then the stronger breezes blow
And stir the gloomy pine-tops with a sigh.
A trivial thing, and yet the reason why?
1 think that countless centuries ago
I lay and watched the branches waving so,
And felt the warm south breezes breathe and die
Away among the hills, or stir the grass
Upon some sunny upland by the sea
(The blue Greek sea), and watched the shadows pass
And heard the sheep-bells tinkling on the lea,
(That was some thousand years ago, alas!)
Knee-deep in the long grass of Arcady.
Compared with the books written for the
Australian selmol-girl there is a dearth ot
books for the Australian school-boy. But
Jack of St. Virgil's, by Lillian M. I’yke
(Ward, Lock), is an excellent and healthy
tale of school-life in a big Melbourne school,
the identification of which should be easy. The
keynote of the yarn is the honor, of the
school; hut there is an interesting plot
which is not confined within the walls of
the school; and there are fun and adventure
and sports enough for the Australian hoy-
reader. Clearly written and well told, this
yarn should find a large school-boy public
throughout Australia.
E. George Marks assures us, in his pre-
face to the second series of his Napoleon
and the War (Dymock, Sydney, 2s. 6d.),
that he has written for Sydney Sun no less
than 50 Napoleonic articles. Such assidu-
ity, such devotion to the memory of a dead
man, seems to this page rather saddening.
Apparently E. George Marks can’t keep
Napoleon’s head out of his story. There
are problems enough in this war without
dragging Napoleon into the cable page.
But this interpretation of modern history
from the standpoint of Napoleon, or the
interpretation of Napoleon from the stand-
point of this war, is interestingly enough
done; and this second series shows the same
:horough knowledge of the Napoleonic era
as was apparent in his first series. It is
good journalism.
Edward Pulsford, hon. member of the
Cobden Club since 1887, states in his pre-
face to Commerce and the Empire, 191Jf and
After (King, Westminster) that for more
than 30 years he has been fighting in Aus-
tralia for Freetrade, and fighting hardest of
all, perhaps, against that form of Protection
called preference. “In the first issue of
Commerce and the Empire, published in 1903,
I said in the preface, ‘A British Freetrade
Empire is a noble object for which to fight.’
I repeat this now.” That ought to satisfy
everybody. Also, Edward Pulsford associ-
ates Empire stability with a commercial
policy of world-friendliness, and to bis last
breath will continue to associate it. So bis
standpoint is clear; and if there are any
other Freetraders left in Australia at this
date this book will appeal to them.

Apparently a number of volumes of “The
Australasian Shakespeare” aye on the mar-
ket, but only one, Henry the Fifth, edited
by J. Le Gay Brereton, Librarian of Syd-
ney University, has reached us. This series,
published by Lothian, Melb., is the result
of the combined efforts of the Anzalian uni-
versities to provide sound school texts,
which will meet the requirements of the
Anzalian examination boards. Such is the
announcement made by the general editor,
Prof. R. S. Wallace, of Melbourne. The
deduction is that the scholarship of England
is unable to provide sound school texts for
Anzalian educational bodies, or that the
school texts provided aren’t sound enough for
the examiners. This page can hardly credit
either assertion: there surely are excellent
school texts in existence; and the examina-
tion boards’ requirements cannot be so
exacting as to require a whole new made-in-
Anzalia Shakespeare. Doubtless, we have
here Shakespearean scholars and authori-
ties equal in Shakespearean learning and
acumen to those over in England; maybe,
despite our distance from the special sources
of knowledge, we have Shakespearean
specialists more acute; but still the work
must be dofie here under difficulties that are
absent from scholars with easy access to the
best libraries. A new series of Shakespear-
ean texts by Anzalian professors will no
doubt be an achievement in scholarship: but
the improvement upon the existing editions
must be infinitesimally small for the effort.
This page is eager to acclaim Anzalian
worth, and applauds the patriotism that
has led these professors to bund such a noble
monument of Anzalian learning; but it
seems an unnecessary labor, when there aie
so many subjects of purely Anzalian literary
interest that are left, undone. If this group
of professors had set to work to bring out
a series of works upon our own literature,
they would be doing a needed service to the
country that pays their salaries ; they would
bring before the world the beginnings of a
new literature; they would encourage our
youth to study that literature; they would
be in an unique position for access to all
the documents, and would possess the neces-
sary authority to speak for us to the world.
At present they leave a Professor of Poetry
in Oxford (Eng.) to discover for us and for
them what is Australian poetry, and whom
are its practitioners, though that distant
professor .cannot possibly have access to all
our publications nor possess the native
knowledge of the cross-currents, the history,
and the tendencies of our nascent litera-
ture.
Are our universities doing their job for
Australian literature, the job they are paid
for? It is noteworthy that the only excel-
lent Anthology of Australian Poetry is the
work of an editor who was not educated in
a university; though Prof. Walter Murdoch,
now of W.A., is about to bring out another.
And surely a living organism like the Aus-
tralian language is more interesting to study
than a dead reputation like Shakespeare?
Anyhow, this page finds it quite, exciting to
attempt to estimate the place of living An-
zalian writers in our literature, with always
the possibility that the pinned and wrig-
gling insect will escape and do something
quite different, something that will make a
mock of all this page’s pigeon-holing. But
Shakespeare is safe, buried a thousand
volumes deep in commentaries and editions.
Possibly the professorial mind prefers to
dissect a corpse. Anyhow here are seven
new volumes to add to the pile.
Brereton announces that in his Henry the
Fifth he has made free use of the labors of
his predecessors, and is indebted particu-
larly to three earlier editions, while he has
referred constantly to standard works of
Shakespearean reference. This page would
have preferred Brereton to have made no
use of any of his predecessors, and to have
Invented an Anzalian Shakespeare of his
own. It would have been so much more
exciting.... and Brereton could have done it
deliciously. However... .the notes are
lucid and sufficient, and where the oppor-
tunity occurs he makes original suggestions.
The printing and get-up are excellently Aus-
tralian. And now we hope the exacting
examination boards will be satisfied!
This page regrets that it cannot discover
any signs of any poetry in A Child of Nature,
and (J. V., by “Austral” (Stockwell, Lon-
don). A fair sample of the contents:—
MR grand to break awa” to the country—
Away from the city’s din;
’Tis grand to breathe the nectareous air
And to hear the skylark sing
•
Letters from Boh (Melville and Mullen,
Melb.) forms an interesting and pathetic
tribute to the late Captain George Pollard
Kay, Flight Commander in the lioyal Flying
Corps. This young Australian, educated at
the Geelong Grammar School, was the only
son of G. A. Kay, of Melbourne. The boy
showed at school considerable literary and
artistic gifts, and his school record was one
of physical prowess allied with a strong
character. A popular leader and a member
of all the school athletic teams —a splendid
type of young Australia. His capacity for
work, his ability and his character, gave this
youth his captaincy and his command of a
flight squadron, which would have carried
his majority with it, before he had reached
his 21st birthday. And then, after. doing
brilliant and dangerous work as a pilot at
the Front, he “crashed” fatally as the result
of an aeroplane accident. Kay’s letters are
delightful to read; his joy in life, his zest
for flying, his love for his people, his
straightness, and his utter absence of
“swank” or self-praise make of these letters
a document worthy of his country, and an
abiding solace to the friends who mourn him.
Incidentally, Kay’s letters give a vivid picture
of the work of the Flying Corps, of the marvel-
lous aeroplanes that are now in use, and of
his exciting experiences. The general reader
will find here the real thing, admirably de-
scribed. His death came, curiously enough,
when he was not in action. He was flying
back to his aerodrome from a visit to a
fellow officer when unexpectedly his machine
was observed to get into a nose spin, out of
which he had not the air-space to get before
he “crashed.” His death was due to pure
accident, as the machine was faultless, and
had been flown immediately before by another
officer who looped the loop 12 times; and
Kay was too careful, though so brilliant a
flyer, to play the fool. The theory is that
his foot got in some way wedged amongst
the levers, and he was unable to extricate
it, as, amongst his other injuries, his ankle
was found to be fractured. Confirmation
of this theory comes from a fellow-officer
who stated that machines were being made
with a few inches more room, apparently on
I account of some difficulty which pilots ex-
perienced with their feet among the pedals.

The patriotism of “Patrius,” author of
Hoisting Our Flag on the Ansae Redoubt ,
and Other War Rimes and Various Verses
(Davies andCanniugton, Newcastle,N.S.W.),
is worth more than his poetry.
There is a fragile, evanescent poetry in
1 Venture in Verse, a first offering by Mar-
jory Nicholls (Whiteombe and Tombs,
M.L.). Most of the verses have appeared
in the college magazine of \ ictoria Univer-
sity, Wellington, M.L. There is nothing
noteworthy except a sense of form in the
earlier verses. Marjory Nicholls has not
much to say, hut she says it nicelj. Natu-
rally she excels in the ' sonnet form—that
neat casket for neatly-packed thoughts. Here
is a “Bed Hibiscus in a Sydney Street” :
When I look up and see your flaunting head
,\ n d the long tongue that serpent-like shoots out,
I ask, as swift thoughts throng in revellers’ rout,
What in the world so wicked is as red?
1 see a columned hall and tables spread,
A woman, white and red, with smiles that flout,
Two wine-flushed suitors and a sudden shout,
Quarrel’s quick curses, and the red wine shed—
A gleam of swords, a bright and startling stain ;
Fear's frantic flight, and silence in the hall;
Save when the night-wind strays in, flower-sweet,
And from the guttering candles white drops fall.
Hibiscus, you do breathe of passion’s pain -
Why do you glow by this grey city’s street?
And here is a simple but palely beautiful
thing:—•
A LITTLE PLACE APART.
A little garden have I made me here
Of tender, fragrant plants —none bright or gay-
And hi tiier shall 1 come in twilight-time
To dream awhile of the dear yesterday.
A little breeze comes whispering from the Past—
A magic whisper, wondrous soft and sweet;
I kneel upon the path, to closer come
To those dear blossoms growing at my feet.
A little scent of lavender, so faint;
And rosemary—and that I pluck and hold
Thinking of vou —Death came so soon to y0u....
Another breeze blows by. ’Tis strangely cold.

Tales for Lonely and Only Children,
by E. J. Donnelly (Diddams, Brisbane),
does not live up to its delightful title. The
stories are commonplace.
On the cover of The Breed Holds Good,
the latest Bookstall shilling’s worth of
Arthur Wright, there is a jockey, a race-
horse and a Billjim in khaki knocking out a
villain, wliom we darkly suspect of being ot
German parentage. What more does an
Australian reader want?
Evidently it is to be Anzalia and ( An« a-
Han, since two other correspondents, Bart
from M.L., and “Creole,” independently
suggest tiie same substitute for the clumsy
and lanky terms Australasia and Australa-
sian.
“Simplicity” misunderstands this page s
vi wpoint of Australian slang, despite our
answer to “Spider” (24/l/’18). Where an
author is reporting, in the first person, the
speech of an individual, he must use that
bloke’s actual words; though in case ot cuss-
words and indecent expletives, some camou-
flage is usually necessary. Blanky ( or- ; )
for bloody is this paper’s compromise between
realism and decorum; though nobody ever
imagines that Australians actually say
blanky. Similarly in the war books Tommy
is supposed to say blinkin’; but he doesn t.
(in fact, he commonly uses something muen
worse than plain bloody.) Shaw and Mase-
-1; id—the former with a theatri-
cality, and the latter with an artistic sin-
cerity—print the word. W. T. Goodge added
to his reputation by not saying the word in
“Th’ Great Australian Adjective”; and
Dennis made his reputation by the same
means with his “Australaise,” first printed
on this page. But the fact that an author
uses slang or dialect in reporting the words
used by another does not imply the author s
approval or disapproval of slang or dialect.
To talk in a pained tone, as “Simplicity”
does, of introducing slang intp Australian
literature, is absurd. The slang is the cha-
racter’s slang—not the author’s. When a
slang word is introduced into literature it is
deliberately done by an author, because he
believes that the literature needs just that
slang-word, sinee no dictionary word will
exactly fill its place. And it is by the
gradual accretion of selected slang or foreign
or invented words tlj&t a language grows.
This war will add a great number of words
to our speech, and xrom that accession a
proportion will ultimately become embedded
in the Australian language. It is only the
dead languages that do not grow.
But “Simplicity” makes the further
point, by forwarding extracts from her cor-
respondence with Australian friends, that
the language of The Sentimental Bloke is
not the slang actually spoken in Australia.
One Melb. correspondent thought ,the lan-
guage horrible, and added. “But as we dont
talk like that in Melbourne we concluded
that it was your Sydney lingo.” Another
correspondent wrote:— >
I agree with every word you say about Dennis and
his Sentimental Bloke language. I _ loathe it. I
strongly resent that such vile distortion of English
should be accepted as Australian. My son is an
Australian—an Australian soldier — but thank God
he does not talk like that, and—again thank God
I don’t know anybody who does.... Dennis s ar ? ( v
is no more Australian than Whitechapel is Englisn
or the Parisian apache is French.

CHLORACO TOOTH PASTE.
Chloraco Tooth Paste is distinguished for
its complete efficiency as a dentifrice, being a
Chlorate compound.
Chlorates, it has been stated in the Pharma-
ceutical Codex, give up oxygen to the tissues,
and it is largely on this account that they
have received wide employment in foul condi-
tions of the mouth and pharynx. Chlorates
are used as a wash for the mouth when the
gums are inflamed and spongy.
The name Chloraco has been adopted as a
specific title and trade-mark to prevent un-
scrupulous persons deceiving the public with
preparations containing no chlorates at all.

Australia Riddled with
Deadly Kidney Disease
ALMOST EVERYBODY COMPLAIN INC.
Kidney disease is most certainly on the in-
crease. Thousands and thousands of people
in the towns and villages of this country are
in terrible-danger of deadly kidney disease.
This insidious disease creeps on, gradu-
ally getting a firmer and firmer grip, until
when it is too late the poor sufferer discovers
he has Bright’s Disease. Have you kidney
trouble? Do you know how to recognise
whether you are suffering from this or not?
Here are some of the symptoms of kidney
disease : Aches and pains in various parts of
the body, nasty taste in the mouth first thing
in the morning, constipation, cloudy and bad-
colored urine, general feeling of weakness
and irritability, pufliness under the eyes—
these are all symptoms of kidney trouble.
And then people who suffer from backache,
rheumatism, gout, sciatica, gravel, stone or
pains in the bladder, muscles and joints are
all suffering these pains through weak or
diseased kidneys, for these are the sure and
certain symptoms of kidney trouble.
All these things mean that the kidneys are
weak and diseased. They need strengthening
and making healthy.
Don’t waste a single hour. Go to your

LORRY TYRES
r.
VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Like Homing Birds.
The children played on the roselit sands,
The roselight tinting their feet and hands,
As ever and ever they danced in glee;
And over the roofs of the little town
And the hills behind the sun Avent down,
Lavish of wealth as a king should be.
The rich lights reddened on ridge and reach,
Yet, watching, I thought of another beach.
Where the “waves cry out with a dirging
sound:
“Oh, pray and pity, and pity and pray,
For here Death moved on a sacred day,
Flinging his largesse of grief around !”
Then up and out of the East there drew,
Each of them lit by the rose-red hue,
White birds of sea with clamorous call;
And the children, watching their flight,
grew dumb,
As home they headed ere night should
come,
Flinging his mantle of gloom o’er all.
And 10, I dreamt in that wondrous hour,
When the sunset flamed like a Tropic flower,
And a pause came over the children’s mirth,
Even as these, do our deathless brave —
White souls winging across the wave —
Homeward hie to their place of birth.
n.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
Irish.
My father and mother were Irish,
And Irish must he he,
With a far thought and a long thought,
Who comes to wed with me.
With the clean dreams of the green land
Stored in his heart away,
Deep with the sob of Ireland,
Thrid through Avith laughter gay.
Upon the grass o’ mornings,
He’ll see the faery ring,
And not despise the warnings
The Good Folk bring.
With the soft kiss of the mist-land
His soul shall still be sweet,
For the high emprise of Ireland,
And a great heart’s beat.
To guard that heart from traitor sin
He’s strengthened Avith the shields
Of the gold songs of Erin
Over four green fields.
Oh, he you horn in Sydney,
Or come from far Tralee.
The Irish soul you bring me
Makes you the hoy for me.
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.
Rondeau.
I am in love with sea and sun,
And with the moon, like some pale nun,
That smiles in sadness from on high ;
With daAvn’s rich rose when day is nigh,
With night’s black plumes when day is done.
With each fantastic pattern spun
By cloud sprites, in their careless fun,
Across the blue demesne of sky
I am in love.
The stars my deep regard have won,
The birds that sing when day’s begun;
I smile to hear the night wind sigh;
Spring’s charms devour with eager eye,
Because —the fact I cannot shun—
I am in love !
victoria.
HARRISON O.
Happy Song.
I am too much alive to sing.
I want to shout and leap and fling
My laughing arms into the air
And dance on tip-toe everywhere.
With not a thought and not a dream,
I want to skip beside a stream
In Arcady, in Arcady.
And woo the wood-gods all to me.
I want to feel them chase me down
Among the grasses green and brown,
With many a mad and merry cry
Of Youth and Gladness ringing high,
Till reeling frolic, drunk with mirth*
Sinks to exhaustion on the earth.
In Arcady, in Arcady !
Tra-la-la-la, who catches me?
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
The Lady.
She smiled into my eyes and said, “Maybe
In time you’ll come to understand me, dear.
When I’m away from you, you long for me;
But still you scold and fret w'hen I am near.
I love you when you smile and Avhen you
frown
With all my heart. The world is upside
down.”
But next day in a cosy little room
She sat with Bilkins, and she crooned to
him !
“I’m yours. I love you so, sunshine or gloom !
Though you doubt still, I love you, sink or
swim !
Love laughs at all we’ve dreamt of, all Ave’ve
planned—
In time to come perhaps you’ll understand.”
A little later, whispering lip to lip,
She fed my blood with kisses, laughing low.
She said, “I’ve given poor old Bill the slip.
Who cares! You’re here, and nothing
matters. So
Love me, my dear. Whatever others do,
I’m yours, yours only, for I’m true to you!”
So, clasped together, with a leap we \yent
Beyond the stars, and I was well content.
To live, to laugh, to love, and—to forget!
I did not any longer scold and fret.
She is a kindly dame, when all is said,
Who flings a starving man a slice of bread.
N.S.W.
JOSEPHUS Q. HARRIS.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 7, 1918.
THE FAMILY DOCTOR GOES ON
STRIKE.
The old dispute between Sawbones and the
Friendly Society which periodically rages over
some part of Australia has been lately stir-
ring Victoria to the boots. And the main
thing about it all is a revelation of social in-
competence. Here is a series of questions in
which every citizen has a vital interest, yet
not even the beginning of a system by which
the questions can be promptly and justly
settled is to be found. The dispute turns
upon the problem whether Sawbones should
be paid 14s. or 20s. per head for each mem-
ber of a society to which he is medical
officer. What he says is that 14s. is not a
fair thing—that 20s. would be. What the
societies say is that 14s. is enough, and that
20s. is monstrous, but they are willing to
give 17s. Having spent many days in contro-
versy over this, the union doctors last week
downed the lancet and the pill, and there
was the grotesque spectacle of the British
Medical Assoc, running a strike. Up to the
time of writing nothing has been heard of
the State issuing a call for “loyal” doctors,
nor did anyqne seem to know where to dis-
cover a formula for reconciling the difference
between 17s. and £l. The attitude of the
societies appeared to be that there was hope
.of a certain number of medical men break-
ing away from the union, while that of the
strike committee was that if poor people are
blistered for 10s. 6d. a visit instead of lid.,
they will be forced to yield the demands made
upon them through their lodges. It looks
a ghastly sort of business, this wrangling
over money for treating the sick, and relying
on human affliction to squeeze money out of
a cashbox ; but there the facts are. The union
of Sawbones simply says: “We will not
have arbitration. These are our terms—take
them or leave them. If you do not take them
we will use the machinery of our organisa-
tion to ensure that the sick citizen who
cannot hand over 10s. 6d. a visit .has a pretty
bad time unless he comes crawling to us as
a pauper.” Yet out of this evil-looking mess
good may come, for the greed and brutality
revealed by the proceedings—they are not all
confined to one side either —cannot fail to
impress the general public with a suspicion
that there is something rotten with a system
out of which such abomination proceeds.
So far as this paper is aware no one can
possibly say whether a medical man is mak-
ing an extortionate or a reasonable demand
in asking that he shall be paid 20s. a year to
attend a member of a friendly society and
his family in time of sickness. This is
taking the thing generally. There are some
practitioners whom The Bulletin would
consider grossly overpaid for the job at 2d.
per month. There are others who would give
good value for £5. What is a moral cer-
tainty is that the yearning of the average
friendly society for cheap medical attend-
ance—for the spot diagnosis and the conse-
quential teaspoonful of something three times
a day ; for a system which drafts men and
women through a stuffy apartment decorated
with a plate bearing the wildly-humorous
“Consulting Room” like sheep through a dip
-—is a pathetic reminder of how tragic
human credulity may be. On the other side
the anxiety of the medical union to continue
in the case of the poorer sections of the com-
munity a system which is in the main an
outrage upon the profession of medicine is
a painful illustration of how resilient a con-
science can become. The friendly society is
in many ways a very admirable institution.
It represents a form of co-operative attempt
to meet the pressure of social conditions
which without some such effort at self-
defence would be insupportable. But there
are some aspects of the business which do
not provoke applause. The medical-attendance
side of these institutions rests upon a basis
which would be none the worse for complete
overhaul. The science of medicine and the
art of surgery are to-day very different things
from what they were when friendly societies
came into being—even from what they were
25 years ago. A system that was good
enough then is all wrong now. The entire
conception of the place of medicine and sur-
gery in the social scheme has altered. At
one time it seemed right that a given number
of people should band together, and by pay-
ing so many pence per week into a common
fund use the proceeds to get for such of them
as might want medical aid the services of a
doctor. To the doctor who would otherwise
have accumulated bad debts for looking at
the tongue of the proletariat the system
offered many advantages. In a sense, it does
so still, provided the pool is big enough. But
it only offers advantages of income. To the
proletariat its advantages are very question-
able. And it is a question of the very first
importance to the State whether the prac-
tice of medicine upon commercial lines, which
secures to an enormous number of citizens
only such service as the poorest among them
can pay for, is socially even tolerable. This
business cannot be limited to the question
whether a friendly society can pay 145..
17s. or 20s. a year per member for medical
attention, or whether a medical man should
derive from the society an income of £OOO or
£l2OO a year. When those passing issues
are solved by Sawbones getting as much as
he can and the wholesale society obtaining
as heavy a discount as possible, both parties
thus getting amicably on the same plane
as the dealers in tripe, a greater issue will
remain where it was. And that is whether
the people directly concerned are securing a
just share of the benefits that might be
derived from medical science, or whether the
medical practitioner is discharging his most
useful function. This paper does not
believe that they are.
* * # *
The treatment and care of the sick is a
social question. It was one of the first
social obligations accepted by man, dating
from the time when he carried the afflicted
individual out into the desert and left him
to die in peace and quiet. It is recognised
in a dozen ways—by the erection.and main-
tenance of hospitals, by the formation of
nursing guilds, by the establishment of
schools of medicine, by the creation of clinics
and dispensaries and Public Health Depart-
ments, and the taking up of collections for
the equipment of medical missionaries to
benighted lands or impoverished slums.
But treatment of the sick is intimately
connected with the care of the sound.
The prevention of illness is as definite
a part of medical science as the elimina-
tion of an appendix. It is of more import-
ance to society that a profession which makes
a special study of, say, those diseases aris-
ing from overcrowding, defective sanitation
and so on should grapple with the causa-
tion of physical disabilities, than that when a
citizen falls victim to illness he should be
a source of income to the profession. As
it is now, the medical man depends upon
the illness and suffering of individuals for
his livelihood; in the case of friendly society
members, the more he can get so much a
head for the better his income will be.
Whether this is better for anything except
the pocket of the practitioner is another
matter. And it is this other matter to
which men and women all over the world
are turning their minds.
* * * *
Social conditions force the medical man,
often against his will and usually against
his ideals, to chase the dollar as hard as
nearly everybody else. His general desire
is to see the community divided into two
mobs —paupers and those who are not of
that class. To the pauper he is prepared to
give certain gratuitous service under certain
conditions. The others he insists must pay.
If they are able to pay something he will
treat them after a fashion in sections at
reduced rates per head. If they are able to
pay more, then they must do so. The
medical man has to live, and is entitled to
get all he can. At any rate, he thinks that
way, and like the lawyer and the cocky and
the bottle-oh. will continue to do so until
changed conditions begin to make him think
otherwise. Wherefore loud, frequent
demands have arisen in Victoria for the
local politician to arise and awake and
nationalise the medical service on non-
competitive principles. If the local politi-
cian were a Bolshevik no doubt he would
proceed to put Sawbones in his place
within 24 hours. But calling upon Premier
Bowser, of Wangaratta, to do this thing is
very like asking Bigjohn Forrest to go out
organising for the A.W.U. Between talking
about making a revolutionary change in the
status of a whole profession and actually
doing the deed there is a vast difference, and
this paper has a strong suspicion that 99
per cent, of the people who make oracular
noises about nationalising medical services
have neither a glimmering of what they want
nor a notion of how they would set about
getting it. Bald statements about making
“all medical men Civil servants and paying
them regular incomes” are meaningless
except for purposes of academic disputation.
As society stands at the moment it is
waste of time to talk about the subject.
What it is not waste of time to talk about
is the duty of the State to secure for its
citizens the opportunity to participate,
regardless of the size of their individual
purse, in all the benefits to be derived from
medical science. To do this —in part, any-
how —it is not necessary to start a contro-
versy about something impossible of realisa-
tion. Through an extension of the functions
of Public Health Departments, the estab-
lishment of hospitals upon the broadest basis
of public utility and the appointment of
district medical officers, a substantial step
would be taken towards the reform that
must inevitably some day come. At the
moment the particular business the Vic-
torian Ministry has in hand is to first dis-
cover whether the system under which
friendly societies and medical men carry on
their joint operations is defensible, and next
to carefully examine the British Medical
Association as a union. It is a registered
body composed of men who are themselves
registered by the State. It has loftily
refused to submit its quarrel with the socie-
ties to arbitration. It has promoted a strike
in time of war—a strike aimed among others
at the families of men who are serving at
the Front. Unions of workmen, with far
more justification for social revolt, have been
held up to obloquy, made the targets of
public resentment and had their registration
cancelled by the courts for doing things far
less morally odious than this association of
professional experts to most of whom the
war has brought increased incomes. The
enormity of this strike is so glaring that if
the Victorian Government allows the thing
to go on it will look about the last thing in
senility. Objectionable though the War
Precautions Act has been made, there could
be no excuse for the Federal Government
refusing to exercise its authority under that
measure and dealing with the Medical Asso-
ciation as it has dealt with other combina-
tions of recalcitrant monopolists.
SCOFFING AT THE AFFLICTED.
Last week certain well-meaning persons got
together at Melbourne and added a trifle to
the superfluous eloquence whose dead weight
already makes Australia tip up at one side,
causing tidal waves and other disturbances.
The Governor-General spoke a piece about
what ought to be done in the way of promot-
ing industry, and was as sensible and as
forcible as an Excellency is permitted to be.
Then Senator Millen, Federal Minister for
Repatriation, held high discourse on the sub-
ject of his dead horse. Carefully shying off
the awkward questions of the need for local
shipbuilding on a big scale, for great local:
iron and steel works and for all manner of
“secondary” industries, he babbled at length
about the necessity for settling people on
the land —returned soldiers and others. This
was Millen, condensed :
The problem was how to bring the land and the
persons desirous of settling upon it together. The
thing that stood between was the capital necessary
to provide plant and equipment.... Might they not
utilise the public capital, etc.
Now, where, in this country which under the
present system doesn’t even contrive to pay
the everyday expenses of Government with-
out the aid of borrowed money, and which
charges all its public-works expenditure to
loans—where is the “public capital” that
Millen talks about? The public shortage
is visible enough, but that is another thing.
And why doesn’t the eloquent but ordinary
senator say something about a free railway
ticket for the farmer who wants to get off
the land ; also a free lunch for him on his
journey ?
Australia’s Lose-the-War Government is
invited to consider the latest developments.
Lord Rhondda, the British Minister for
Hunger and Food Shortage (that is not his
official title, but it very well describes the
nature of the job he is up against), has
broken the news to the United States that
unless that country can supply 75,000,000
more bushels of wheat than it has exported
up to January 2 it is impossible to say that
there Mtill be enough provender in the Allied
countries to make the war a success. An
army travels on its stomach and fights on its
stomach, but not on its empty stomach. And
Uncle Sam has replied manfully that the
food will be there up to time, and that the
.ships to carry it will also be there. Already
restrictions are being put on the consumption
of wheat and meat in George Washing-
ton’s country, so that the big surplus avail-
able for the use of the hungry Allies who
stand in the fighting line in Europe may be
a little bigger. Nobody makes any attempt
now to disguise the fact that victory and
victuals go together, and that without a
reasonable supply of victuals the prospect is
gloomy. The British Minister for Hunger
and Food Shortage didn’t send any cable-
gram to Australia on the subject of an
extra wheat supply, because Australia has
vanished out of ’notice in this connection.
It has sunk into contempt or beneath con-
tempt. The bottom fell out of its great land
settlement and imported-goods policy when it
struck its one real emergency. It crumpled
up at the first time of asking. The country
has about 8,000,000 tons of wheat on hand,
but it has also a Ministry which, after more
than three years’ consideration, hasn’t devel-
oped enough knowledge to be a success as a
common carrier, like the humble individual
who shifts a trunk to the railway station.
True it is now getting a few belated ships
built by Private Enterprise in the United
States, after completely ignoring the offer
made nearly a year ago by local Private
Enterprise in the shape of the Broken Hill
Proprietary Co. to wade in strenuously at
the building of steel vessels. This was
characteristic of the Hughes Administra-
tion. It is a stalwart believer in Govern-
ment control of industry and a staunch
opponent of Private Enterprise—unless it is
the foreign article. And it has a great talent
for discouraging local skill, having the
Foreign-trader’s childlike faith in the advisa-
bility of importing things. It couldn’t im-
port the silos in which the unsaleable wheat
is to be stored, but probably it would have
done so if it could. It is the sort of com-
bination that would gladly import post-holes
and bi-eathe nothing but bottled air brought
from the Saliai-a.
« * * *
This paper urges Senator Millen and his
party to cease insulting the farmer with their
lavish conversation about land settlement.
That individual has had his wheat comman-
deered by the Government at a low rate, lie
has guessed for quite a while, as he saw it
accumulating at stations and sidings along-
side the wonderful loan-money railways
which never manage to meet their interest
bill, that even the very modest payment is
precarious, and now he realises that it may
stop altogether in another year or so. He
has missed a great deal of profit that might
have been made by prompt disposal of his
sui-plus ci-op. He knows that people of his
own race and their allies are going hungry
for want of the crop, and he has a suspicion
that Germany is holding out the glad hand
to him on that account, and saying “Well
done, good and useful traitor!” To be lec-
tured about the need for increased land
settlement, and to be threatened with the
competition of a new crowd of farmers sub-
sidised in one way or another by “public
capital,” are matters that don’t cheer him.
Millen and co. are invited to remember that
the person who throws the mud of sarcasm
at the agriculturist who is down is un-
worthy of the name he bears —whatever it
may be.
* * * *
The lazy, easy, shiftless policy of low
revenue tariffs and huge imports has burst,
as already mentioned, and Hughes is its
prophet—likewise Millen. The importation
of capital in the shape of loans, the importa-
tion of ships, of almost all our clothing and
machinery and implements, of a great part
of our railways (they were brought out in
parts from better informed countries, and
Australia supplied the land), the importa-
tion of hats and hurdy-gurdies and corn-
sacks and cups and saucers and railway
managers and bug experts and a thousand
other things saved incapable political leadei-s
a heap of trouble in teaching the nation the
way it should go, and made it a very humble
dependent on foreign aid. A million times
Australia has been warned that if Britain
lost command of the sea and imports were
cut off it would become little better than an
aborigine becaxise it didn’t learn to do things
in a big way beyond tearing raw produce
out of the ground. Britain hasn’t lost com-
mand of the sea, but the dislocation of sea
carriage through the w-ar has brought along
about 5 per cent, of the possible catas-
trophe. And this country will be very naked
and vei-y much ashamed if another 5 per
cent, arrives, let alone the whole catastrophe.
The Bulletin doesn’t believe that the
political shuffler who is Dictator of Aus-
tralia cares twopence for the gi-eat import-
ing policy except as a matter of conveni-
ence and habit arising out of convenience.
But he has no visible qualifications except a
terrible fluency and a marvellous capacity
for hanging on to office. Nobody can dream
of him tackling a big policy with a view to
making Australia a self-dependent country;
that would be work for Hercules, not
Hughes. The difference between the old
god and the bustling and talkative modern
politician has been noted by many observers.
It isn’t on record that the former made a
single lengthy speech during his .famous
labors, and he never gave way to terror and
swore in 59 special constables.
FAITH.
In the trinity of noblest thought Faith has
the first place. It is the strong undertone
in human life, the structure on which most
other things that men regard as worth while
are built; but it requires a lot of assistance
from the companion graces Hope and
Charity to enable it to keep its torch alight
in the flickering and variable winds of the
world. Still, that assistance is generally
forthcoming; and it is marvellous how in
such a matter as the present war the true
believers outnumber the doubters and pessi-
mists. We are going to lick the Kaiser. Of
that the man in the tram is assured and the
fiercest anti-gambler would wager his next
year’s income upon it, as a patriotic duty.
The breaking-up of Russia, the devouring of
Roumania, the smashing of the Italian line
and the hunger that grips the British
stomach have scarcely an effect on public-
opinion. The war can have only one con-
clusion —the conclusion that public opinion
desires. Yet national faith has not always
been justified. For five hundred years
Montenegro held her rugged rocks in the
belief that she would finally x-estore the
ancient Serb empire. Her great legendary
hero lay asleep in one of the mountain caves,
some day to awake and drive off the
oppressor who held the fruitful lower
country. Never in all the years did the
Montenegrin lose faith, but with knife and
blue metal hung to his eagle nest waiting
for the glorious resurrection. And then the
Austrian cannon battered down the defences
the Turk could never take, and the faith
was i-educed to very small potatoes in the
neighboi-hood of Salonika. It may be justi-
fied yet, but not by the power of Ivan the
Black nor the mountaineer’s knife. Simi-
larly, the faith of the Welsh in the return
of Arthur and of the Poles that they could
regain the old territory where their former
nobility had treated them like dogs has worn
very thin and sorry with the ages. It may
be that the long wish behind the faith may
in almost every case materialise at last, but
it does not do so in a way that gives, much
satisfaction to the earliest and best believers.
St. James, who solidly maintained that faith
wasn’t worth a row of .Chinese crackers
unless it had a solid backing of works, has
been justified on every page of human his-
tory.
Yet faith is a tremendous weapon. The
belief of the Allied peoples that they must


win gives a tremendous impetus to every
offensive and keeps the grin good on the faces
of suffering peoples when the pain of war
racks most. Unfortunately, the Teuton faith
appears to be almost as firmly entrenched ;
and the game now being played in Europe
is mostly one of trying to shake the other
fellow’s confidence by a long-distance fire of
aggressive speech. If the German could by
any means be made to understand that he
hasn’t a chance the war would soon be over;
but, on all appearances, the only way in
which that can be proved to him is by
making him too dead to hear the Kaiser’s
speeches. Behind all is, of course, the gen-
eral theory that right triumphs over might.
It is an idea inculcated by all religions and
by every nursery tale; but in practice it
doesn’t amount to much. Not only can a
little oblique reasoning make the wrongest
thing look right in certain circumstances,
hut the establishment of any direct codes
of right and wrong in such a business as
war becomes itself an impossibility. Out-
side the Ten Commandments, which are all
smashed to dust by both sides at the first
blare of the war-trumpet, the only code of
right is the consensus of opinion of a com-
munity ; and as there lire two distinct and
opposing communities in any war, so there
must be two distinct codes. That is why
both sides strenuously pray to God to help
them, with perfect earnestness and no sense
of hypocrisy. So faith in the ultimate
triumph of right is double-barrelled; and the
faithful face one another to bomb the rival
righteousnesses into each other’s souls. The
thing can work itself out in no possible way
except as a commentary on the opinion of
St. James that faith without works is dead ;
for works are the onlv thing in which people
can have faith during the struggles of
nations. That the Allied faith is stronger
than the German faith seems probable, but
it can only remain so if the Allies’ works or
potential works are stronger. That is why
politicians still brag in the face of partial
defeat, and tired armies sweat and swear to
make the bragging good. If faith could win
without works it would be easier and
cheaper; but faith can only remove moun-
tains with a pick and shovel and get guns out
of the road with high explosives.
Yet justification by faith, without real toil
having a look in, is still the religion of a lot
of people who steadily keep their coats on
in the face of tremendous issues. Nowhere does
the doctrine seem to be more deeply rooted and
grounded in the popular imagination than in
Australia. A Win-the-War party with in-
tense jaw-power is in office, but our share of
the war is not being waged by Australia at
all. It has been undertaken by individuals
who, acting on their own initiative, have
decided to pull down the Kaiser for the
benefit of the race. As a Commonwealth our
faith in victory is, therefore, a faith in
something which as a Commonwealth we
decided not to attempt. But it is not only
to the war that this applies. Australians
are supposed to have great faith in Aus-
tralia. Any politician of any color will
brag that in the ears of the faithful; but
what does the brag really amount to? Has
there been any sort of. indication on the part
of the people as a whole that they are will-
ing to unitedly develop the continent of their
faith? Private citizens work to that end
and other private citizens loaf or exert them-
selves in an altogether onposite direction;
but the works, that should prove our faith
are mostly occasional noises of the political
people on the eve of an election. Take an
outstanding example. Eighteen years ago
the new Australian Federation was supposed
to have abolished trade restrictions at the
State boundaries; but because of the broken
railway gauges those restrictions have not
in a real sense been abolished yet. If the
progress of Australian works, wffiich are the
only basis of Australian faith, can go no
faster than this, what is the faith worth?
Montenegro looking for the resurrection of
Ivan the Black seems less futile than Aus-
tralia clinging to brag in the face of the
political calamity of successive do-nothing-
in-particular Ministries. But the winning
of the war is our first concern; and the
brag to the faithful has shifted its centre of
interest to the bombing of Berlin and the
slaughter of the Kaiser. Some day, to the
infinite sorrow of our politicians, the war
will end; and then the faith that really re-
moves mountains of incapacity and talk may
have its innings. For there are men who
really believe in Australia, and some of them
must sooner or later get into one of our 14
Houses of Parliament, or otherwise assert
themselves. Australians are not without
works to justify their faith in their own
capabilities; it is Australia in the mass that
fails. The dynamic force of reform must
lie in the faith of those who have really done
things, and they are not numbered among
our present politicians.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Is the Farmer Being Fleeced Again?
There was a steady demand in Sydney last
week by speculators in wheat scrip, and a
good deal of business was done. While these
transactions were going on argument was
proceeding in the wheat-growing States as to
whether it was worth while planting any more.
The discussion arose from a combination of
circumstances which included- (1) semi-
official recommendations to Cocky to take
to cultivating flocks and herds; (2) a
general feeling that if more wheat is pro-
duced it will be devoured by mice and other
wild animals; (3) an announcement by
Hughes that no buyer has yet been dis-
covered for the current harvest; (4) a
popular impression that, in the absence of
shipping and buyers, Australian Govern-
ments in “financing” farmers to grow addi-
tional wheat would be going full speed
towards blue ruin; and (5) a suspicion that
the whole situation regarding wheat pools
and all the rest of it has fallen into chaos.
The net result of all of which is that people
who are not wheat growers are risking their
money on a theory that something satisfac-
tory is on the eve of happening, and the
citizens who actually deliver the goods are
in a state of pathetic uncertainty as to
whether it is worth while producing any
more. The position is not creditable to
any of the Governments concerned. Unless
this is altered, and quickly, too, it will be
mightily discreditable. It has been stated
by Prime Minister Hughes that the wheat
in sight in Australia cannot all be shipped
for four years. There is the additional
statement that for the 1917-8 harvest there
are no buyers. If this represents the bases
upon which Cocky must consider himself
and his future, it would seem to be plain
lunacy to continue on the old lines and to
be the plain duty of the Federal Ministry to
severely limit the guarantee of 4s. a bushel
promised on the 1918-9 harvest. If it does
not —if there is likelihood of transport being
made available, and if the British and
American Governments can be prevailed
upon to give a plain answer to the question
whether they want Australia to grow wheat
—then it might be madness for the Common-
wealth not to plough the last available acre.
It can be taken as a moral certainty that if
this and subsequent harvests are to be piled
up in stacks to be soaked with rain and
overrun by mice and weevil for four years,
the rest of the story is going to be entirely
devoid of humor. But an emissary of the
British Wheat Commission has discovered a
method of preservation, and if this be effec-
tive one of the great dangers of production
is removed. It is certain that the world is
growing desperately short of wheat—that to
supply her Allies America is being stripped
of every bushel that can be spared. Britain,
France, Italy and the United States want
wheat as urgently as they want shells. It
is no part of this paper’s job to advise the
Australian farmer to grow more wheat, but
it is part of its job to call on the Federal
and State Ministries to face this matter in
a way that can be understood. Nearly all
of them are given to spasmodic blithering
about their anxiety to “organise industry.”
Well, this particular industry is, on the side
of production at any rate, organised suffi-
ciently to produce millions of tons a year.
The organisation of the marketing has been
taken over by the various Governments; but
because of a yearning to do everything in
secret, or because of ineradicable ineptitude,
the possibilities of the business being landed
into a state of complete disorganisation look
fairly numerous. What The Bulletin
wants to know is whether Hughes will
make a full statement as to what the Wheat
Board is doing, as to what the programme
of the British and American Governments is
in respect to Australian wheat, as to the
measures that are being taken to protect
the stocks already on the premises and the
possibility or impossibility of shipping being
diverted from the Atlantic to the only place
in the world with a surplus of cereals await-
ing transport. If he will not, then the time
is about ripe for the States to take a hand
in the proceedings independently of the
Commonwealth. The existing uncertainty is
preposterous, and only needs nrolonging for
a few months to be disastrous. If
the Allies cannot send for Australian wheat
and are not looking hitherwards for further
supplies, why not say so at once? If they
do want Australia’s wheat and will be able
to liberate shipping from the Atlantic to
get it, then why not make the grower as
wise as the speculator in scrip?

The State as Storekeeper.
From a man who has grown up in the
business: —
It should be possible for a scheme of price-fixing
to be brought about within the trade. All but the
straight-out business shark will admit that protection
should be granted to the lowest paid or most improvi-
dent class as Tar as certain of the necessities of life
are concerned. Price-fixing as at present practised
by well-meaning amateurs, however, is the crude
rule-of-thumb sort of thing the doctrinaire evolves
out of an excellent theory, and it fails because its
basis is unsound. I have had a finger in a good few
“honorable understandings” (as price-fixing is called
when it isn’t done to win a War), but they never
went beyond “bread-and-butter lines,” as the trade
calls them—goods that everybody buys. These are
the lines most frequently cut and most in need of
protection. Any attempt at comprehensive price-
raising has failed, because someone always broke
away—just as Government price-lowering is failing
now, because nearly everybody is chancing prosecu-
tion. The Prices Board dabbles in too many things
—pokes its little stick into too many interacting
business cogs.
In a free market price-manipulation is done far
more to regulate demand and output than as a sinis-
ter result of “combining.” It is an ordinary busi-
ness process that acts instantly, even in a tiny
suburban pie-shop. Profiteering in staple lines
defeats its own end, for people cease to buy luxury-
lines at a certain point, and they so raise Cain about
the price of bread-and-butter ones that no combine
would dare face the music.
In every shop there are certain lines that fail to
pay directly, yet they are stocked by every store,
except a few of the great “multiples,” which are
strong enough to defy both manufacturers and public
demand. Generally they are commodities so widely
advertised by their proprietors that they sell them-
selves. I can call to mind a tea, a pill or two, a
galatea, a boot, a shovel, a kerosene, a whisky. They
are called “proprietaries,” arid it is for these the
shopkeeper always offers a substitute “just as good”
—often it really is so; and always it pays better. The
proprietors of these non-paying lines give the ad.-
men their big profit-margin instead of the retailer,
who only stocks the lines because he may lose trade
if he doesn’t. Avoiding their sale shows the re-
tailer’s adeptness as a salesman, and his justification
for being there at all.
Now, as a basis for a price-fixing scheme, I suggest
a State adoption of the proprietaiy-article idea. Let
the State choose and put upon the market a series
of State-originated staple commodities sufficient for
the whole simple needs of a people— and fix the
prices of them. The State would control the produc-
tion of these, from the seed to the completed article,
so that the parasitic middleman might be entirely
eliminated.
Men outback have told me that in the early days
they have done well and provided for a family on the
old weekly station-ration of “10-10-2-4”—101b. flour,
101 b. meat, 21b. sugar, and Alb. tea—augmented by
the ancient £1 a week in cash. The State might
therefore choose as its own “proprietaries” a whole-
meal flour, a joint, a cheap but wholesome sugar, a
tea, a flannel, a cotton cloth, a tweed, a wool, a
burning oil, a hardwood timber—even (if anxious
for work) a boot, a suit, a hat, a tobacco, a beer, a
furniture suite, a go-cart, a corset and a chewing-
gum —grown on its own farms by its own labor and
made up in its own factories (it already has some of
them), selling each commodity at its actual produc-
ing-cost f.a.s. at the place of distribution. The rest
could well be left to Private Enterprise, business
competition and our old friend Supply-and-Demand.
(It will be noticed the war has forced Britain to do
a little in this way. My father is using yards of
good ad.-space to beg people to come and buy sugar
from him at no profit at all!)
Retailers would gladly stock the State lines for the
sake of the other custom they would bring. If they
gave credit for State goods, delivered them, or other-
wise piled up “overhead” on their sale, it would
be the retailers’ own funeral. The people, even the
poorest, woidd be able to get in any centre whole-
some if not luxurious living, at bedrock cost unin-
flated by middleman’s wiles; and what more could
anyone ask? This done, ordinary business competi-
tion could be left to handle other commodities.
Such a system would save the State the heavy cost
of distribution in State shops—the item that has
euchred State commercial experiments in the past.
The State does not avail itself half enough of the
“customs of the trade”—an allowance post-office
never “pays” the allowee; but did one ever hear of
a shopkeeper refusing to take the job on?
Rule-of-thumb price-fixing as at present carried
out is illogical, often grossly unfair to i ho “fixed”
and satisfactory to no one. It, moreover, puts a
premium on dishonesty and evasion of the Law, and
that is bad for any member of a community, even a
grocer.

A Possible Funeral for Party
Government.
Dear Bulletin, —Last night the previous
clog next door kept me half awake by his
discourse with the subsequent cat up the
street, and I dreamed about a way to under-
mine the Party Government curse. (Perhaps
the idea isn’t new, but that can’t be helped.)
Let Tudor move :
This House has completely lost confidence in the
Prime Minister, Mr. Hughes, and, besides, it never
had any.
Probably the Speaker would rule the motion
out of order, but a majority can disagree
from the Speaker’s ruling. The House hav-
ing disagreed, let Tudor state his case
against the Dictator with all the force at his
command, but say not one syllable against
the other Ministers. Let instructions be
passed to his followers to stick to the doings
of the Dictator, and speak nothing except in
kindness about liis colleagues. Of course
there are difficulties. First, it would be
hard to prevent the rank and file from flying
off the handle, and attacking the Cabinet in
general, thereby including them in the gen-
eral condemnation and driving them to sup-
port the leader of whom they are so very
tired. Secondly, the move would imply that
Tudor refrained from pegging out his pros-
pective allotment on the Treasury bench—
that he made no claim whatever and left the
question of Hughes’s successor quite open.
To prevent newspaper paragraphs about
coalitions, or the division of the skin before
the bear’s death, or about how Smith (who
is uncertain) may become Trade Commis-
sioner at Punta Arenas, or how Jones (who
wobbles) is marked down to make inquiries
about shipbuilding at Timbuctoo, Tudor’s
followers and well-wishers should simply
receive an intimation at the last moment like
this: —
Be in your place when the House meets, and vou
may see an INTERESTING DOG-FIGHT.
Certainly the leader of the Opposition might
get neither place nor pay out of the business
for himself, but on the other hand he
couldn’t get less than he is getting now. If
he failed he would be remembered as the
man who blazed a new track and hit an old
superstition intelligently in the waistcoat.
He would extend his influence whether he
won on lost. And if success came his way
he might work out on top in the general
scramble and breakage. To the man who
established the rule that the House can sack
an obnoxious Minister without being com-
pelled to sacrifice the entire Ministry many
things would be possible. The first result
of the victory would be visible when Hughes
went to Government House with a new
reconstruction scheme, and was told that he
was the only man in Parliament who was
disqualified.—Yours, etc., Henry Horse-
collar.

Unemployment Insurance.
Unemployment insurance is one of the
newest Ma State schemes for spending money
and buying the applause that means votes.
It is a German device, and it worked well in
Germany before the war. Thanks to a high
scientific tariff and a fine system of technical
education, there was work for practically
every able-bodied Hun bar lunatics, and a
very able-bodied law pursued the impecuni-
ous loafer with a job in both hands. In easy-
going Australia the Government flatly re-
fuses to find more work for the able-bodied
citizen —the aged and crippled are already
provided for by old age and invalid pensions
—per medium of a tariff like that of Ger-
many and an anti-loafing policy akin to that
of the spiked-helmet country. In other
words, there is a tendency to adopt the
spending part of the German policy, but no
other part. The cost, in consequence, is
likely to be bigger than anticipated. That is
always so. As a case in point, consider the
Federal outlay for old age and invalid pen-
sions. There was no increase in the pension
rates between 1910 and 1916. The invoice
didn’t include war pensions. There was
nothing to cause a great increase in hopeless
invalids among those who didn’t go to war.
There was no special outbreak of poverty.
The population grew by only 10 per cent.
The aged didn’t grow old any faster than
usual. Yet old age and invalid pensions rose
from £1,497.485 in 1910 to £2 891.597 for the
12 months to June, 1916. That is to say,
they very nearly doubled. It was a jump in
six years from 6s. 9d. per inhabitant per
annum to 11s. lOd. per inhabitant. That
doesn’t prove fraud or mal. dministration or
carelessness, but it does prove that the hill
has a tendency to grow up in the night like
Jonah’s gourd without visible cause; and
an eye should be kept on all such vegetables.
The City State.
The large boasts of an empire on which
the sun never sets, or of a federation spread-
ing over so many thousand miles of fertile
territory, which have been absurdly mixed
up with the idea of patriotism in the past,
should have a lot of stones thrown at them
now when the world is on the eve of redis-
tribution of rights and reorganisation of
nations. Size, wealth, territorial expansion
and the like are not the things that make
a nation or a people great. The man who
owns a big, badly-cultivated farm is not
greater in any sense than the man who has
a small one which has been taught to grow
things to its utmost limit. No sane person
would say that Babylon was a greater city
than Athens, although it was probably a
hundred times larger, or that the vast empire
of the Moguls was worthier than the in-
finitely smaller empire of Augustus. It is
cjuality and not quantity that makes a State
great or small ; and the only conceivable
reason for a far-reaching empire is the sup-
ply of man-power for special extravagances
of stoush. If world-peace under an inter-
national police system could be established,
there would be nothing more desirable than
a return to the old city State, or something
as like it as possible. The tendency of large
States is to keep a dead level of mediocrity
in all matters that really count, while the
small State, complete within itself, can leap
away into the front ranks of progress and do
things in the waiting for which the hearts
of the people in a larger community would
be broken. The two greatest world-influ-
ences in the history of man were those of
Athens and Jerusalem; and both could to-
day be lost in the meat jungle of Chicago.
.
The True Story of the Celtic Movement,
Trousers are the emblem of the great Celtic
victory over the races of the earth.
Crushed, beaten, bruised by the various mobs
of Teutonic plunderers who in turn deso-
lated Europe, the Celt won to final triumph
by imposing on the legs of all nations his
own ancestral bags. When the bare-legged
Roman struck the Gallic tribes beyond the
Alps the first thing he noticed about them
Was that they wore trousers; and the
antiquity of the garments in other Celtic
lands is proved by the sculptured efiigies of
certain early residents of Ireland which still
preserve in mouldering stone the tailoring
modes of a very ancient time. How deep
and far the conspiracy to clothe the nether
limbs of the nations must have gone can be
judged by the fact that, to allay all sus-
picion, those astute Celts adopted the kilt
to their own discomfort, and gradually wove
a web of desire for the discarded pants
about their future victims. After a long
night of agony the Irish section of the Celtic
race was heard at the beginning of the 19th
century making a loud outcry about the loss
of its Parliament and the breaking of the
Treaty of Limerick and other matters; but
secretly it was keeping its tongue in its
cheek. For in truth the triumph of the race
had begun to blossom on the ancient stem.
Man by man the English people became
trousered; race by race the nations of
Europe followed the fashion. The move-
ment reached America, spread to Africa,
touched the uttermost bounds of Asia. Mis-
sionaries preached trousers to the heathen ;
picturesque Oriental potentates put their
robes aside to wrestle with braces and but-
tons ; the very feminist movement itself
hoisted them as its banner and insignia. And
the Celt, particularly the Irish Celt, looked
on and smiled. Without a blow he had con-
quered the male world up to the waist.
Fired with the splendor of the triumph, the
Celtic literary revival made an assault upon
the top end of the human race. Rapidly
it is subduing the brain and heart of man-
kind ; and while it is doing so, France—the
splendidly Celtic France which stands as the
noblest defender of freedom and well-cooked
meals —has made its delicate attack upon the
world-stomach. So the victory promises to
be - complete all along the line; for when
trousers and food and thought combine they
are practically masters of the whole man.
True, in spite of submarines, Britain con-
tinues to rule the waves; but she does so in
ancient Irish trousers of the very widest
sort. What matters the nailing of colors to
the mast if the very holder of the hammer
and tacks has the sign of the conqueror
buttoned on his legs?

If Germany didn’t pay somebody for this,
then somebody did a job for Germany at
much too cheap a price. The Allies are in
great danger of being starved into submis-
sion. This country’s men don’t very seri-
ously matter to Germany; but to keep Aus-
tralia from sending away its 8,000,000 tons
of wheat —enough to feed Britain and its
army for a year—is worth millions to Ger-
many. Now, on July 27 last, according to
Holman, Premier of N. S. Wales, “a definite
proposal was made by the Broken Hill Prop.
Co. for the commencement of shipbuilding
in this State.” On inquiry as to what pre-
cisely it was prepared to do, the eo. inti-
mated that “it would immediately commence
the building of four steel ships of about 5000
tons.” The offer of this powerful and ener-
getic co. with ample resources of steel and
money was made on July 27. At the end of
January—six months later—not a tap of
work had been done; and Lord Rhondda,
the British Food Commissioner, was cabling
to the U.S.A. that if food wasn’t forthcoming
the Allies must give in. Now, who was re-
sponsible? Who served Germany, for money
or otherwise? Holman blames the Federal
Government. This paper doesn’t know. All
it knows is that it is somebody, and it is a
solemn national duty to find him (or them)
out.

classes conducted by the W.E.A. The method of
one-hour lecture, followed by one hour’s discussion,
reinforced by essay work and a good library, is

REDUCING THE FARMER’S EXPENSES.
Every farmer in the Commonwealth knows how the cost of his necessities has increased during the last few years. Any scheme which will reduce
his expenses should, therefore, command his earnest attention.
Ti e Farmers’ & Settlers’Co-operative Insurance Co. of A
ustralia
i Limited
is designed for one object—to CHEAPEN THE COST OF FARMERS’ INSURANCE.
Farmers’ insurance business is productive of good profits, and the farmer-promoters of this Company realised that if the farmers of the Commonwealth
could be organised to co-operate to undertake their own insurances, the profits would be retained for distribution among the co-operating po.ici
holders themselves —thus materially reducing the cost of their insurances.
This Company was formed for that purpose over two years ago; and thousands of farmers have already co-operated. Dividends of SIX PER CENT,
were paid on the operations of each of the two years.

No other office can offer the farmer more; but by becoming a co-operator in the Farmers’ and Settlers’ Co-operative Insurance Co. of Australia, Ltd., he
is eligible to share in the entire profits. Full, particulars will be supplied on application to the Manager.

The Pending Collision.
That there have been serious riots and
strikes in Germany seems to be a fact; and
the suggestion that the disturbances are part
of an elaborate system of camouflage for the
undoing of the Allies may be swept aside as
too ridiculous to bother about. It is con-
ceivable that the official Hun may find com-
fort in the reflection that news of “indus-
trial unrest” in the Fatherland will en-
courage the proletariat elsewhere to get
restless. But it is inconceivable that Pots-
dam should not prefer to carry on business
without the strike and the distribution of
inflammatory literature and crowds of men
and women yelling outside the palace for
peace and food. For once disturbances of
this kind get into working order, how far
they will go and what the consequences will
be is beyond the wit of anyone to foretell —
especially if the pantry is getting empty.
In all probability the unpleasantness in
Germany is an indication of the supply of
food being desperately short rather than of
popular anxiety to challenge the authority
of the mailed fist. If this is so the circum-
stance will most certainly encourage the
Kaiser to push on with the offensive on the
Western Front and in the direction of Italy.
Apart from the domestic usefulness of secur-
ing a victory if the thing is possible, there
are strong military reasons why the German
should attack the Allies with all the force
at his command without delay. There ap-
pears to be a general belief that the enemy
intends to move more or less promptly.
Whether the fronts will hold firm remains
to be seen, but we had better get out of our
mind any lurking ideas that France will
be able to withstand this sort of thing
indefinitely. She has been most terribly
mauled, and the warning given last year by
Joffre to the United States Congress rested
upon something more solid than rumor.
Uncle Sam is urgently wanted at the place
where everything else is being decided, and
the man from over the Atlantic will be
called upon to take a very much more im-
portant part in the disturbance by the end
of the year than was previously imagined.
Britain and America may yet, indeed, stand
almost alone between European democracy
and a militarist mastery of every nation in
Europe and of many far distant.
