The Way to Berlin.
Pessimists are remarking that the Huns
seem in a fair way to do unto the Allies all
that the Allies threatened to do unto them.
Germany was to be starved into submission ;
it is the Allies that tremble for their food
supply. Germany was to be invaded from Italy
via Austria ; it is Austria that holds a bit
of Italy. At the beginning of last year
Bonar Law talked knowingly of what a dif-
ference it would make if we could detach one
of her allies from ‘ Germany. Then came the
detachment of our Russian ally from us.
Our side is the biter that has been bitten —
thus far. But there is at least one good
chance to hoist the Huns with their own
petard. Germany for three years has
threatened to make Britain sue for peace
by bombing and bombarding her non-com-
batant population in unfortified towns.
When shall we begin to test German forti-
tude in the same way? Germany’s eager
expectation of a panic throughout Britain
as the result of aerial raids argues that she
judged British civilian character by her own.
How much longer is the world to Avait before
Huns at home are put to a trial under which
they were sure an enemy would crumple?
A Maoriland soldier, long-time prisoner in
Germany, but hoav interned in Switzerland,
says that he witnessed the first of the Brit-
ish air raids on Mannheim, and, “though it
failed, it had a considerable effect upon the
people. He is convinced that if raids were
continued vigorously they would do much
to hasten the end of the war.” Since then
there have been raids on Mannheim which
didn’t fail, and the latest bombing of Karls-
ruhe is said to have moved a large section ot
the population to march around town howl-
ing for peace. If we can thrash the llnns
anywhere it is surely in the air, where their
40 years of preparation, their organisation,
their central position and strategic railways
don’t count.
♦
The boldest woman this paper has met for
a day or two is Miss Ettie Rout, hon. sec.
of the Maoriland Soldiers’ Medical Club at
Hornchurch, Essex, England. The purpose
of this club, “established with the approval
of the M.L. military authorities in England
and at their request'’ is “to provide members
of the M.L. Expeditionary Force with the
necessary articles for the medical prevention
of venereal disease.” Says Miss Rout:
If it were possible for me to run a properly-
conducted licensed house for our troops here I
would do it, for the sake of protecting the M.L.
nation against further invasion by venereal disease.
It is not possible to do that at present, and therefore
we have to rely merely on prophylactics... .1 or
three years we have refused to accept our soldiers
views: that women are indispensable. The result
has been utterly deplorable. In the nature of
things we M.L. war-workers can give only social
values to our men abroad; the “other women give
more. I think it is clearly my duty to see that
this latter “giving” is done under reasonably safe
conditions. There will be neither more nor less
because of the element of safety. But the dreadful
menace will be removed from many a M.L. soldier's
home, and his children and grandchildren escape the
blighting mildew of venereal disease.
So this brave woman, recognising that
“patriotic funds” may be jeopardised it used
for the purpose without the express sanction
of the subscribers, is making her own appeal
(money may be sent care of the High Com-
missioner for M.L.) ; though, seeing that,
according to official records, the war services
of from 00 to 70 Maorilanders are being tem-
porarily lost every week through infection,
there ought to be no question whatever about
supplying the necessary money from public
funds. The medical authority that under-
takes to cure a disease and pay the sufferer
wages while he is in hospital, but won’t pre-
vent the disease, surely has a kink in its
intellect.
Presumably the public knows by this time
whv the building of ships to move our grain
and help the very nearly starving Allies h'as
not been started in Australia. Apart from
the suspicion that it has been made profitable
to some people to stand in the way, it will
be noticed that, so far as the Commonwealth
Government is concerned, prodigious efforts
have been exerted throughout to throw the
entire responsibility for delay upon the
unions. When the Broken Hill Prop, offered
in the middle of last year to build four 5000
tons ships at once, it did not say: “We’ll do
this if we can make an agreement with
unions beforehand not to have any rows.’
The co. was prepared to take the risks, as
every business man has to. When ploughing-
time comes round a farmer doesn’t leave liis
teams unyoked until lie can get an under-
taking that there won’t be any labor troubles
during harvest. If he did the world would
soon be without wheat. No manufacturer
asks or expects to be guaranteed immunity
from labor troubles before lie puts in new
machinery: he knows, in fact, that any
guarantee would simply be useless. _ In
demanding such a guarantee from the unions
before starting shipbuilding the Common-
wealth Government was demanding some-
thing which some Ministers at any rate
knew they would not get; and they relied
upon that refusal to damn the unions as
disloyalists. In plain words, these A in-the-
War Ministers are quite satisfied that the
wheat should stay in Australia, even if the
Allies are starved into submission, so long
as the unions are sufficiently damned. It
is time the daily papers refused to be a
party to this conspiracy. They could knock
the bottom out of it inside a week if they
plainly intimated that they are concerned
more about feeding the Allies and saving
civilisation than in feeding a grudge against
local unions and saving the face of one or
two vindictive politicians.
.Tudoed by the fine levied on Admiral Poore
for having in an isolated county-house sup-
plies in the cupboard that would look ordi-
nary in an Australian home a few miles
from a railway, food-hoarding in England is
now so severely interpreted that the only
way in which a family can safely feed itself
is to run out to the shop and buy what is
wanted for each meal. The bitterness of the
deer-park must be felt at last at John
Bull’s table. Had the nation been forced
to maintain itself in time of peace on home-
grown products, the land question would
have been settled long ago, and the produc-
tiveness of the country made so great that
the submarine would have been a jest. On
the other hand, it is very questionable
whether the great overseas trade in food is
good for the peoples who produce it. for
everywhere in new countries that trade
has the development of industrj.
t 'loseness to the soil seems necessary to keep
man at his best; and the person who drinks
milk from Switzerland and eats buttei tiom
Denmark, fish from Newfoundland, mutton
from Australia and sugar from South America
Ims lost touch with the realities. And the pri-
son who supplies him is only a degree better.
The carrying trade of the world may be a
great thing to possess; but the less distance
life's necessities require to be carried the
bettter for everybody.
Australia lias been smitten hard now in
its rabbit. That animal has gone to join the
surplus wheat. In each case we relied on
selling the surplus to the British Govern-
ment. Bull to take delivery here in his own
ships. Now he has intimated pretty clearly
that he can’t spare the ships for such long-
distance carrying. This, it is estimated, will
throw 10,000 or 12,000 men out ot work, per-
haps more - it will badly damage the ireezing
industry - and about 22,000,000 rabbits and
hares which used to bring in much money
will have to be pursued at great expense
else they will eat the country out of house
and home. The rabbit difficulty, in its small
way, is worse than the wheat problem. The
wheat at the worst can be burnt to make a
Roman holiday, and the ash may serve as a
kind of fertiliser, but bunny won’t stand
still to be lighted.
The glad news is to hand that during the
week ended January 20 1,086,851 bags of
wheat were brought to country railway
stations in N. S. Wales, and a trifle of
205.202 bags were got away. This left over
11,800,000 bags (close on 9,000,000 of it a
full year old) doing the dry-rot act in the
country and hoping for the best. But all is
not lost. The Hughes Government states
that no wooden ships—the kind that can be
built quickly—will be constructed, but in a
short time—how short no one knows —the
keels of certain steel vessels will be laid
down, and in two of these cases “prelimin-
aries are well advanced.” Unhappily all the
preliminaries on earth won’t carry one bushel
of grain. Preliminaries don’t float.
From an old chief: —
Nearly 30 years ago Edward Gillon, then editor of
the Post in Wellington (M.L.), acquired a new re-
porter who had drifted into the inky way through
the hamlets ,of Hawke’s Bay. He was a long, raw
youth decorated with a crop of flaming red hair
and possessed of a confidence that was sublime.
Before long Gillon turned him on to Parliament and
in five minutes or so the young man had a news
scoop which made the Windy Isles rock. He got
possession of a report furnished by Colonel Fox on
the local defences while the document was in its
confidential stage, and the publication started a
violent rumpus whose sole result was to advertise
Gilion’s recruit. The recruit’s name was E. D.
Hoben, and he died this week after a sorrowful ill-
ness at Melbourne. Hoben left the Post in the long
ago to join Sydney Telegraph, but soon went over
to the Fairfaxes, and was something or other on the
Mail for a dozen years or so. Then he drifted back
to Maoriland with a young, motherless family, had
exciting experiences on the AVellington Times, experi-
mented with country journalism for a while, had an
adventure in matrimony and came again to Australia
a few months ago to join the Melbourne Herald.
He was then 54 and a broken man. Getting that
Fox report seemed a great achievement at the time.
But it was the worst thing that could have hap-
pened to Hoben. He never got over it nor con-
quered an impulse to stand up and make a speech.
He was one of the promoters and first secretary of
the Maoriland Rugby Union.
Died this week, John McLaughlin, one of
the best known of Sydney solicitors. He had
been in poor health for months, and the
death of a brilliant son in action was the
last straw. John McLaughlin got often into
the public eye—years ago as a politician;
later, after an illness, as plaintiff in an
action which he brought against Sydney
Telegraph and other cos. for registering
transfers of shares of his while he was not
attending to his own affairs. On his re-
covery he carried the action through court
after court and won, achieving a judgment
which no company secretary is likely to ever
forget.
There was a curious study in the psycho-
logy of crowds on the Semaphore (S.A.)
beach on the last holiday. 'The crowd was
surrounding a mild sort of Wild West Show
run by a person of great respectability and
the ancient name of Smith. Events hap-
pened rapidly in this order: (1) A small
girl tried to butt in under the canvas, was
pushed out again, bleeding slightly from the
forehead, and raised a yell. (2) An elderly,
excitable and eloquent stranger addressed
the crowd on the proposition that to stab small
girls is Hunnish and intolerable. (3) The
crowd pulled down the tent and wrecked
the show. (4) Smith in his turn made a
tearful speech to the effect that the girl had
got her face scratched in being quite law-
fully turned back by a woman usher, that
he had been unaware of the matter unai
taken by the storm, and that he and his
large young family (produced) were now
ruined. (5) The crowd changed its alleged
mind again, and sent round the hat—not for
the kid, but for the Smith family.
Died at Melbourne, in his 36th year, Pri-
vate Francis C. Mason, second son of the
one-time Victorian Speaker. A chill caught
in camp shortly after lie had enlisted, and
followed by a more serious trouble, was the
cause of death. Before becoming a private
Mason was a clerk in the Leg. Ass. A bro-
ther is a major with a D.S.O.
McGirr, Labor M.L.A., on the N.S.W.
censure motion
He was against the motion because he was confi-
dent it was just what the Leader of the Government
wanted. He knew this, because he had sat behind
the Premier. But, being a member of the party, he
would vote for it.
And there you have another fine advertise-
ment for the honesty of politics under the
party system.
The State War Councils are doing their
bit in the matter of repatriation, at any rate.
In 1916 Parliament handed them £250,000 to
go on with, and this is how the cash went up
to June 30, T 7: —

Auditor-General Israel says the Queensland
accounts were kept in a most unsatisfactory
manner, quantities of private contributions
to the fund being omitted, while irregulari-
ties and illegal disposals of grants have been
reported in all the States. Furthermore,
large numbers of instalments of loan repay-
ments are in arrear, Victoria having achieved
the feat of collecting only 14 per cent, of
repayments due. But auditors make no
allowance for what may happen when one is
winning a war or eating dirt or working a
point or whatever you like to call it.
“Square Deal”: S. Australia takes a pride
in the care it gives to its sick and afflicted;
and for that reason the Defence Depart-
ment’s neglect of its obligations with regard
to shell-shock and other mental cases among
S. Australian returned soldiers is provoking
the bitterest resentment. With the excep-
tion of the ordinary accommodation at the
Parkside Lunatic Asylum, absolutely no
provision is made in the State for the treat-
ment of these most unfortunate of our sol-
diers. Slight cases may be treated at one
or other of the Military Hospitals for a time,
but are usually discharged long before they
are cured, with the result that they wander
about as they like, and no attempt is made
to train their faculties and gradually win
them back to normal. Bad cases are sent to
the asylum, where they are on exactly the
same footing as the other inmates. There
are about half a dozen “mentals” who go
regularly to the Cheer-Up Hut. Their pre-
sence is very bad for the “nerve-y” men who
go there, and they are often a considerable
embarrassment to the workers. Attention
lias been again and again called to the need
for a proper Home, but so far no announce-
ment has been made of any such institution
being under consideration even. Most of
these men could be so easily restored by the
right surroundings that it is outrageous
that they should be allowed to drift about
the streets uncared for and unguarded.
“N’importe”: Having brought the Aus-
tralian pastoralists to heel in the Federal
Arbitration Court and secured a liberal
award, the A.W.U. is after the scalp of the
cockies in the N. S. Wales State Court.
Claims have been filed, and the opening
skirmishes took place just before Christmas.
A 48-hour week is asked for workers in the
agricultural, horticultural, viticultural and
dairying industries, and wages at various
rates up to £4 per week and keep; also that
no infants under 14 be employed. While the
Federal award applies only to those station-
owners who were big enough to be “cited”
before the Federal Court, the State award
when made will apply to every cocky employ-
ing labor, no matter how small his holding
Maybe £4 and keep for 48 hours’ toil is a
fair thing; but as cocky has to send his stuff
away to compete with people who don’t work
on those lines, there seems to be a tough
time ahead for somebody.
“Fore-Stay”: While Australia is still
chinwagging as to how, when |md where, a
start is to be made at shipbuilding, the prin-
cipal Commonwealth ports-are populated by
vessels constructed since the war started.
The four-, five- and even six-masted schoon-
ers now familiar in Australian harbors are
nearly all new ; also they are floating gold
mines for the strangers who own them. One
Canadian syndicate alone has turned out 12
large schooners in the last few months, and
the profits from these vessels repay the capi-
tal cost in two or three trips. One Yankee
skipper visiting Sydney rec ntly showed me
that his “dinghy” was returning his owners
£24,000 a year.

If “The Easiest Way,” produced at Mel-
bourne Royal last Saturday, were a little
less monotonous in tone, the dialogue, which
is only sometimes excessive and tedious,
would keep it entertaining. The simple story
is cheap and false, but the talk mostly has a
ring of truth, and where it touches the eter-
nal question of Sex against Sex it is very
forcible. Instance the indignation of the
fair-dealing profligate on discovering that his
mistress has made him appear dishonorable
to a Man —another Man! And the inability
of the two ladies of easy virtue to think it
possible that a man could have any feeling
but jealousy in warning another man against
marrying a pretty girl. Of the four charac-
ters in the play (two others being only
make-weights) the bloated capitalist is suffi-
ciently real, and the incidental Elfie, who
considers herself as a mere business proposi-
tion, is an excellent photograph of a type.
It is, of course, easy for a smart girl with
a hard voice to naturalise such parts; but
Miss Norma Mitchell is a conspicuous
success. Frank
Harvey does well
as the usually
well - conducted
“protector,” pre-
sented as a bearish
person when the
sympathy of the
audience is most
ea mostly desi red
for the soiled
heroine, but really
a decent sort of
giddy bachelor who
doesn’t take women
seriously, and is
not likely to
change hi s views
after his experi-
ences with Laura.
She is a somewhat
vixenish Camille,
without the con-
sumptive cough.
From first to last
the character is
unconvincing ;
nevertheless Muriel
Starr, despite her
weakening voice,
gets some good
theatrical effects
out of it, especially
when she assures
The Panto., Melbourne
King's.
The Widow Twankey
(Bert Bailey).
her generous
friend that she hates him. The part, how-
ever, gets too much for her at the finish.
Kimball, as the noble journalist, gets in his
one touch of nature just before the end, when
he turns suddenly cynical and cold-hearted,
and calls Maggie Moore in to see her mis-
tress funk the ordeal of shooting herself.
Atholwood gives a capital stage-character
study of an angelic, Dickensesque old battler,
and three scenes by Upward, the first a
rather ultra-picturesque landscape, are far
more effective than the orchestra that has at
least a couple of long, long waits to fill in.

At Melbourne Princess’s the sun of melo-
drama has set for a while, and Australian
comic opera is about to shine. “The Rajah
of Shivapore,” by Alfred Hill and David
Souter, will get a chance on Saturday.
Because it is a purely local production “The
Rajah” will be interesting to every good Aus-
tralian, and inasmuch as it has received its
baptism of praise on the other side of the
Murray, the show should attract those others
who have no national sentiment to speak of.
The Puller management promises a liberal
supply of orchestra, chorus and ballet, whilst
prices remain popular as usual.

Melbourne seems to have made up its mind
that “Winnie Brooke,” at the Tivoli, is a
particularly nice thing in widows. Her im-
personations of Ada Reeve in vaudeville
would make her popular if she had nothing
else to do, but, as a matter of fact, she
does a lot of other things in a w T ay which
Ada Reeve must have taught her. Finally
she leaves the audience in doubt as to
whether it saw Ada impersonating Winnie
or Winnie impersonating Ada. The identi-
ties get mixed.
In “The Spirit of Romance” at Iloyt’s
Melbourne house this week, Vivian Martin
is a rest cure to a millionaire debilitated by
dollars. Vivian is a great success in the
part. She belongs to the demure class of
flappers who act on men like a Sunday even-
ing in Geelong. The Artcraft feature,
“Down ,to Earth,” gets all its dynamic
energy from Douglas Fairbanks. That
absurd young man sneaks into an hypochon-
driacs’ retreat and proceeds to restore the
apparently dead with judicious shocks to
their nervous systems.

Douglas Fairbanks was also let loose for
the first half of this week at Melbourne
Britannia, where he revived a previous phase
of his excitable existence called “Manhattan
Madness.” To-day Irene Hunt is showing
the awful possibilities of female perfection
in tiie Triangle attraction, "The Stainless
Barrier.” The Melba commenced this week
with Theda Bara in “The Darling of Paris,”
which has been adapted from incidents in
“The Hunchback of Notre Dame.” Theda
appeared as Esmeralda, and repeated her old
trick of using the dove as a spiritual camou-
flage for the serpent. Miss Bara will be
succeeded to-day by Carlyle Blackwell in a
now World film entitled “The Burglar.”

of Eliza Allen at Melbourne Auditorium to-
morrow. On Saturday “The Conqueror”
gives place to a Fox version of “Camille,”
in which Theda Bara provides the tubercu-
losis and tears.
The dainty little pseudo-Jap romance,
“The Willow Tree,” having got over its
teething troubles, is doing well at Sydney
Criterion. Kathlene Macdonell’s artistic
presentation of the lady who is “half a
woman and half a tree” is being rapturously
received by a repentant audience.
Little Maie Baird, the smiling dancer in
the freak dress, is one of the daintiest items
in “The Bing Boys Are Here,” a stupendous
show whose plot is as disconnected as a
Sydney telephone conversation. But if there
were more plot there would be less room
for the specialties; and the specialties all
glitter and tinkle beautifully, whereas a plot
very often only thuds. Business is so good
that Geo. Matheson nightly stands at the
back of the circle wishing that Sydney
Majesty’s could eat up as big a crowd as the
sister house in Melbourne, which can swal-
low 500 more.
“Turn to the Right” will be turned to the
right-about at Sydney Palace after Friday
night’s performance. “Peg o’ My Heart”
starts a brief revival on the following even-
ing.

“Steve Hart”: Out West (N.S.W.) weVe
got the mouse plague again, and, worse still,
it’s responsible for another horror. I lie
beasts are simply swarming with microscopic:
fleas. Starling vermin are nothing compared
with them; horses, cattle, dogs, cats, etc.,
are nearly driven mad.
Bowen, up near Townsville, is a peaceful
•spot with plenty of time on its hands. A
cow asleep in the afternoon is revolutionary
by comparison, and is conspiring to upset
the throne. Yet there is a Federal police-
man trampling down the street grass at
Bowen, and if somebody maddened him by-
raising social and political and religious dis-
sension outside bis house when lie was asleep
he would have to run the criminal in, for

“Camelero” : Born in Russia and educated
in Germany and France, Frank Matzonas set
out to see the world just before Berlin Bill
lifted the lid of Sheol. After doing U.S.A.
and the South Seas, lie drifted to Sydney,,
and considering it a good country to fight for
he joined a Light Horse unit leaving for
Egypt. Later on, he transferred to the
Camels, and after two years’ campaigning
a German machine-gunner rang down the
curtain on him at BeCrslieba. The same
machine-gunner also killed Tom Stewart,
known almost wherever there’s a call for
“Tar 1” in the backblocks of Queensland and
New South.
An old hand analyses the Commonwealth
Government’s failure as a Win-the-War
trader: —
This week we could not buy more than 21b. of
sugar in our town. Now, in 17 years of selling
sugar—-with the exception of a few weeks while
Irvine was making his name settling the Vic. rail-
way strike —I never knew what shortage meant in
the sugar department. The price fluctuated a bit,
perhaps, but there was always sugar to sell. This
•illustrates the difference between State and private-
trading. The private trader owns an abstract thing
the State can’t understand —business reputation. He
prides himself on being always able to “deliver the
goods,” and he goes to much trouble and expense
(which he passes on, of course) to fulfil that obliga-
tion. Tlie State doesn’t get down to this abstraction.
If it is in difficulties about getting sugar or cornsaeks
or ships—well, its customers have just got to jolly
well wait. Now the price of a thing is not half such a
vital point as the yelpers after fixed-price would
have us believe. Most folk would much rather have
plenty sugar at 4d. a pound than no sugar
at all at 31d. —a woman can’t make jam out of
gratitude to a grandmotherly legislature. In tin-
first sugar shortage, when Hughes dabbled in
C.S.R. affairs in 1915, I was repeatedly offered os.
a bag on my regular price to induce me to let go
the sugar I was holding, and the majority of people
would gladly do it now, even for second-rate Java
piebald crystals.
THE EASIEST WAY.
For love did giddy Laura yearn
E’en when a pleasant broker.
With hundred-dollar bills to burn,
As concubine bespoke her.
A life of ease and smooth content
The ri<h “friend” freely gave her,
Till fate—ah me! —unkindly sent
A sweetheart pure to save her.
To rectify her moral twist
Fate sent a Noble Journalist.
She shook her grey “protector’s” hand,
So open and forgiving,
And gave the gent to understand
She meant to earn her living
Upon the stage, where for a year
Her charms had been distracting,
Ere yet Temptation pinched her ear
And made her sick of acting.
The broker, calm and cool, but trist.
Left Laura to the Journalist.
Brave oaths of fealty they swore,
Those lovers twain converted;
She vowed she would trangress no more,
And he his trust asserted.
She went to town to look for work,
Her lode-star eyes a-shining;
Young John departed, with a smirk,
To make a pile at mining.
With swelling chest and clenched fist
Went forth that Noble Journalist.
Poor Laura seeking for a job—
An actress out of collar —
Soon sobbed the old dramatic sob
And blewed her bottom dollar.
Her jewels went, her gowns of silk;
Meanwhile her rich adorer
Thought—“ Being good on bread and milk
Is not much fun for Laura.”
And then the Serpent slyly hissed:
“Deuce take your Noble Journalist!”
There came a festive, gilded “tart,”
A sinful household treasure,
Who harrowed Laura’s aching heart
With talk of ease and pleasure;
She brought the broker to the room,
And he was all politeness;
Where poverty had shed its gloom
His money scattered brightness.
But, though her lips again were kissed,
She did not tell the Journalist.
Then he came back, a millionaire,
Or something very like it,
Who’d looked for gold in black despair,
And somehow chanced to strike it;
Came back, with wedded-bliss in view,
To bless his benefactress.
Sweet Laura still, for all he knew,
Was but an artless actress.
(She kept the facts and hid the gist
From that confiding Journalist.)
Fate, frowning on Miss Laura’s guile,
From grief did-not exempt her;
John heard of her deception vile,
And then he met the Tempter,
Who said that loose, illicit loves
(Though matters for deploring)
Are good enough for soiled doves—*
But no dove’s worth adoring.
Thus spake the man of 46:
“The love game is the same as whist—
Each player trumps the other’s tricks.”
Thus spake the world-wide moralist
Unto the rich-quick Journalist.
And Laura saw, to her dismay,
That girls may be too clever.
The good young man and broker grey,
With w’hom she chose the easy way,
Had chuckt her now forever.
The greenhorn and the cynic wise,
Each having kissed and trusted
The charmer who had told them lies,
Were each in turn disgusted.
Ah, girls! fool whoso else you list,
But play fair with a Journalist.


All are Dumb when
Beauty Pleads.
The summer is here. The Australian
climate is a severe one. Surfing, motoring, picnick-
ing will absorb much of your time. Be prepared for
the burning sun and a dry, tender and chapped skin.
It is the weather which affects your skin. If you
allow it to remain lifeless and inactive you cannot
have that rich velvety skin with a radiant color that
comes and goes.
Do not say it is useless to try to change the skin
itself. It changes every day in spite of you ; as old
skin dies new skin forms to take its place. Ihis new
skin will be just what you make it: by attention
you can have that charm of “A skin everyone envies.”
Think how many years older your face looks than
skin that is unexposed. Yet the world judges you by
your face, and that criticism is going to be more
severe through the wastage of manhood by war.
How unfair to yourself, then, not to counteract the
ill effects of dust, dirt, wind and weather, and to
have the clear, youthful, healthy and attractive skin
that is rightfully yours.
It is essential to free your skin of those tiny old
dead particles, cleanse the pores of unseen grime, and
hr in" the blood to the surface and stimulate the small
muscular fibres. It is no use doing it for a couple
of days, then neglecting it. If you persevere the
charm will come to stay. Treat your skin properly
and persistently, and you will reap a big reward.

SOCIETY
MINCHINBURY
When the Argus supports a strike it cer-
tainly goes the entire hog. The other day
rhe old paper published a letter from “A
Doctor’s Wife” in reference to the dispute
between the Victorian friendly societies and
the lodge doctors, who are striking for in-
creased pay, which contained this impressive
sentence:—
If the doctors are spineless enough to attend
patients after the threatening attitude of the ter-
rorists, one can only hope that the treatment will
he as they deserve.
Of course, ordinarily the Argus would not
dream of printing an incitement to sabotage,
because machinery and such-like proputty is
sacred ; but when it comes to messing about
with human lives that merely belong to the
—ah —lowah ordalis, the Argus does not
frown out a suggestion that, if they are not
granted the additional tuppence, or what-
ever the amount is, the doctors should prac-
tise a particularly horrible form of sabotage.
“Digger” : During one of the closing bat-
tles of the Somme, when we were held up
by mud and Fritz’s machine-guns, we were
greatly heartened by the sight of a tank
floundering to our rescue, with the inspirit-
ing legend on its side: “The Bing Boys are
Here.” Blucher was not more welcome to
Wellington than were the Bing Boys to us
that day. Months later, when going down
to billets, I saw the same tank lying smashed
up by the side of the Pozieres-rpad. Along-
side were seven graves, the resting-places of
the crew. The tank had been knocked out
by a direct hit from a shell and the crew
were burnt to death. They were buried
alongside their fortress, and their battle-cry
was their epitaph.
“Bill Bowyang”: In the recent fighting
around Beersheba and Gaza, Abdul settled
the name as a clean fighter which
he earned on Gallipoli. He repeatedly bombed
and fired on hospitals and stretcher
bearers, and the other day he sniped
wounded who were crawling to the rear. In
one part, of the line the Scotties sent us word
not to take prisoners, as Jacko was using
reversed bullets, i.e., the lead was turned so
that the blunt end entered its human
target. In most cases the wounds were
ghastly, and one-half of those who have been
sent to hospital in Cairo will return to Aus-
tralia. Copper machine-bullets accounted
for many deaths before hospitals were even
reached. Billjim (I will say it, even if he
is my mate) is the most chivalrous, the most
tolerant, enemy that ever God raised on two
legs; but at this moment he is the maddest
thing in Palestine, and God help the Turk
who comes our way!
Just as it comes from John B. Simpson :
We had just occupied the possy and were being
sniped by Turks, who were lying- about everywhere.
Good old Billy and I were together as usual. II e
were also thirsty—as usual. As we loaded and
fired, we talked of the bet we had made in Cairo
three weeks before —the loser would shave first.
Constant trekking had compelled us to give our
razors a rest for three weeks, and we were shocking-
sights. Suddenly my mate noticed two chaps get to
the cookhouse and back again. “I’m going to have
a cut at it, Doc.,” he said. “Those blokes were
getting water.” I warned him it was risky, but he
jumped out and ran down safely. 1 saw him fill his
own bottle and mine. Then he started back and
raced to within ten yards of our possy. Two bounds
from home they dropped him. I jumped out and
pulled him in. He had a wound through the side
of the head and the blood was slowly pouring out.
He did not cry out, but grinned his old cheerful
grin (I see him now as I write) and said: “Send
some of my things home, Doc., old man. My address
is in my pocket-book.” He waited a minute or so ;
then, looking up with his scrubby-bearded chin
almost touching mine, he added, “Doc, I win—you’ll
have that shave first.” A minute more and he was
dead. I buried him there and left his bandolier and
rifle over his grave.

“Camelero” (from Palestine) : While our
fellows in France have been living in puddles
our wounded here have been suffering the
tortures of the damned from thirst. Natu-
rally since our fighting has been in open
order, mostly mounted, it has taken hours to
collect the casualties. And God knows what
water is to a man suffering the agonies of
torn flesh! One of the toughest cases of
hard luck happened in our little lot. A
trooper was badly wounded and his horse
shot dead under him. Unable to move he lay
beside the carcase and waited for the ambu-
lance that did not come. His water-bottle
was on the saddle—the side of the saddle
that was underneath. He could not drag
himself round the horse; but with his
unwounded hand he began slowly scratching
a hole under it, and at last, when just about
done, his fingers touched the bottle. That
gave him hope: he burrowed a chamber and
liberated it —and then found that in the poor
beast’s struggles the cork had been released !
That was the end of the section for him, he
thought. But it wasn’t. At midnight an
English patrol heard his moans, brought him
in to a dressing-station, and he was taken
off to Cairo yesterday.

One wild night last week five Sydney
policemen were killing time inside a Syd-
ney house while the lunatic they had called
for was sitting on the roof in torrents of
rain. He had his pockets full of brick, and
bowled down repeatedly to the miserable
Hops, who wanted to see him back to the
asylum, that he would leap into the street
and provide them with an untidy inquest if
they crawled up to take him. After haggling

Anyhow, no one can reasonably say that
the Newcastle (N.S.W.) Children’s Court
should be economised out of its job because
it has nothing to do. According, to the
iccal police inspector, “Eighty per cent, of
the crimes of the district during the past
few months have been committed by chil-
dren.” On one recent day 23 youngsters
faced, the magistrate—mostly on breaking,
entering and stealing charges—and that was
reckoned typical of every weekly court day.
Seemingly the immature brigand of Coalo-
polis quite understands the value of “organi-
sation” ; in almost every suburb he has
formed himself into a “society” of from four
to 30 members, elected a secretary and
treasurer, and christened himself the Black
Hand, or some such fearsome monster. The
Court dealt with 364 cases last year; and
(he only explanation local moralists can find
for the epidemic is the tired old one of “the
evil example of the moving pictures.” It is
much more likely to be the demoralising in-
fluence of coal. Even a politician doesn’t
»eem able to touch the substance without
getting his hands soiled.

“F.N.T.”: The N. S. Wales Wine Ass.
doesn’t approve of The Bulletin sugges-
tion, put forward also by Commonwealth
Analyst Wilkinson on the same day, that
the foreign names port, sherry and so on,
as applied to Australian wines, should be
abolished. These names, the growers de-
clare, “have become generic; and when
people ask for sherry, port and other classes
they do not expect the product of France,
Spain, Italy or Portugal.” P’raps not; but
that doesn’t alter the fact that when I want
a glass of the very best claret obtainable in
Australia, I have to soecify a certain brand
of Australian wine. Otherwise I may be
served with a tiny glass of inferior, imported
wine at a higher price.
DOGS AND THE MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
v • S ,' l Maurice Abbott Anderson and Lady Anderson
jmed a taxi-cab to take them to Molesworth and
over GO miles, at a cost of £lO. The object
ot this journey was to convey the remains of a
tavonte pug dog- for interment in a 'dogs’ cemeterv.
Jhere is a dogs’ cemetery in London, but the place
was too crowded, and the calm and quiet of the
country were preferable. The corpse was encased in
a leaden casket, placed in a white wood coffin. The
long journey involved the consumption of much
petrol, and the magistrates showed their disapproval
of the whole business by fining the chauffeur £5O.
Lady Anderson paid the money, but under protest.”
A man of “blue” or baser blood
Gets killed whilst danger scorning;
Mates bury him in Flanders mud,
But have no time for mourning-.
No leaden case for his remains,
Enclosed in coffin costly—
Just earth to earth, where weeping rains
And chill winds blow across the plains.
Thus men are buried mostly.
He died to keep Old England free
And fearless as aforetime,
That rich folks, guarded by the sea,
May live at home in war-time ;
lie lived with vermin, fleas and bugs,
Whilst wasters, young or hoary,
Sat nursing sick and sorry pugs,
And wrapped the dogs in cosy rugs,
And talked of England’s glory.
If one dear doggie dies, alas!
The parlormaids and footmen
Treat Fido’s flesh as finer grass
Than that of soldier brute-men:
Of bitter cups and meagre fare
Let heroes be the tasters.
God save the Right! God save the Fair!
God help the soldiers over there!
But, O God, damn the wasters!

A CLASSICAL MASTER’S LETTER to CLEMENTS TONIC
Mr. G. A. Lundie, M.A., formerly Assistant-Headmaster High School, Palmerston North, N.Z, writes of this Medicine
And recommends it to all professional people, especi-
ally to teachers in the Educational service of Aus-
tralia, who suffer from weak nerves, insomnia or
brain fag.
“Palmerston North,
“New Zealand.
"CLEMENTS TONIC LTD.,
“I am an unsolicited witness to your remark-
able remedy.
“I suffered for years from a form of nervous
complaint, which manifested itself in profound
melancholia which I could not throw off.
Clements Tonic Ltd., Rozelle, N.S.W.
“My appetite was capricious to a degree, and
I developed ill-health, till life was a real burden.
“I was, furthermore, threatened with a com-
plete nervous breakdown. I could hardly fol-
low my duties as a high-school master, much
less give attention to serious teacher’s duties.
“I can honestly say your potent medicine
came to me as a revelation in healing, for I
regained my health completely through its con-
sistent use.
“For teachers engaged in mental strain and
arduous study, your tonic is indispensable as a
stand-by.
“I have recommended it to dozens of other
teachers, with most beneficial results.

“G. A. LUNDIE, M.A.”
There are hundreds of teachers of both sexes employed in
the N.S.W. Educational Service to-day who suffer from weak
nerves or acute nervousness, through continuous nerve strain in
teaching and study, who never really enjoy good health. It is
as much the worry of keeping their schools up to the educa-
tional standard as the work which accounts for their feeling of
general ill-health. They should keep Clements Tonic on hand
as a “stand-by” medicine, feeling certain that in its possession
they will have a sweet soother of the nerves, a promoter of
keen appetite, and a general healer of the rundown and over-
wrought system. It makes weak people well and strong people
stronger. Get it to-day.

A picturesque incident didn’t happen
amid the Rockhampton floods. The report
had it that an aged craft which had been
tied for years to the river bank broke loose
and fled headlong down the river. Its sole
human contents were an ancient sea-captain
aged 84, and his chances as he vanished into
the storm were voted hopeless: The vessel
didn’t become a complete wreck, after all.
and the aged mariner wasn’t on board. You
can never rely on these aged mariners. The
only thoroughly trustworthy people are
reporters.

“Machete” ; “Joyce’s” yarn of the Solomon
Island medicine-man (B. 10/1/T8) recalls
the performances of members of the pro-
fession practising in New Guinea. They are
a very close corporation in those parts, and
their privileges are jealously guarded against
encroachments by missionaries and Govern-
ment M.O.’s —it’s just the same as the
B.M.A..’s attitude to the non-elect on our
side. One of them once cured the first
Bishop of N.G. of an attack of neuralgia.
Maclaren had been condemning the prac-
titioner for roguery and general unworthi-
ness, when the latter offered to cure him of the
pain of which he had complained. The antics
of the old tough during the treatment so
interested Maclaren that when challenged he
had to admit that the pain was gone. Then,
to the vast admiration of some two or three
hundred niggers who were looking on, the

“Cherokee” goes down the Bay (nit!) :
The great, thing on these hot evenings in Melbourne
is to be one of as many sweltering thousands as can
pack on board the side-wheeler Hygeia, and embark
for a “pleasure” trip on that flat, wet expanse known
as Port Phillip Bay. In the absence of Mrs. Cherokee
I decided to take it on when the Vic. Railways
Institute ran its moonlight picnic, for which tickets
were sold on Port Mel-
bourne pier. 1 never
reached the steamer.
Large, thick men ran
amok and' went “over
the top” at the ticket
offices; women tore off
each other’s hats and
back hair; a plank fell
out of the pier and
dropped into the sea, and
so tight was the jamb
that mere lateral pres-
sure kept those above
the hole from following
the plank. People on
the steamer rushed to the
side next the pier to bar-
rack for the fighters, so
that the Vessel canted
until her inshore paddle-
box was level with the
string-piece. I prayed
that everyone would slide
off the deck, but the
passengers stuck like
flies to fly-paper, while
rampant women sprang
from pier to steamer
with small kids describ-
ing surprising parabolas
beside them. The police
stopped the sale of
tickets, and the fight
moved nearer the steamer.
At last she slid away, leaving a thousand surging
trippers behind. Melbourne has quaint ideas of
amusing itself.
“Tarago”: The new “depth bomb” that
has been designed to fall like a welcoming
bouquet on the shy U-boat makes a consider-
able stir. The other morning I w r atched
from near this English hospital a shore bat-
tery practising with these fireworks. The
course of the shell seemed similar to that of
the howitzer, and when it struck the water
it was almost perpendicular. Its entry was
followed in every case by a mild report like
that of a cracker; but there was no mistak-
ing the concussion, which sent a shiver
through 500 ft. of solid rock beneath me, and
heaved up the sea like a submarine volcano
in the neighborhood of the hit.
“Kei Viti” : Recently I went to a Fijian
meke given in aid of the Red Cross funds. A
number of highly-polished brown ladies sat
in a row with a backing of highly-polished
and hefty bucks, and sang interminable and
quite irrelevant songs in perfect time and
harmony, to the accompani-
ment of a lalli, the hollow
log beaten with sticks which
is their chief musical instru-
ment. They were gorgeously
attired in wreaths and
tappa, and they smelt to
high Heaven of coeoanut oil
and warm Fijian. At inter-
vals a magnificent native
came forward and made a
few remarks in his own
language, such as: “When
Kitchener went down in the
North Sea his last message
to the Fijians was: ‘Help
England, and down with
Germany.’ ” Then he se-
lected a plump damsel from
the chorus, made her stand
up. and introduced her thus:
“This is a Red Cross nurse.
She received a medal from
the King for killing 10
wounded Germans.” Tre-
mendous applause from the
audience, and bashful retire-
ment of the supposed minis-
tering angel. Another was
brought forward: “This is a
Red Cross nurse who was
specially decorated by the
King. She had to nurse a
very great German general.
This is what he looked like
when she had finished with
him,” and he hobbled across
the stage with the help of a
stick, a dreadfully deformed
and misshapen specimen of
humanity. The audience
roared, and I came away
wondering whether they’d
think the Red Cross was
worth helping if they knesv
what it really is and does.
On February 1 Hobart citizens missed the
sound of the 1 o’clock gun which has been
fired at the Domain batterv for about half
a century to let them know whether the
clock was 10 minutes slow or only five. It
costs £75 a year to fire the gun, and last
year the Assembly, in one of its spasms of
economy, cut the amount down to £37, which
has all been blown away.
"Oheckaquo” : Prendergast, M.L.A., whom
no one would suspect of such an interest in
Art, recently instigated Premier Bowser
(equally guiltless of such suspicion) to make
inquiries for a picture of early Melbourne,
drawn by one Jackson in 1841. The Vic.
Government bought it in 1882 for £4OO, and
in 1892 lent it to the promoters of the old
Cyclorama on Eastern Hill. Then it van-
ished from public ken. A ’tec. was put on
the job, and the work of art was found,
according to Bowser’s information, in a
heap of debris at the Exhibition, where the
State Parliament has its murmurous hive.
When the discovery got press publicity,
’Enery Weedon, chairman of the Exhibition
trustees, rose in his frock-coated wrath to
protest that the drawing wasn’t among
debris. It was perfectly secure, having been
locked in a safe for 26- years. (This is
just what you’d expect of Weedon and his
committee. Hang Art! It is safer under
lock and key!) The drawing, which is 15ft.
long by 2ft. wide, is in black-and-white,
one in the Cyclorama being a copy. The
artist drew it sitting in a large barrel on
the site of Scots’ Church, taking out a stave
at a time as he required a fresh section of
landscape.

WAY OF A MAID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was a soulful maid. Her mouth
Was sweeter than a honey bud.
The warmth and freshness of the south
Had put its color in her blood.
He was a man of thirty-two,
And tall and elegant was he,
With quiet ways, but bold to woo,
Just as Prince Charming ought to be.
She met him, and her air was cold :
She heard him, and seemed not to heed;
The little tale he might unfold
Was very wearisome indeed,
lie was not rude in any way,
Yet artfully she did contrive
Throughout a long, delightful day
To scarce admit he was alive.
He left her not a whit concerned,
And gave no thought to her at all;
She to her simple life returned,
And said she liked them slim and tall.
She told herself she loved his hair,
She wondered who would be his wife,
His e.ves remembered year by year,
And dreamed about him all her life.
VICTORIA.
E. D.
THE WISH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The moon was as thin as the shining edge
Of an ancient and very well-worn half-crown,
As 1 saw her rise through the prickly hedge
When the meek little evening came trembling
down.
’Twas a chill little evening that seemed too young
After all the rain that the clouds hail flung.
Hut the clouds had gone and the sky was blue ;
And right on the top of a ruffianly tree
A little white star, like a fairy’s shoe,
Seemed merrily dancing a jig to me ;
While the moon, like a shaving of very white pine,
Couldn’t And enough darkness to really shine.
My hand in my pocket forlorn and deep,
I turned all the cash that I happened to find—
One threepenny-bit that had gone to sleep
With a penny the world had left behind —-
And the little star laughed for the thing lie knew,
And wished me the luck of my dream come true.
And it came like a shot. Oh, young romance
That flutters when evenings have moons to wear.
And the small stars wink with their toes that dance
On fifty millions of leagues of air!
For the bell rang out with its call to me —
And we didn’t have hash that night for tea !
K.S.W,

A BITE.
ENTHUSIASTIC SURFER : “I say, old girl, I'm
learning to ‘shoot’ beautifully.”
DITTO RECRUITER: “Bonzer! Why, I didn’t
dream you’d even think of enlisting.”
Peter Simonoff, who was lately appointed
Russian Consul-General for Australia, is 30
and a Bolshevik. Arriving in Australia six
years ago he toiled a while in Queensland,
then went round to Port Tine, and being
an "internationalist” naturally drifted on to
Broken Hill (N.S.W.), where he got a job
as a trucker. He used to be a clerk. Last
year he returned to Queensland as editor
of the Russian paper, The Worker’s Life,
which was lately very unwisely suppressed
by the Commonwealth Government. One
thing he reckons sure : “Russia is not going
to make a shameful Kaiser’s peace.” What,
lie seems to overlook is that Peter and Paul
can’t jointly resist the Kaiser by hitting one
another on the nose.
Samuel Taylor, who passed out lately at
96, was the oldest Anglican priest in Vic-
toria. He came to Australia from Dublin
nearly 70 years ago, and from 1853 till 1889
was stationed at Brighton, where he had
among his curates Dr. Green, who afterwards
bished at Armidale (N.S.W.) and Ballarat,
and Dr. Stretch, who still wears Anglican
gaiters at Newcastle.

The Patagonian Consul’s wife at Stewart
Island (M.L.) writes: —-
Count von Luekner, the German sea-raider, now
doing penance for an indefinite period on Ripa
Island, Lyttelton Harbor, is perfectly delightful. I
travelled with him on the ferry-boat from Welling-
ton. He was beautifully uniformed, in a sweetly-
fitting tunic with a perceptible waist, and his man-
ners w r ere so distinguished, yet with a, soupcon of
sadness. He didn’t seem a bit to resent his capture.
Ilis aristocratic features wore only a gentle melan-
choly. Stories from other quarters credit von
Luekner with enormous wealth. It seems to be
true, at any rate, that he fitted out the See-Adler
at his own expense, and that he was given a free
hand by Berlin’s navy heads, a roving commission
after the manner of the old-time privateers. Neither
M.L. nor Australia is new to the captive pirate. He
was in these parts a few 7 years before the war.
It isn’t all buttered toast to be an arch-
bishop. A while ago Archdeacon D’arcy
Irvine (Sydney) was made Vicar-General.
Then Archdeacon Boyce resigned,, but allowed
it to stand over till the beginning of this
year so that the Archgaiters might think up
something. The Archgaiters got out his pipe
(Australia has corrupted him—he smokes a
pipe now), decided to have an Archdeacon
of Sydney, and Boyce got the job—as com-
pensation, no doubt, for the gout that the
strenuous old water-waggoner is a martyr
to. And all is peace again.
The Anglican Church at Mackay (N.Q.),
destroyed in the cyclone lately, was built in
the early ’eighties by Albert Maclaren, who
afterwards founded the Anglican Mission in
Papua. His curate was C. G. Barlow, who
later on wore gaiters at Townsville and
Goulburn (N.S.W.) ; and Melba sang in his
choir for a while. Dr. Anderson, who now
bishes at Ilay (N.S.W.), looked after his
first collection-plate at Mackay.
“Reggie” Black, M.L.C., the Pioneers’
Club spruiker on Anniversary Day in Syd-
ney, is the grandson of a man who left Eng-
land in 1797 as purser on a vessel of which
he was part-owner. When some months out
the crew mutinied and killed almost all the
officers. Black was spared on account of his
knowledge of seamanship, and was put with
some others in an open boat. They landed
on the shores of Brazil and then went to
Rio, where they were treated with the
greatest kindness. The party was soon after
picked up by a warship, and when some
Spanish prizes were captured. Black was put
in charge of one, which he sailed to the Cape
of Good Hope, where the cargo was sold, the
captain’s share being £2OOO. With that in
his pocket he arrived in Sydney Cove in
1795. He was the father of John Black,
who became manager of the Bank of
Wales; and thus son Reggie, now 73,
turned to finance instead of the sea. The
grandfather, by the way, was lost sight of
on a voyage to China.
Dead at 77, Colonel Hume, whose uame
had been on the Maoriland pay-sheet and
pension-list for nearly 30 years. After serv-
ing with a Highland Regiment in India, Hume
went back to England to become an officer
in the Prisons Department, and was deputy-
governor of the great penal establishments
at Millbank and Dartmoor. Maoriland,
wanting an Inspector of Prisons in ’SO,
naturally imported one and Ilume got the
job, subsequently becoming Commissioner of
Police. He was a typical product of the
school he had been reared in, caring abo t
as much for modern methods of prison ad-
ministration as he did for poetry—a. short,
stockily-built man. with a harsh voice, an
enraged moustache and a kindly feeling
towards everyone who didn’t run foul
of red-tape and regulation No. 1691. Most
ot Hume’s official life was spent in telling
someone to go to blazes. Most of the rest
was spent going to the races and trying to
pick ’em. lie was a confirmed punter.
After the Big Push in Palestine
Tom Stewart, a well-known Queensland and N. S.
Wales shearer, was shot through the arm; it was
a ghastly wound, yet he refused medical assistance
until his more dangerously-mangled mates were
looked to. Eventually he was placed on a camel,
but halfway to the dressing-station shrapnel killed
the camel and blew off the wounded arm. Tom
managed, for all that, to reach the Australian hospi-
tal in Cairo; but there septic helped one of the
bravest over the river. He had an old mate of
the shearing-sheds with him at the end —Trooper
Wharton. There’s none of us not proud to call him
cobber.
Captain Frank Worsley, R.N.R., who
skippered Shackleton’s lost ship in the
Antarctic and navigated the explorers’ open
boat on the great 760-mile voyage from
Elephant Jsland, has won the D.S.O. for
a smart bit of work with a submarine. A
Maoriland boy, he was raised at Akaroa and
went to sea in the sailing clippers of the
’eighties, .first learning to send down a royal-
yard on the old ship Wairoa. Later he was
an officer in the Government steamers Hine-
moa and Tutanekai, and as second mate was
the navigating expert. Then he was given
the Government schooner Countess of Ran-
furly to run in the South Sea trade, and the
way he had of sending his three-master full
tilt at the reefs of Aitutaki or Niue or Su-
warrow and bringing her up within a neck
of the hurdle, sails, ropes and blocks making
a hurricane of a row and scaring seven bells
out of his passengers and the folk ashore,
brought him fame for daredevilry all over
the Eastern Pacific. He is a number-one
chanteyman, and if you hear a South Sea
brown sailor with a tattooed face and rings
in his nose bellowing “Blow the Man Down”
or “Sally Brown’s a Fine Mulatto,” the odds
are that he was in one of Frank Worsley’s
schooner crews.
THE TIME FOR SELF-DENIAL.
OLD PHATT ( reprovingly): “No, no,
Thomas. ’ARE a chicken I ordered, an’
no finger-hotels! We must think uv
the poor fellers in the trenches!”
Lots of ancient mariners are off back to
the salt sea these days. Shortage of quali-
fied sailormen for oversea voyages is one
reason, and the resurrection of all sorts of
old sailing craft is the other. An Auckland
(M.L.) veteran who went off blithesomely
to the South Seas the other day, though
somewhere in his seventies, is Captain W.
Ruthe, one-time a skipper in the Shaw-
Savill windbag fleet. He commanded the
barque Helen Denny 30-odd years ago. Ruthe
was a “square-rigged” sailor in his clipper
days; nathless he is wise to the ways of fore-
and-afters. Once upon a time the Old Man
owned Rotoroa Island, in the Hauraki Gulf,
and ran a fishing business. Nowadays the
isle is one of M.L.’s homes for dipsomaniacs:
the Blood and Fire army owns it and diets
the chronic beer-cliewer on fresh snapper and
ditto air until he is O.K.’d and released —
whereupon he mostly makes furiously for the
nearest pub.
The biggest testimonial that the unpopu-
lar Asquith is likely ever to get is con-
tained in the new biography of Charles
Dilke, who knew everybody worth knowing
in his generation. He preferred Winston
Churchill to Randolph, and gave Asquith
the advantage over Disraeli and Gladstone.
But he admitted that no modern conversa-
tion came up to that he heard in Boston
about IS7O, from Longfellow, Agassiz.
Dowell and Oliver
Wendell Holmes.
On the verdict of
Stepniak that
Holmes, Mazzini
and Bismarck 'were
the three finest
talkers of modern
times, he said that
Mazzini was in-
clined to be “a
little of the bore,”
and Disraeli
“sometimes singu-
larly silent.” For
himself he had a
fancy for two Rus-
sians as conversa-
tionalists —Tourge-
nief and Herzen.
MULGA
TAYLOR,
New Speaker of
W.A. Assembly.
Died last week C. A. Chesney, old N. S.
Wales mining man, who made his biggest
punch out of the Chesney at Cobar. He
had a good scientific training, but was
always too overcrowded with schemes to
make a fortune out of any of them. Sam
McCaughey was a cousin, and Chesney spent
a lot of time on Yanco laying out irrigation
channels and so on.
How Admiral Creswell’s son died (the
story comes from old-contributor “Bowyang,”
of the Camel Corps, “on the way to Jeru-
salem”) : —•
We have suffered to-day. Captain R. W. Cres-
well, who was feeling his way forward, ordered his
men to lie low whilst he took a peep over a hill—
it was in the fighting around Beersheba. A Boche
machine-gunner was watching for a felt hat to rise
over that part of Palestine, and Creswell never knew
what hit him. Back with the men Lieut. Dawson,
of the same company, saw Creswell fall. He raced
up to see what had happened. Again the Boche
machine-gunner pulled the lever, and Dawson fell
across Creswell’s body. Four men rushed to his
assistance, not heeding Dawson’s last words: “Look
out, hoys; we’re covered with a machine-gun.” The
four men fell around their officers. Captain Creswell
was a good sport and a good Australian; to his
men a mate, only differing from them in this —that
he thought it was his business to take all the chances.
“Bill Bowyang” : If Australia wants immi-
grants, there’s a few with us
here in Palestine we could do
with. One Tommy, wounded
in three places, staggered into a
dressing-station at midnight,
dragging an unconscious xAus-
tralian. He wasn’t talking,
but they pieced the story to-
gether. He had come across
“the dinkum” two miles back,
and, finding that there was still
a flutter under his tunic, had
dragged him in, the journey
taking five hours! Another,
with both legs fractured, was
surrounded by wounded cobbers.
He managed to crawl from one
to the other of them and ban-
daged them. He was still look-
ing for somebody that he reck-
oned needed help when he sank
on his arm unconscious from
loss of blood; and he never stirred again.
Parkes’s old mountain residence at
Faulconbridge has just been sold by the
mortgagees, the Allen family. The old Par-
liamentary hand took up 640 acres at £1
per acre, with a residence condition. The
Allen family advanced £9344 on it, and of
course had to foreclose.
Albert Edward Budd, executed at Mel-
bourne Gaol the other day, was the 173rd
person to be given to the gallows in Vic-
toria. Four of the sacrifices were women.
TWO SCOUTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spent is the bitter storm of lead,
And sorrow upon my heart is lying,
For smiling Arthur Malone is dead,
And Harry Devine is dying.
All night long we surged, and crept,
And crawled, and streamed over hill and hollow ;
Ml night long the guide-lights leapt,
And the ghostly squadrons strove to follow.
Often the awesome hush was rent
By a rudely-startled Mauser’s rattle,
And shrapnel rained from the firmament
In the gloom before the battle.
Grey dawn broken on the Ziklag post,
A dense, white fog came hovering over ;
Broadsides chorused along the coast,
Driving the foe to earth and cover.
A filmy veil of mist was drawn
On the ridge where two scouts paused and waited ;
The east was blurred in the weeping dawn,
And never the fog abated.
Two scouts mounted and rode abreast
Towards the east where the Turks were lying.
Squadrons streamed from the hoof-stirred west
When a fireless sun through the mist was prying.
Many a sniper loosed his bolt
In the autumn morning grey and chilly—-
Malone rode forth on the sorrel colt
With Devine astride the filly.
A mile behind we followed fast,
And the red sun burst the clouds asunder ;
The whole line paused and stood aghast
When the Turkish guns began to thunder.
The fog had cloaked the danger zone,
And proved a boon to the troops advancing
On the ridge where the Turks were lying prone,
The sun on their bayonets dancing.
Two scouts halted a mile ahead,
And turned their backs on a hedge of wire
That fenced a low ridge whipped with lead
And loud with the roll of rifle fire.
Sunlight glistened the dew-damp rock,
And lieliographed with tell-tale flashes
On buckle and spur and rifle stock
A rhythm of dots and dashes.
Eight hoofs rang on the stony flat,
And two scouts galloped for home together;
Shrapnel riddled Malone’s felt hat,
And severed Devine’s off-stirrup leather.
Knee to knee in a hail of lead
They raced, low over the withers lying. . . .
But smiling Arthur Malone is dead,
And Harry Devine is dying.

Said wise old George Judah Cohen, the
chairman, addressing the shareholders:—
When we last met I called attention to the neces-
sity for economy to enable the country to bear the
enormous burden of debt caused by the war. I can
on this occasion only reiterate what I then said.
I regret that so far there is no evidence of any
serious attempt at economy by the people. Extrava-
gance is only too apparent everywhere and in every
form, and unfortunately seems to be on the increase.
It is easy come easy go. The Defence
Department is pouring out money. When
war starts all ordinary rules are thrown
aside —or it has been so everywhere hitherto ;
whether it is going to be so in the U.S.A.
this time is doubtful. Expense doesn’t mat-
ter. The military person—on the whole the
most incompetent person in the world —
takes command. He has usually spent his
life filling in time. At the art of bluffing
lie has become a pastmaster, from the general
down to the private. Now he proceeds to
justify himself. He magnifies his job and
his importance and his sole capacity for
dealing with this matter by pushing the
“civilian” aside. Expense—how dare any-
one talk about expense! So shins, which
cost tens of thousands to fit up, are gutted.
Take the fittings out in sections, so that
they may be presently replaced? Rubbish !
In goes the axe. Of course in such circum-
stances the thieves soon congregate. Turn
on the light of public criticism? Who dares?
Criticise the military? Reflect upon the
administration of the War Office or the
Defence Department, as the case may be?
Actually these people make this an offence!
In England, to a great extent, they have
stonned this nonsense. The War Office (and
this is more largely due to Asquith than to
Lloyd George) has to put up with criticism.
But in Australia the old bluff still prevails
—simply because there is more reason why
it should prevail: more reason, that is. in
the incompetence of the administration.
Three months ago a commission of commer-
cial men reported on the business side of
the Department. That report has not been
published yet. And that was not the first
committee of inouiry. Another sat for
months: it included two men of high ouali-
fieations as accountants and business sys-
tomatisers. Its report was never published.
With such a department, thus spending,
let us reflect, now £100,000.000 a year—say
the average value of five Australian wheat
crops, or the total Australian production of
gold for 10 years past—with a Prime Min-
ister whose idea of settling a miners’ strike
is to instruct an “Arbitration” judge to put
up the oriee of coal 3s. per ton. and with
State Governments whose way of getting
votes is to buy them with loan and other
money, is it any wonder there is extrava-
gance? But. as the same wise old financier
added in Iris address to the shareholders in
this bank :
That strict economy will sooner or later be forced
on the community is clear to everyone versed in
financial matters, and the sooner the public can be
made to realise this the better it will be for the
country.
Meantime N. S. Wales remains the most
shocking example of extravagance. With
money to burn, the public is bled by every-
body with anything to sell. According to
the invaluable Ivnibbs. what could be bought
for £1 in lflll cost, as an average, 28s. Id.
in 1917; in Melbourne it cost 255. lid., in
Adelaide and Perth 245. lid. and in Bris-
bane 235. 7d. In the ordinary course of
business the Commercial of Sydney takes its
bite out of the abnormal profits made in
X. S. Wales, where most of its business
lies; hence, though it is pretty certain that
the management made provision for all sorts
ot’ contingencies, the disclosed profit for last
half-year was a third greater than it was
for the corresponding half of DIO. The
shareholders, however, get only the old 10
p.c., though £lO is worth no more now to a
bank shareholder than to anybody else.
As usual, the Commercial maintains a
very strong position so far as liquid assets
are concerned. This table shows that and
a few other features: —

Liquid assets are thus nearly 12s. in the £ of
liabilities—an exceptional holding. Of course
a lot of it is earning good money—rather
more than half of the total: there is, roughly,
five millions of it in public securities and
over a million at short call in London, most
of it no doubt awaiting transfer on account
of sellers of Australian products.
It is not much use talking about the
future. Nobody knows what it will be.
Nobody knew, apparently, what the condi-
tions were going to be during the war. In-
stead of hard times, such as the people have
felt during every other great war, there have
been bulging purses. The change has been
due to the development of credit. Credit
means, in plainer language, going into debt;
and unless you happen to be a Bolshevik,
going into unproductive debt to-day means a
struggle to get out of it to-morrow. This in
the ordinary course of things would, mean low
property values ; and that is liable to mean
heavy loss of principal by banks that have
been imprudent in making advances.
Whether they can hide those losses depends
then upon the inner reserves they have put
by. But as to current profits the banks in
Australia will perhaps not suffer seriously.
For one thing the earnings of their own capi-
tal promise to be large. This bank, for in-
stance, has visible reserves which have just
exceeded its share capital of £2.000.000. In
other words it has over £4.000,000 of its own ;
and interest is not going to be low for many
years—perhaps the general run of accommo-
dation charges will never be as low again :
taking all things into account in Australia
they have been astonishingly low; and so,
of course, has the interest allowed on
deposits. •

Another pound or two better than the pre-
vious best. The N. S. Wales State borrow-
ing boom and the consequent inflated prices
have been right into the hands of this bank.
Eight years ago it had £980,781 of old assets
in the shape of station properties, etc. They
were mostly sediment, and, though severely
written down, many of them were non-pro-
ductive or thereabouts. The chairman told
the shareholders the other day that of the
whole £980,781, only £00,895 was left on the
books —“and we are satisfied that the ulti-
mate result will be perfectly satisfactory to
the shareholders. Where there has been any
loss on the book value the same has been pro-
vided for.” In the early days of this,
clearing-out there was no contingency
fund to make losses good, and any such
losses had to be charged against profits on
current business, which probably accounts
for the poor little net profit of 12. and 14
thousands disclosed in some of the earlier
lines of the table. But later on, apart from
any profits made on sales themselves while
high prices were ruling from 1912 onwards,
the bank acquired a contingency fund pro-
vided by the repurchase of 4 p.c. inscribed
deposits. Under the bank’s articles £15,000
is to be employed each half-year in buying
that paper, and, as it is done in the open
market, this means a profit of about £lO.OOO
a year—money which could most properly
be applied towards making good any losses
on the sale of old assets. This source of
profit won t dry up for a good many years—
will keep running so long, in fact, as money
is worth more than 4 p.c. and there are any
inscribed deposits to buy. Already there
ought to be a handy inner reserve; and there
is still £1,514,705 to be repurchased.
Seven years ago current and fixed deposits
totalled £1,975.313, and capital and inscribed
deposits were £2,878,000. Figures like that
made a strong appeal to people with money
for deposit. Some consideration had, of
course, to be given to the quality of the
assets. And that was all right even seven
years ago; and with the conversion of the
old sediment into cash or new live advances,
the position has gradually become still
better. It is not very surprising, therefore,
that in seven years the deposits have almost
doubled: they stood on December 31 at
£3,751,522. The difficulty has been to get
new sound advance business to keep pace
with this. For it was not only a matter of
getting employment for the new deposits: as
previously shown, nearly a million of old
station and other assets have been sold and
tlje purchase money has become available,
too. At December 31 the bank had liquid
assets equal to slightly oyer 50 p.c. of its
liabilities apart from inscribed deposits.
Moreover, it owed (deposits, etc.) only £3 ss.
for each £1 of share capital and reserves,
reckoning the inscribed deposits neither as
capital nor as debt. In the hunt for new
advance business the plan of getting it in a
wholesale line by buying up a smaller bank
at a valuation was of course discussed ; and
during the past half-year arrangements were
made for the purchase of the City Bank of
Sydney. This will satisfy the appetite of
the A.B.C. for a while; but there is no
reason why it shouldn’t take another couple
of millions of deposits and make another
million or more of advances.

will be, if the A.B.C. holds all the City’s

eight times is not regarded as out-of-the-way
in Australia and 12 times is reckoned a fair
thing in England ; and liquid assets will be
•approximately £2,607,000, or a shade over
41 p.c. of the liabilities, and that is a reason-
able figure even for these uncertain days.

The 13 p.c. dividend is paid free of income
tax, and the chairman confessed that it
“really amounts to £l7 6s. Bd. p.c. gross.”
Prior to the war the dividend was 8 p.c., also
free of tax ; so that the war, out of which
nobody ought to be making a penny, has its
compensations so far as the shareholders of
this very fortunate co. are concerned. And
if it were not an English eo. it might easily
'have paid another 40- p ; c. The British, Gov-
ernment has, however, been less content
than the Australian Government to pass the
war bill on to the future; during the past
year it called on this cq. for no less than
£175,710. The following little table is a
bitter reflection on Australian war finance;
it shows the amounts paid by this co. in
taxes: — • * ■

More shocking still, of the £62,767 paid in
Australasia last year £9455 was the total of
Federal taxes. The State Governments have
no war bill to pay; nevertheless they have
taken advantage of the war to put their
taxes up to £41,537. The British Govern-
ment collected out of the abnormal profits
which this co. has made upwards of £150,000
towards its war bill. Australia, where the
bulk of the profits were made, collected
under £lO,OOO for the same purpose.
Meantime, to make its neglect worse in
this case, the Commonwealth Government
put business into Dalgety’s hands—gave it,
in fact, a monopoly in some lines—from which
the co. must have derived many tens of
thousands of pounds in commission. In the
year preceding the war the gross earnings,
before paying salaries and current exes.,
were £570,510. In the past year they were
£861,793. The bulk of this increase has been
made out of war prices. The wool clip, for
instance, was sold at the average price per
ib. of the 1918-14 clip with 55 p.c. added :
and on metals and some other products the
advance has, on the whole, been a good deal
more than 55 p.c. The British Government
gave us a lead. The Commonwealth Gov-
ernment followed the abominable old Aus-
tralian practice of putting the bill on the
file, so that those who have made no profit
out of the war, but probably have suffered
heavily by it, will presently have to pay
almost as much as those who have made
fortunes.
The co., like most others in the pastoral
finance business, had a large amount of pro-
perty thrown on its hand in the 'nineties;
and for some years thereafter its principal
asset was “advances on land, stations, stock,
wool and other produce, including property
held by the co.” Proporlios have, however,
been realised; and if the co. holds any at all
in these days, apart from freehold and lease-
hold premises, there is no specific mention of
it in the accounts. Nowadays the co., backed
by its good name, has developed the finest
commission and agency business in Australia,
assisted thereto by its very ample resources
of secured capital. This next table shows
how various items have moved during the
past few years :

by another £100,000; and these and sundry
plant and furniture accounts will now stand
at £388,848, which is considerably,.less than
half their cost and a great deal less than
their market value.

Not quite so glittering as the previous year ;
still a very fine performance, particularly in
view of the directors’ decision not to bump
up the selling prices of most of their staple
lines. “In fact,” they say, “papers, plates,
spools and cameras were sold last year at
a very much lower rate than in England and
America.” That is probably good business
policy; Australia offers an unlimited scope
for camera work, and the future of this co.
depends on the extent to which it can popu-
larise the hobby. And bumping up prices
wouldn’t help. The co. has had a remark-
ably successful career. Formed in 1908 to
take over and extend the business of Baker
and Rouse, it started with a clean balance-
sheet, nothing being paid for goodwill or
patents and processes, while establishment
exes, were only £478. For the “actual tan-
gible assets” of the old business £74,900 was
paid in £1 shares, and £75,100 of new capital
was put up.

In 1909, plant, etc., was valued at £28,120;
and though at least £25,000 has been
spent on new plant, as written down the
item now figures in the balance-sheet at onlv
£21,724. The increase of stocks, valued “a't
cost or under,” has reached the speed limit:

that increase is due, no doubt, to war prices;
and as there is almost certain to be a slump
after Sammy has finished the war, there is
liable to be a considerable amount of writ-
ing down to be done on the stock-sheets of
most Australian importers. All the ships
of the world, says Prof. Lefroy, the British
Government’s wheat expert, will race to the
countries that have food when the war ends;
and that is pretty certain to mean heavy
imports to Australia. Hence heavy stocks

have always to be viewed with suspicion.
With least suspicion, of course, in a trade
whose lines are handled by few people ; tor
there throat-cutting can generally be avoided.
This is one of those trades.
There are few trading cos. which can show
such fine, quick results. Since 1910, in
addition to a bonus of £30,000 in shares,
shareholders on an capital _ot
£150.000 have received £184,500 in divi-
dends. And for every £1 of share capi-
tal there are assets worth over £1 14s.
Some of those assets (like the stock)
may be liable to get into trouble._ But there
are others, like the co.’s remaining interest
in the Eastman-Kodak Co., which are no
doubt worth a good deal more than book
value. Nice little profits have been made
by the sale of Eastman-Kodak shares in past
years: but the holding is now down to
£12,430 —rather less than half of what it
was originally.

The liquidator has declared a first and
final dividend of 3d. per share on the shares
of Hargraves Consolidated (N.S.W.). This
old show- was resurrected five years ago by
H. E. Pratten. He spent £13,000 trying the
known saddle reef and chasing new ones.
The 10,000 tons crushed yielded only IOOOoz.
gold, so it’s not surprising that Pratten
transferred his affections to Malayan tin
shows.
* * * *
C.S.A., Cobar (N.S.W.), has now its two
furnaces in commission, the first treating ore
and metal-bearing fluxes, and the second
raising the grade of the matte produced in
No. 1 furnace to 45 or 50 p.e. Last week 728
tons were smelted for 25 tons copper, an
average extraction of 3.4 p.c. ;* also 90 tons
first matte were retreated for 52 tons of
shipping matte containing 22 tons copper.
Assuming that C.S.A. can maintain an out-
put of 80 to 100 tons per month, its revenue
with copper at present price will be much in
excess of its issued capital (£78,000), a con-
dition very favorable for dividends.
* * * *
The Yetliolme Development and Mining
Co. has been registered to take over the
Molybdenite Options Syndicate’s ground (42
acres) near Bathurst (N.S.W.). This will
put three cos. on the field—the Mammoth,
Aiount Tennyson and Yetholme —and that
very nearly covers the ground. The Yetholme
has done very little work, but the ore is
believed to be worth as much as the Mam-
moth’s alongside; and “trial parcels from
each treated by the same process assayed re-
spectively 1.7 n.c. and 1.8 p.c. molybdenum
disulphide.” The Mammoth’s plant is just
about ready; but it ought to be made quite
clear that it is going to be put on picked
stuff at the outset, and what the whole mass
is worth —or whether it is worth anything at
all —has yet to be demonstrated. The man
who feels like a plunge on the strength of
the early Mammoth returns ought to remem-
ber that it is only the favorable opinions on
Yetholme that have been published.
Bob Turner’s Bodangora (N.S.W.) is
moulting badly. In Dick’s mine six months’
work gave only 2750 tons of ore, a good deal
of which was stope remnants and pillars.
Mitchell’s shaft workings furnished 362 tons,
and the Horseshoe shaft 84 tons. As cash
returns amounted to only £6852, the co.
drifted to the tune of £SIS. Ore reserves
are 2670 tons at Dick’s shaft, 650 tons at
the Horseshoe and 1530 tons at Mitchell’s,
averaging about 355. per ton, so that unless
prospecting at the Horseshoe and Mitchell’s
looks up it won’t be long before the co. will
have to think about a final clean up.
Assets about equal liabilities, but a peanut
may be got out of the stopes if development
is abandoned.
