Just wdien C. B. Longbottom handed in
his checks, his pet mine, Cope’s Creek Cen-
tral (N.S.W.), turns the corner and pays
the mostly-Melbourne shareholders a div.
Want of labor kept No. 1 plant down to
two shifts last half-year, so that only 18 tons
tin oxide were got from 86.254 cub. yds. The
ground is very tough and cemented, so costs
mopped up 11.821 d. per yd. This is lid.
per yd. less than in the June term, but it is
much too costly to give the profits that the
ground should have yielded had sluicing gone
without a break. The barge is now in No.
37 paddock, where better conditions and
higher-grade ground give promise of a very
profitable average. Nos. 2 and 3 plants have
had easier jobs on Skeleton Creek. There
they handled 136,400 cub. yds. and 113,287
cub. yds. respectively, and garnered 681 tons
tin oxide at a cost of from B|d. to 91d. The
result of all this is a net profit of £2496 for
the half-year. The co. ended 1917 with
liquid assets of £7365 to set against £2719
of debts, so it can afford the shilling div.
In April, 1916, the sinking of the shaft at
the State-subsidised Iguana Consols, Croydon
(Q.), was commenced with the intention of
reaching the main body at 1200 ft. By the
end of 1916 the shaft was down 67ft., and
the whole of last year’s work added only
another 113 ft. Croydon is depending on
this shaft to give it another lease of life,
but if sinking operations do not proceed at
a greater pace than last year’s record of 2ft.
per week the chances of Croydon ever seeing
the bottom of the shaft look black. Working
one shift, with four men per shift, part of
the time with hand steel until the ground got
too hard and then with Jack Hammers,
doesn’t make much impression when the hole
has to be cut 15ft. by sft. in hard granite,
with water increasing at every foot sunk.
Tenders have been let to sink 600 ft. by con-
tract, but this when finished will only see
780 ft., with still 500 ft. to go. Meantime
surface expenses to the tune of £lOO per
month go on all the while. By the way.
isn’t that 2ft. per week about a record in
shaft sinking?
* * * *
The outlook for Croydon (Q.) is very
gloomy. Since August last, when all opera-
tions below the No. 1 south level at the
Content Block ceased, work on the field’s
last productive mine has been mainly con-
fined to driving this level and stuping above
it towards the grass roots. While patches
of good stone worth up to 4oz. over a width
of 15in. have been obtained, its days are
apparently numbered, for on following the
stone up the main break nothing worth work-
ing was disclosed. As the gold won here is
worth under 30s. per oz., there is not much
left after expenses are met.

At Gympie (Q.) last year’s dividends
totalled £20,808, and calls took £21,433. The
field crushed 91,054 tons for 40.8370 z.. but
of this Scottish Gympie alone put through
86.400 tens for 27,9810 z.. averaging 6dwt.
llgr. No. 2 N. Columbia crushed 1541 tons
for 50580 z. and paid £13,000 in dividends,
while No. 1 N. Columbia next door crushed
349 tons for 1009 oz. and distributed £3459.
No. 3 N. Columbia, which did so well three
years ago, is now on the call list. An old
Sydney favorite, the S. Glantnire and Monk-
land, is distributing 2d. per share from
proceeds of the sale of plant.

Buttrey’s Reward, Bendigo, is to tackle
outside ground on the Brunswick line, six
miles south of Pall Mall. The co. has 650
yds. on the line of reef, which shows its
modesty when so much vacant land is avail-
able round about. Fred Tricks is the plucky
one to take it on with a £10,500 co. His
attraction is a 4ft. reef on an east back and
a parallel western formation, out of which
old-timers are said to have scooped 2oz. and
3oz. stone. Two shafts are down, so every-
thing is O.K. for the jig—a good record,
stone showing and a market that wants a
tonic. m
Bendigo Amalgamated’s yield for the past
fortnight has dropped to 1158 oz. from 5076
tons, so all the newspaper talk about better
prospects didn’t stand the battery test. An
extraordinary meeting this month is to give
directors power to go to the bank to get a
blister on the assets. The need for accom-
modation is because of low yields, recon-
struction of plant, purchase of machine
shop, and the starting of a stores branch.
But why go to the bank, if the promoters
guaranteed £100,060 on the contributing
shares?
* * * *
Dalmayne Collieries (Tas.) directors have
been in a nice stew over their mine. Owing
to a misunderstanding the rents were not
paid ; and the co.’s leases were bunched with
a lot of others and gazetted as forfeited.
Of course there was a scamper then: not
only the co. but two others paid the pre-
scribed rent, and in the ordinary course the
other two would have as good a chance as
the co. The directors were, however, soon
on the doormat of Mines Minister Lewis,
and barrister Alban C. Morley was able to
persuade his also-learned friend that (1) it
was all an accident, (2) the co., having
spent £20,000 on the show, had given ample
evidence of its bona fidcs, and (3) nobody
in sight had as good a moral claim to the
property or was likely to do as well for
Tasmania. So Morley left with the assur-
ance that at the next Executive meeting the
leases would be submitted with a recom-
mendation that they be regranted in pursu-
ance of powers in the Act which cover acci-
dental occurrences of the kind. The co.’s
jetty is being extended 150 ft. to a total of
550 ft., and the directors are considering the
desirability of a further extension of 150 ft.
The ropeway was tested 10 days ago, and is
in good working order. The loading scheme
on the jetty was also tested, and shows a
capacity of 100 tons per hour, and save for
minor adjustments it is complete.
• * * #
High prices and a good supply of water
for sluicine’ helped Arba (Tas.) to make a
profit of £9598 last half-year. At the main
workings most of the term was occupied in
stripping overburden, only 26.595 cub. yards
being sluiced in five weeks for a return of
32 tons of tin oxide. The average yield was
a very satisfactory 2.691 b. per yard, and costs
averaged 10.64 d. At the Easement property
71,885 cub. yards of wash and overburden
returned I.Blb. oxide against a cost of 8.9 d.
per yard. The total yield from both sections
was 89 tons 14cwt. black tin, for a profit
averaging over £lOO per ton. Two six-
pennies absorbed £3150, and £4772 was
written off for depreciation. On December
31, tin, stores and cash totalled £9944 and
liabilities only £403.

Fenian Syndicate, Meekatharra (W.A.),
held its half-yearly meeting the other day,
and sharenolders were told that there was
two years’ supply of ore in sight. To date
250,000 tons have been crushed for £BOO,OOO,
»>r thereabouts; and nearly one-half of this
has been paid in dividends. Several attempts
have been made to buy the mine for dota-
tion ; but nothing has come of them. The
show is owned by a local syndicate, in which
Dung figures prominently. Apart from the
Fenian and its equally productive neighbor,
the iugliston Consols, and one or two
smaller shows, such as the Gwalia and
Ingliston, life has now almost passed out of
the once flourishing Meekatharra field.
“Oof Bird” :Edna May Central’s reconstruc-
tion is another instance of what happens after
directors have distributed with a loud whoop
what should have been held in reserve. I
don’t know if that was their object, but 1
do know the result of the dividends was to
prop ui) the market to the advantage of in-
side operators. When I saw the mine a
couple of years or so back it was being
worked in the typical go-as-you-please way :
no proper sampling or plans showing ore
reserves and values —only estimates, which,
as usual, were not borne out by battery
returns. An mgs are different now, and
shareholders cannot blame those at present
in charge of the mine for the collapse which
has taken place in the co.’s finances. For
a Board of a weakly-financed mining ven-
ture to pay dividends before its permanency
has been established is reprehensible; but as
shareholders do not seem to mind, and as
those who fell in are silent as the grave,
perhaps it is not worth butting in. Still,
the public should remember to stop, look and
listen when early dividends are paid by a
mining co. whose shares are much used as
gambling counters.
■»•***
The hopelessness of making money out of
Payne’s Find ( W.A.) has at last p been ad-
mitted. For some time the show was
kept alive by Perth and Adelaide call-payers,
and then a number took The Bulletin’s
advice and dropped out. Later on, when
the co. had had enough, one of the biggest
shareholders, C. M. Yeomans, bogged in. and
now, after years of battling with short
shoots and hard country, he has given in
and shareholders are told the mine is no
good. It never was for a co., anu never
should have been acquired by one.
« * * #
Leonora Blocks (W.A.) has again
changed managers, this time .Tim Cole, a
large shareholder, taking control. As this
mine appeared from frequently-published
assays to be opening up well, some hard-hit
people are wondering why results have
turned out so badly. The fact is the show
once had a fairly good shoot of ore which
yielded profits to a party of working miners,
but so far nothing has been found big and
good enough for a co.
That pallid Empress, of Mt. Magnet
(W.A.), is still calling up cash to hunt for
something south and north at 400 ft. W.
Rickard got 430 tons out of the mine last
half-year for 1400 z., and since then has
raked up another 350 tons, which averaged
lOdwt. He is going strong, but if he doesn’t
ferret out a rosier prospect south under the
main shoot the directors should find a better
outlet than the Empress for his and their
energies.
* * * *
Theodore Hoover and his pals A. F.
Ivuehn and W. F. Turner have dropped off
the Ynanmi Board, but even with them
absent it takes £2264 to run the co. in Lon-
don, which is a heap too much in war time.
The battery is eating into ore reserves: at
43.767 tons there are 7541 tons less than in
1916. Still values are up 6s. per ton. while
costs keep at 41s. 3d. The co. got £70.000
of gold last year, but after deducting mine
development exes., the credit for the term
dropped to only £1340. With a shoot of
good ore 350 ft. long at No. 6 level south and
a chance of picking up a fresh lens of ore
out to the east the shaft goes down another
lift.

Several molybdenite deposits are being
opened up around the disappointing gold-
mining centre of Warriedar (W.A.), and
an option for £l2OO has been taken over
two of them by A. E. Morgans, who has
other irons in the fire there. A number of
alleged molybdenite shows at Leonora and
elsewhere are at present on sale in Perth
and Adelaide, but so far no one has been
game enough to buy them. Someone with
apparently more confidence in Western
mining men than in Western offerings has
ene"i?ed Harley Wright, metallurgist at
Boulder Perseverance, to run a N. S. Wales
molybdenite venture.
Westralian Government lmd a chance the
other day to revive mining interest in the
State and missed it. An Under-Secretary
for Mines was wanted to take the place of
H. S. King, who becomes Surveyor-General
and permanent head of the Lands Depart-
ment. and the selection of a trained mining
engineer with a sound knowledge of local
mining was widely urged. Such an appoint-
ment would have imparted a stimulus to tin-
whole industry, but Cabinet thought other-
wise and gave the next man in the office the
job. An agitation has now started for the
appointment of a Commissioner for Mines
free from all political control, and with
power to help those claim-holders who show
any inclination to help themselves. At pre
sent all sorts of barriers and obstacles are
placed in their way.
* * * *
The old Silver Spur, near Stanthorpe
(Q.), is to be worked again under the
management of M. A. Corrie. The furnace
has been repaired and warmed up, and every-
thing is ready for another smelting run from
a mine that in early days proved exception-
ally rich in silver. A streak of ore has been
discovered parallel to the old workings, and
this formation is now being driven on at
200 ft. A 32-ton parcel of ore treated at
Cockle Creek (N.S.W.) returned 18oz. gold.
10450 z. silver and 2 tons lGcwt. of lead, of
a gross value of £358.

Sydney, February 4, 3915.
My Dear Auini, —
Mrs. Donaldson, wife of the skipper of the
missing Matunga, was the hostess at a little
dinner and theatre party the other evening.
The first difficulty at the matinge for the
Crown-street Women’s Hospital was to find
the programme. It was entombed in £llO
worth of advts. Add £25 from the sale,
and you have something solid towards the
£5OO which lion, organiser Nellie Leach set
out to get. Nellie herself sang at intervals,
and looked like a French bonbon in a tulle
decoration made with a bustle. And hon.
business-manager Gard Trouton —he who
stalked every well-known firm in town for
the advts.—wipes his pince-nez and plainly
resolves to go on paying the bachelor tax.
Girl in the abstract may be in the bloom of life.
Girl in battalions, especially when she’s in
her smart society undress, each little angel
wanting her own way and fighting for it
with her most alluring dimples, is a menace
to the peace of Man.
The matinee was a triumph, in spite of
one aisle in the Tivoli being choked with
the Smart Set’s presentation bouquets to
its dear friends in amateur grease-paint.
Make-up was good, bad and worse; one fair-
haired damsel was rouged to look like a
clown, with hectic spots on each cheekbone.
Goossens was magnificent in the “Barber
of Seville.” Mrs. Bertie Kelly and Neville
Mayman, with Molly Levy and Barbara
Bowker, snipped a page from Peg Woffing-
ton’s life. Peg had ninety-and-nine lives,
judging by the literature that still stirs her
ashes. Barbara Gordon got a cwt. or so
of flowers from her pals in front of the
house. Maggie Jarvis, beautifully frocked
in a blue hat, a very large pendant, and
yards of accordeon pleating, warbled in
French. Miss Segur was in a Paris frock.
Aggie Mitchell squirmed through the Per-
sian Garden in a glittering gold and silver
dancer’s outfit. Lionel Lawson came from
the Flying School, bringing his magic fiddle.
And the stalls had an unrivalled view of
local society strutting across the stage as
drapers’ models.
In consequence of Nellie Leach’s success
at the recent Hospital matinee, H. D.
Mclntosh has persuaded her to take a turn
on the vaudeville circuit. It required some
persuasion, for John Leach pere has been
steadily “agin” professional life for his only
chick. She is a pupil of the late Marchesi
—the wonderful old woman who discovered
the pearls in Melba’s voice. Before going
to Paris to the Marchesi studio, the Neutral
bay girl had lessons from Tosti. This was
during her school days in London.
The Most Excellent Order of the British
Empire is a war-time rash, and various Aus-
tralians are to bear the marks of it. The
inoculation is in the hands of the Governor-
General ; and Easter is likely to witness the
first outbreak. Thereafter, so far as the lady
is concerned, she’s to be “Dame” this or that.
Fortune was very kind to Mrs. Theo
Marks the other Sunday evening. Few
women can let a suit-case with £2OOO-worth
of gems go astray with any chance of seeing
their glitter again. But the aesthetic
Dorothy Marks managed it. She left the
treasure under the seat of a mountain train.
The carriage linked up with an ensine bound
for Albury, and the perambulating jewels
went to the border and came back to Sydney
with never a stone the less. Of course it
isn’t the sort of thing to do as a habit.
Recent visitors to “the East” that is North
say that the N. S. Wales Commercial Agent
of those parts has done what few Europeans
do—he has conquered colloquial Japanese.
At big Japanese functions no man is more
sought after than the tall, white-haired and
masterful member of the Suttor family.
Suttor’s “beat” extends from Peking to
Sourabaya and from Manila to Tokio. As
the man who carried out most of our big
N. S. Wales railway deviations, he knows
the short cuts, or he could not be so well
known as he is throughout “the East.”
A son of the late Frank Norrie, of Rose
Bay, was wed in Kent (Eng.) tftwards the
end of 1917. He’s a Lieut.-Colonel in the
A.I.F. The bride was Jessie Frances
White, a daughter of the manager of a P.
and O. branch line. Second-best man was
Captain Godby, son of the Melbourne Canon.
Vera Samuel, a grand-daughter of the late
Saul —he who achieved a baronetage when
such things were going in Australian official
circles in the wliile-ago—was one of the
attendant maids.
An offshoot of the big Windeyer family,
Alice W. Thompson, of West Maitland, has
been married very quietly in St. James’s
Church to Alfred Gale, of this hamlet. All
the fuss was a day or so beforehand, when
scores of friends and relatives mounted
Farmer’s lift to say nice things to the girl.
One of the well-known Hume family,
originally of the Yass district, was married
in St. James’s the other evening. The bride
was Margaret Edith, a daughter of the late
Andrew Hamilton Hume, of Rye Park. Bro
ther Bruce, of Yass, gave the girl to her new
husband, James B. Dalton, of Epping. Gwen
Slatter attended to the bridal finery, and
H. Milford was second-best man. The first
Hume to make his presence felt in Australia
was the late explorer Hamilton, whose old
home is set in green pastures some miles
out of the town that once had visions of
being the Federal capital.
There’s word of yachtsman Walter Marks
—in peace time commodore of the R.P.A.
Yacht Club —coming home on leave. After
about three years on active service in patrol
work lie’s going to have a whiff of his native
gum-trees.
Sporting man Oxenham is holding his head
a bit higher this week, for son Norman, who
wears a major’s crown in the A.M.C., has a
D.S.O. to his name. Major Norman, three
times mentioned in despatches also, is an
old Riverview boy, who knew the Hun in
his own territory before getting up against
him in France, Macedonia and Egypt. A
younger brother, Gordon, is piloting a ’plane
in the A.F.C.
A former Maoriland resident, A. D.
Spence, now located here, was wed in St.
Stephen’s the other noon. The pretty dark-
eyed bride is a Melbourne girl, Gwladys
Brierly. Mother gave her lass away. There
was a snow-white tailor-made for the cere-
mony, and a dove-gray frock to go away in,
and a very quiet party after the vows were
made.

text in ugly All Saints’ at Woollalira, had a
weddinv of his own the other afternoon. The
bride was Eliza Skelton, of Laurella, Croy-
don, and the knot was tied in that suburb
by brothers Best and W. L. Langlev. It
was a black-coat affair, with cleric Alexander
te help the ’groom on his pilgrimage.
The c o u n t r y
teacher needs spark-
ing up when she (or
he) comes to town.
And that is where
the Hereford House
Reunion Club comes
in. Founded in 1912
by Lockhart Easton
as a non-political
meeting-place for ex-
students, the war
presently swept out
both president and
secretary on active
service. Other mem-
bers joined the Great
Push, and the Club
almost wilted away
till the beginning of
1917. Since then a
vigorous organisation has been able to send
out a friendly hand to the most isolated ex-
students. District secretaries have waved
their fountain-pens, and the school marra out
back responds. A new constitution —a seri-
ous matter —has been drafted and softly
flicks the existing Summer Schools.
“These’,, says energetic Miss Baker, the
secretary, “left a want. The staff of the
Training College was willing to help us, and
Unfler-Secretary Board has given us the
concession we asked for. An extra School
will be held in one week of our time, another
week being given by the Department. The
main difference between this extra school
and those in existence will be in the range
of subjects. Much midnight oil is spent
over unfruitful study for higher-classifica-
tion exams.; what is needed is a little special
coaching. That’s what it will offer.” A
hostel for young girl-students is another
dream of Hereford House, and a co. may
blossom forth with this as its purpose in life.
There is, in fact, hardly an end to what
Hereford House may do for the teachers of
N. S. Wales; and the movement has in its
lion. general secretary and treasurer,
Frances Baker, a live wire to give it energy.
I send you Judith Fletcher’s little picture
of her.
The minor horrors of recent first-nights in
Sydney theatres may result in comparative
peace amongst the young ladies in the front
of the gallery. Their engaging habit of act-
ing as arbitrators of fashion, and “guying”
anyone whose dress they don’t approve of,
has driven some of the stall-fed theatre-
goers back to the less public d.c. One quiet
maiden who went with a few friends to the
gallery on the occasion of Ivathlene Mac-
donell’s tears says the whole nuisance was
connned to 20 or 30 lady galleryites with
their gentlemen friends to encourage them.
Obviously the general public must get its
quills up when an inconsiderable section of
a crowded theatre takes on the job of dicta-
tor. It may allow one man to be Dictator
of the Commonwealth. But when it comes
to interfering with pleasures, it’s a serious
matter.
Maud, the second daughter of the Ilpar-
ran-Glen Innes Ogilvies, has promised to
make Ernest White, of Bluff Downs, Chart-
ers Towers, North Bananaland, more satis-
fied with things in general. The Ogilvies are
indefatigable war-workerfe. Their Red Cross
toil fetches mamma and the girls to Sydney
fairly often. The bride-to-be is a grand-
daughter of the late E. D. S. Ogilvie, of Yul-
gilbar station—an immense cattle run at the
head of the Clarence River. A matter of 45
years ago grandpa Ogilvie entertained the
then Governor, the Earl of Belmore, at the
homestead, Yulgilbar Towers, a picturesque
many-roomed edifice overlooking miles and
miles of fertile country and many flocks and
herds. Guests came to a great ball, given in
his Ex.’s honor, from all parts of the rivers.
It was a royal entertainment, and the
ancient inhabitants will tell you about it
with goggly eyes to this day. Belmore ex-
pressed himself as having spent there the
most delightful time of his stay in Australia.
Characteristic of Sydney Uni.’s soldier-
Prof. David that he should spend a short
furlough in London attending a conference
of wiseheads who are investigating the better
use of water-power. And it is characteristic
of the muddling of the Defence Department
that a favorite brilliant mining engineer
whom David wanted as his lieutenant is
still kept hanging around an Australian
camp, though he enlisted at the earliest pos-
sible moment —about eight months ago.
In Sydney, on a visit, well-groomed S. A.
Whiteman, who has lived and traded in Ger-
man New Guinea for years. When the Big
Fight struck the Pacific Isles like a typhoon,
Whiteman and British Consul Jolly were in-
terned, but were offered their freedom if they
would give their ’'parole not to do or say
anything to help the Fatherland’s enemies.
Both declined with thanks—and I fancy I
should have liked Whiteman better if he hadn’t
afterwards made a claim on the Common-
wealth for a reward of £5OOO for the help he
says he did render in finding the German
boat Comet when it was cunningly hidden
in a welter of green vegetation.
Mrs. Young, of the Brisbane Courier, has
been here on a brief holiday. Of her two
fine lads one had just passed his final den-
tist’s exam, with great credit when, entering
a Queensland camp, he “contracted typhoid”
—I think that’s the official way of putting
it —and died. The other boy is with the
Engineers at our Roseville camp. Which
means that he will be almost sure to get
away within two years.

A correspondent across the seas: —
The Australian Red Cross in London is now in
the handsome, roomy mansion at 36 Grosvenor-plaee,
once the town house of Tasmania’s wealthy Governor
Newdigate. It overlooks the grounds of Buckingham
Palace; wherefore it has more light and air than
some of the walled-in tenements that used to shelter
society at a terrific rent in the season. The big hall
of the new premises and the adjacent library are
now offices and an inquiry bureau, where Chief
Commissioner Murdoch and his staff roll up their
cuffs.
There is another pretty Australian girl
in mourning in England. She’s Hilda Rix,
whose first dabble in art was in connection
with the Education Department’s “School-
Paper,” but who later developed into a can-
able miniature painter. She is the widow
of Major G. M. Nicholas, D. 5.0., whose
brothers, Lieut. B. Nicholas, M.C., and Cpl.
F. Nicholas, have also been sacrificed in the
European madness.
For bravery while doing his bit with our
Engineers at Ypres, Vincent Dalziel, of Mos-
man, has’had a Military Medal served out to
him. By way of a change, he lias got a
commission now in Britain’s busy navy.
A former Sydney pressman, T. R.
Roydhouse—he used to edit Sunday Times
—is over from Maoriland. Only for a trip,
He still works away with the nibs, but has a
farm to tether him to the Fern Isles.
The Phil. Society gave its first Saturday-
night pop with a big house to fight for
encores. But conductor Joe Bradley put both
feet on the nuisance, and the programme
had a clear run.
The schoolboy D.C.M., Wallace Freeborn
(you’ll remember how he returned to school
after doing a man’s work in the Big Fight),
has acquired some more honors—three first-
class and four second-class passes at his
leaving exam. He is now booked for the
engineering school of the Uni.
Born in Maoriland
—Wellington was
her native town—
Ella Gorinley, 8.A.,
has lived here long-
enough to have been
a Fort-street girl.
She meant to join the
ordinary teaching-
staff of the N. S.
Wales Education De-
partment, but the
finger of destiny
pointed towards the
sea. About six years
ago the ’partment
put one toe into the
water, so to speak,
by appointing this
virile young woman,
who swims with her
brains, to teach teachers of her own sex
physical culture, life-saving, hockey, etc. The
amphibious billet has grown into an im-
portance not contemplated by the origina-
tors of the experiment. Ella Gormley’s
gift of organisation (during 10 days in the
December holidays 5000 N._ S. Wales school
kiddies were taught to swim) has brought
her. a new billet —Mistress of Physical Cul-
ture in the High Schools. From February 1
she amalgamates that job with her other
work as overseer of the art of making young
primary-school Cornstalks swim like fish
and take first place in athletics in the
field. Here is the energetic lady’s photo.
Judith Fletcher’s camera has done its best,
but it can’t reproduce the fresh pink com-
plexion of the out-o’-door school-marm.

y urs, Jules Neuburger,” begging me to

16 Rue Cadet, Paris, cordially invites all the
members of the Order to call when in Paris. Eng'-
lish-speaking brethren will be in attendance daily
from 2 to 5 p.m.
“And you, my dear madam,” adds M. Bes-
nard, who is Worshipful Master or some-
thing such, “will, for your sex, doubtless be
glad to know that, in assuring our fraternal
help to your gallant Australian brothers, we
shall be glad also, in case they are ill or
wounded, to serve as intermediary between
them and their relatives.” And, as an after-
thought he adds “etc.” to the “relatives.”
Even Worshipful Masters were boys once.
From “Australia Jane” in Brisbane:—
Died of pneumonia in France, Corporal Russell
Love, eldest son of James Love, of Isles, Love and
Co. Young Love was turned down three times on
account of defective eyesight, but at last signed
on for special duty, leaving Brisbane with the
Motor Transport Column in December, 1916. He was
in the fighting along the Ypres front, and was
reported injured last November. The boy, who was
only 25, was a keen sport and a crack shot. Captain
Tom Macleod, the airman who recently acquired a
Croix de Guerre of the first class, is his uncle—-Mrs.
Love’s brother.

month, with promises of tailor-builts. Taf-
fetas still crackle and rustle in the nattv
high-waisted skirts of the moment.
The late Matthew Harris, knight, left
£32,000, to be cut up amongst sons and
daughters. But there had been a previous
carving. Ultimo, the big dusty estate which
came from his father, the late John Harris,
was sliced up under a deed of settlement
during 1913. Hence the comparatively
small fortune just mentioned in the probate
list.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

Melbourne, February 4, 1918.
My Dear Yarrie, —
It is far from the Malvern Ladies’ Rifle
Club to the trenches of France; but George
Williams, the club's original instructor, has
made the connection. Williams was one of
the practical souls who helped to get the
Australian soldier acquainted with the ser-
vice rifle. He wasn't able to jump away him-
self, having a considerable domestic estab-
lishment to attend to; but many an early
soldier in the field owed to Williams his skill
in potting a far mark. By-and-bye in his
ardor Williams took a hand in starting the
Malvern Club, with Governor Stanley’s own
missus to pull a trigger on the mat. Then
he made arrangements to show the Hun
what could be done in the field, and departed
with the light of battle in his fatherly eye.
lie did more instructing in England before
he crossed the Channel, hut was finally
allowed to hop over and see what it was
really like. Now he has the Military Medal.
From Beersheba to Johanna:—
I hate to contradict a lady; but Mrs. Norman
Brookes in her “Broken Idols” is off the track when
she states that many of our soldiers in Egypt marry
girls of mixed nationality. I’ve been over here
since December, 1914, and during that time I have
only heard of four Billjim weddings, and the other
parties were French ladies whose parents are well
known in Cairo. I’ve heard yarns and yarns about
our chaps having linked up for keeps with ladies
who—who —well, 3 r ou know who —but it's pure fic-
tion. I’ll admit that the gay girls residing in the
Wazar tried to capture Bill, with an eye, of course,
on his three bob a day; but Bill gets sober very
quickly. No, Johanna —Bill’s heart is back in Aus-
tralia. How utterly lots of him didn’t know till he
had tried something else.
Lieut. Ken Stephen has won the Military
Cross. The good-looking youngster is a
nephew of the Madden dame and a step-
nephew also. His dad married Blanche
Travers, whose stepmother was the Madden
dame’s elder sister. The stepmother afore-
said, after some years of widowhood, married
a Polish Count in England—and is wonder-
ing, no doubt, what the title is worth.
Mrs. Norman Brookes is just back from
another submarine-defying act, looking rather
the worse for wear. Red Cross Commis-
sioner Norman, who had been stalking health
in England after a heavy go of Mesopotamian
malaria, has recovered sufficiently to go back
to his work in Bagdad. So Mrs. Brookes
packed up and got back to her trio of young
daughters.
Frank Crozier, who is mentioned now as
a likely successor to popular Fred McCubbin
at the' art school, is at present in France
doing war work. Though of the younger
school of artists, he has exhibited several
times in Melbourne, usually in conjunction
with others. There are examples of his work
in the Anzac Book.
Norman Hodges has had the Military
Cross pinned on his tunic. Just before the
war began he married dashing Vanda
Clarke, daughter of the alphabetical Clarkes,
of Toorak. Vanda has been doing her bit
at a Russian hospital in London. Norman
was born lucky, for he owns, besides
a charming wife, a charming stepmother,
who lives at Macedon in a poetical humpy
composed of rose gardens, mullioned win-
dows, pansies, white walls and flower-covered
pigstyes. Before she became the second
Mrs. Hodges she was widow of Bob
Chirnside.
You oegin to realise that war is Hell
when you get into the Victoria Barracks
and read the solemn inscription at the
inquiry office : —•
NOTICE.
If, after making an inquiry, no reply is received
within 20 minutes, you are kindly requested to
remind the inquiry clerk.
But, of course, the time-allowance for the
Minister of Defence is more liberal.
The field coffee-stalls instituted by Red
Cross efforts for the Australian divisions
have helped old Billjim along. A big officer
says that during the offensive at Ypres men
were heartened wonderfully by a hot drink
and a cigarette shortly after being wounded.
Their appreciation was beyond words —-the
portable stalls made miracles possible; for a
wounded man will bear almost any torture
if he can only close his lips on a cigarette.
Captain Rob Cussen died in a Melbourne
military hospital last week after a hard fight
with pneumonia. A fine fellow and universal
favorite. He was the son of the late Martin
Cussen, M.L.A., of Tally Ho. Mooroopna,
and a brother of Leo, the well-known lawyer.
Here are two more Australian war-babies
with great records. Lieut.-Colonel Donald
Tieehurst Moore (he belongs to both Mel-
bourne and Sydney) commands a battalion
at 24. lie has been wounded twice, D.S.O.'d
and awarded the Belgian Order of the
Crown. Warren Anderson was a Duntroon
lieutenant at 18, and got the crown on his
21st birthday, lie is now on stall duty in
Egypt.
Courtly Staniforth Smith is at present in
Blighty. After a term as Administrator ot
i'apua Staniforth joined the A.I.F. as a
private in 15)10, and eventually got his com-
mission. Having injured his ankle at
Messines. he is doing home duty at Tidworth
until a Board declares him fit for active ser-
vice once more.
They are telling of a refreshing little inci-
dent at a recent function. A venerable per-
sonage commenting on a politician who had
just spoken said: “I can’t stand the d—d
fellow, lie cleans his own boots.” Where-
upon the municipal party on his right
chipped in: “I’m like you, ; my wife
always cleans mine!”
Mrs. E. Wynne, wife of Kew’s First Lord
of the Civic Cupboard, was so unlucky as to
pick up typhoid after she and the family
had tracked to the sea-shoye for a little extra
health. The invalid, who was Miss Rankin,
daughter of a one-time noted city doctor, is
doing well now; but typhoid is a long job.
_ Old Hobart is going gay. The event of
last week was a dinner-party at Government
House, at which the Elect were fed. Lady
Monash had a seat and a plate, and Major
Cottrell Dormer and his wife (he passed
through Melbourne recently returning from'
France) were guests. Others were Captain
Glossop and his new bride. The Bartons
(they are staying at Hadley’s) also managed
to peck a few grains.
Regatta Day in Hobart was gorgeously
trimmed. The Newdigate Excellency and
his lady, with Genthelp Captain Fitz Gerald,
were received by Warden T. Murdoch and
his wife and taken out on the launch Egeria.
Captain Glossop and his bi’ide and Judge
and Mrs. Ewing also lent glitter to the show,
and all hands had morning tea on the U.T.S.
Dart as Captain Newman’s guests. And
when all was over, the sun went down back-
wards in a swirl of tea leaves.
Fritz Hart’s boy Basil is still an inmate
of Mount St. Evins hospital. The fine kiddie
has had to endure several operations since
iiis gun accident of some months ago.
An embittered taxpayer suggests what
looks like a simple remedy for the expensive
Government-motor habit. This ingenious
Goth would have all Government cars painted
a brilliant red, like the postal vans, with dis-
guishing marks to show which Department
was having a week-end joy-ride. The idea
should be adopted at once.
A Victorian squatter-lawyer has a stage-
struck daughter who went to London with a
meagre allowance and without the paternal
blessing. Now dad is worried because his
girl is bringing an expensive breach-of-pro-
mise suit in London against an Australian
squatter who is in the war-zone.
Lady Helen had a small informal tea-
party last week for some returned invalided
soldiers —mostly homing Maorilanders. Our
Brig.-General Johnston and his lady, also the
Norwegian Consul-General and his wife, who
had been lunching at Government House,
joined the tea-party. In the evening the
Maorilanders had another party at the
“Aladdin” panto. There was a delighted
roar from them when the Maori girls
marched on in their brown-grey tights and
grass skirts. Those wahines revelled in
flowers and chocs, for the rest of the week.
The Last of the Belltoppers died on St.
Kilda-road during a raging north wind one
day last week. A newly-decorated personage
was going to lunch at Government House.
He shared an open motor with his missus,
who was in danger of losing her ospreyed
pancake. So Sir Galahad chivalrously let
go his own glittering tile to help his spouse.
Immediately he relaxed his grip, the topper
shot off his head and trundled playfully
under the juggernaut wheels of a road-
mender’s steam-roller.
It’s one thing to get into Gippsland, and
quite another to get out of it, as manv a
roads-and-bridges deputation will yet explain
to Works-Minister MoWhae. Their troubles
are heaviest in winter; but McWhae’s own
summer experience will teach him to lend an
attentive car. He motored lately to Mor-
well, whence he intended to visit his pro-
perty at Budgeree, 15 more-or-less vertical
miles further On. In the hills he took the
wrong turning, and as he couldn’t ’bout-ship
in the narrow cutting, he had to back his
car up a slope for a mile. The machine
caught fire, but Mac., with a touch of genius,
put out the flames with his cargo of soft
drinks. If it had been whisky the car would
have been fumes and ashes in five minutes.
When eventually he set sail for home he
wouldn’t risk the unlucky route again, and
engaged an ox-persuader with six bullocks
to tow his fiery chariot up and then down a
mile-long hogback, until he safely reached
the Boolarra-Welshpool road.
Our pet beauty-consul, Cavaliere Lies, of
Italy has been promoted to the first-class ot
his rank. I don’t know what class he was m
before, but his costume was most becoming.
Bigjolm Forrest, whose head has lately
been the object of medical attention, is now
out of hospital and back at the Grand.
Lieut.-Governor and retired Chief Justice
Stone, of Westralia, who has been in Mel-
bourne for some weeks having his eyes
attended to. is about to take a whirl with
Fate and see if he can’t get a few more
years’ good eyesight by having the cataract-
film removed. Meanwhile he is hob-nobbing
with the G.-G. and generally having as good
a time as the gathering mists will allow.
He nearly perished of laryngitis, by the way,
before making the trip across from Perth,
but now lie feels fit for anything.
Sister Adelaide’s chronicles: —
On Saturday at Mount Lofty there was a carnival
for the benefit of the Prisoners of War Fund. The
promoters had better luck than last year, when the
festivity was washed out by rain. Money came in
freely this time. The Premier’s wife, Mrs. Peake,
formally opened the show, and the Governor put in
an hour or so watching the sports before picking up
the train for Melbourne.
Lieut. Whitington, who was reported killed in
action at Passcliendale, but afterwards turned up as
a prisoner in Germany, has written from Soltau that
he is “getting along famously” and hopes soon to
have his wounded right arm out of plaster of paris.
Members of the Yacht Squadron, with Rear-Com-
modore and Mrs. A. E. Tolley as host and hostess,
turned up in force on Saturday night to say good-bye
to the Commodore, Dr. E. W. Morris, who is going
back to the Front. Every Eligible man belonging to
the club has volunteered for active service and no
eligibles are accepted as new members.
F. J. Mills has resigned the editorship of the
Returned Soldiers’ magazine, and his place is being
filled by Donald Kerr, M.M., who annexed an LL.B.
degree at the University before enlisting.
Dr. Francis Muecke, who is best known to fame as
Ada Crossley’s husband, had the bad luck to break a
leg while tree-chopping in England. It seems a dull
way to get wounded after working in hospitals at
the Front practically since the outbreak of war.
Deserting is not all beer and biscuits. Witness
the case of a quitter who wrote his wife that
he was coming home for a day. The patriotic lady
promptly informed the military police and had them
waiting for him with a boisterous welcome. There
was also the case of the man who was run to earth
between the wire and flock mattresses of a bed, with
his sister reclining on top. The lady in that case
was given her choice of parting with £2O or three
months’ 'liberty for aiding and abetting her frosty-
footed relative.
The State War Council isn’t finding it an easy
business to arrange patriotic appeals for 1918 so
that there shall be no overlapping of the big efforts.
The last half of February has been allotted to the
Central Trench Comforts Fund, to the chagrin of the
Glenelg Progress Committee, which wanted to hold
its usual summer carnival at that time for the benefit
of Red Cross, Trench Comforts and Cheer-Up Funds.
Glenelg next asked for permission to go on with its
carnival, restricting the object to its own particular
Trench Comforts Fund (3rd Light Horse), but that,
too, was refused, and it looks now as if the under-
taking will have to be cried off.
Beryl Cussen, daughter of Dr. and Mrs.
Gerald Cussen, of Sydney-road, Brunswick,
has said “Yes” to David Peter, of Dunalister,
Oaklands. David has returned from the war
and deserves his good luck.
Lou Cadby, a wealthy jeweller, of St.
Ivilda, has quietly taken wife No. 8. She is
Winnie Hogan, a young Tasmanian girl.
The bridegroom, who is 70 or so, is a lover
of horses, two meals a day and the fact that
lie’s entirely self-made. She’s musically
inclined, a good housekeeper and has money
in her own right.
Guido Baracclii, the plutocratic Socialist
who lately got into hot water with the Uni-
versity authorities, has acquired a wife in
the pleasing person of Katrina Tobin,
a decorative feature of the “Aladdin”
panto, chorus. The lady is a brunette with
as beautiful a pair of eyes as any in Mel-
bourne. She has for long cherished histri-
onic ambitions, and had a walking-on part
in Allan Wilkie’s production of “Richard
II.” The deed was done privately at the
house of the Rev. F. Sinelaire, the pictur-
esque Socialistic parson. Guido has given
up his diggings at Oxford Chambers, and the
two have taken a flat in East Melbourne.
The sky was like a tremendous canvas by
Dor£ just before the cyclone hit Brighton
on Saturday. Clouds were heaped in an
appalling jumble, mountains high, and
burned with a hot bronze color as they
raced overhead. A fringe of ragged grey
rain-carriers whirled with the cloud-moun-
tains, waving like a punksfti worked by
Satan. Before the Saturday-afternooners
could turn and fly the fury was upon them.
Shrieking wind, dust, rain and fragments of
ice were the principal ingredients; but there
were hundreds of others, some of them torn
off the houses. The calm came as sud-
denly as the storm. Then the ambulance
helpers went out and brought in the wounded
and the half-drowned.
A Melbourne Billjim on the other side has
been listening to Rosina Buekmann singing
Wagnerian roles, and she’s “as plump as the
plumpest songstress we know.” You may
have one guess who that is.
Letters from the U.S.A. report that Melba
is suffering badly from nerves. Her recent
stage accident and the complaints of Uncle
Sam papers that several Germans were in
her company of songbirds probably helped
to upset one who is always going at high
pressure. Nowadays she is so “jumpy” that
she thinks twice before stepping into a car.
Pianist Nellie Billings, who lately wedded
the exquisitely-gloved and accessoried .Mai
don Robb after a well-advertised courtship,
has entered on a musical teaching-partner-
ship with singer Marguerite Henderson. This
Australian soprano used to teach at the Uni-
versity Con., while the other half of the firm
has done it at Hast Melbourne. The singer
has had a considerably-travelled musical
education. Her mother used to be the best-
known Government-servant of her sex in
Victoria, with the job of leading lady at Pent-
ridge Stockade, where she did more than will
ever be known to put the State’s female
boarders back on the right track.
The panto, people are busy assuring the
Melbourne public, too, that the kiddies just
love it. In “Aladdin” the official watch has
been put on the small performers, and their
seven appearances run to only 18 minutes
altogether. For the rest of the time they’re
mostly waiting for “Mr. Bailey” to come oil’
and amuse them. There never was a boss
more popular.
When sounds of revelry by day disturbed
the quiet of Brighton in the vicinity of Vera
Pearce's villa, the Ancient Order of Wow-
sers at once had visions of a wild orgy, with
champagne magnums lying about the floor
and drunken actors trying to do the Turkey
trot on the dining-room table. What they
would have seen had they been allowed to
enter was a pack of happy and noisy
youngsters busy w T ith cream puffs, lemonade
and other indigestibles, the occasion being a
bonzer party given by Vera to the kiddies
who take part in the “Dick Whittington”
panto. Even the most blase member of the
profesh, aged 15, admitted that it was “very
enjoyable.”
Muriel Starr put on “The Easiest Way,”
her new piece, on Saturday night, and a full
house stewed gently through the show. Very
rich old Saddler deserted his club for the
evening and was stalled with a party of
womenfolk. Returned soldier Char. Crespin
(he was once a genthelp) boxed with young
De Bavay and two De Bavay flappers—one
of them in dimity-flowered silk and the other
in vivid pink. Mrs. Geoff Syme, in another
compartment, wore a twilight-purple ninon
shoulder-cape and waved a fan of real lace.
Mrs. Dearden was in the audience, which
also included the Charlie Campbells. Mrs.
Charlie wore the demurest of pink frocks.
Aide Conant, off the chain and minus the
duty lapels, sat in the stalls.
Muriel Starr has a cousin Starr on with
her in the place of Tien Hogue. The cousin
has brought some very-latest dresses from
’Murka. One is a grey velvet with the
blousy skirt hobbled into the ankles with
embroidery—-the new silhouette, my dear.
The straight shoulder-cape is another new
notion. It falls from the shoulders in a
straight line to the ankles. Muriel manages
to achieve a blase, worldly look in her
clothes by wearing a hat lined with hob-red
velvet and clothes built of furniture plush.
She wears a horrifying morning dress to her
stage breakfast. It is of rose silk patterned
like wallpaper with silver and edged with
fur and a train. The top is missing and the
“angel” ninon sleeves are apparently glued
on to her arms.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Quan”: A Lake Cowal (N.S.W.) man,
William Low, has got rid of the flies at his place by
means of some kaleid wasps. Blowflies are the pet
aversion of these insects, which can be got for next
to nothing from the Government Entomologist.
“Greasy”: “Condamine’s” flight of foxes
60 chains wide and half an hour to pass would be a
mere straggling mob (B. 10/1/’18). I have seen
camps on the Tweed-side that would provide a flight
over a 10-mile stretch from sundown to milking-
time. The biggest camp of foxies is to be found at
Double Island, in the Norman River. I had the
luck to be passing the island early one morning as
the things were getting back to hang. They
attacked the ship ’and swarmed the rigtging. The
skipper in an ill-advised moment let go the whistle
and the whole colony rose as one bird or animal.
The sun was blacked out. The foxies in their wild
alarm mistook the funnel for a hollow tree, and
Martin Penn, the fireman, got the surprise of his
life, when he opened the fire-box to find roast fox
come rolling out by the score. They were being
shoved right through by the mob behind trying to
get in to camp. Then the fires went out, and we
had to drift down some miles before we could stoke
up again. This is official.
* * * *
“Jimmie Pannikin”: I met her at Goat-
andgalah (N.S.W.) Provisional School. Her pupil 3
drilled remarkably well ; so I said: “The junior-
cadet training is admirable. The whole squad does
its turnings beautifully. How did you manage to
make them remember ‘left’ from ‘right,’ and so on?”
Said the bright young Australian: “I had a lot of
trouble at first with ‘left turn’ and ‘right turn’ till
I translated the terms into bullockese and used
‘Wey; whoa back!’ and ‘Gee off, there,’ for a fe.v
months.” “How did you manage ‘about turn’ ?”
“Oh, I called, ‘Gee off, there, right round!’”
* * * *
“Kawana” : You needn’t go thirsty in the
Maoriland bush, even on a range-top, if there are
rata vines about. Some years ago 1 came across a
survey party in Auckland forest country debating
as to which side of a high ridge they should descend
for the purpose of finding a creek. “What you want
to go down te gully for?” asked Tuhi, the chain-
man. “You no see te ratat” Then Tuhi, seizing
a depending vine of five- or six-inch diameter, toma-
hawked it into two-foot lengths. “Homai te billy,”
he said, and from the nearly blood-red cut ends of
the hollow vegetable he ran out a billyful of good
clean water, a trifle woody in taste but none the
worse for that. The rata vine is a good friend to the
bushman on the high tablelands, such as the Mamaku
and the Patatere, where you may bullock your swag
along for a long day’s march without striking creek
or spring.
* * * •
“Sarto”: If ever you run out of wire-
less in the bush, here’s the way to produce a call
that's always sure of a hearing. Fold the fingers of
one hand tightly over those of the other, leaving a
cavity between the palms. Bring the thumbs
together and leave a small opening between the
palms at the wrists. Bend the thumbs over and use
them for a mouthpiece, blowing through the hole
that forms between them. The wind escapes through
the opening between the palms at the wrists pro-
ducing a dull whistle. Straighten out the fingers of
the top hand, and you get a shrill, piercing sound
which can be alternated with the duller one by open-
ing and shutting the hand. This call can be heard
for a great distance. I hope the Religious Editor
doesn’t go practising it in George-street and stop the
traffic.
ZERO.
“And was it cold so far above the clouds ?”
“Cold! Why, when I struck a match to light a cigarette the flame froze solid!”
“Wandi”: Some of the orchardists at last
have recognised that birds are their best friends and
have appealed for their better protection. The work
of combating insect pests is growing yearly more
strenuous, and the cocky yells now for more birds
where the fruit grows. Formerly’, orchardists and
others were too prone to jump to the conclusion that
because birds frequented gardens they
were there to feed on the crops, whereas
many species went to feed on the insects
and scale that usually find congenial lodg-
ings on the premises. Numerous small
birds are attracted also by the suitability'
of hedges, orange and other trees for
nest-building. Some of them attack
fruit, but the damage they do in this
way is small compared with the services
they render the planter; and he is a
mean person who would begrudge his
little feathered police a few morsels of
fruit or grain in return for the good
work they do.

“Machete”: Biographers of the late
Nicholas Minister, otherwise Nicholas the Greek, have
forgotten to mention the exploit he was particularly
proud of. It happened in his more stodgy and
respectable days, when, having given up battle, black-
birding and other pastimes, he settled down to a
humdrum existence as king of the Greek pearling
colony in the Trobriand Islands. The feat in question
was the complete disorganisation of the dusky young
ladies’ sewing class at the Methodist Mission, which
disputed with Nicholas the sovereignty of those
Arcadian atolls. The young ladies of the Trobriands
appealed to Nicholas, for they were as pagan as
he, and it grieved him exceedingly, on a return from
a long cruise in other waters, to find them all
gathered safely under the wing of the “Taparora.”
Now the particular section of the Mission which
harbored the sewing-class was close to the beach of
the lagoon. Nicholas anchored his schooner just
opposite, close inshore. That afternoon he issued a
general invitation to the nobility and gentry of the
neighborhood to an At Home on board the schooner.
The Mission authorities kept a vigilant eye on the
sewing-class, but rumors floated ashore of unlimited
calico and tobacco aboard the schooner, to say noth-
ing of tinned salmon and golden syrup. Then in
the tropic dusk Nicholas slily started the first phono-
graph ever heard in the Trobriands. The schooner
sailed at daybreak for a destination unknown. The
At Home and the phonograph were still in full
swing, and the craft was dangerously overmanned
with the entire sewing-class. As Nicholas said when
he told me the yarn: “Watta t’ ’ell dose girla want
witta sew ? Dey weara nuttin’ but da pandanus
leaf, an’ mos’ time notta dat!”
» * * *
“L’Fou”: Looking backward it seems that
the man on the land has something to be thankful
for. In 1887 the badly-scared N. S. Wales Govern-
ment offered a £25,000 reward for a method of
exterminating rabbits. A Commission appointed to
sort out some 4000 applications for the bonus turned
down, among other schemes, the introduction of
disease, as chicken cholera, liver coccidia, dog
mange; also destruction by electricity; likewise the
introduction of natural enemies, including ferrets,
stoats, weasels, Siberian sables, grisons, civet cats,
lynxes, foxes, jackals, eoyottes, raptorial birds and the
larger snakes. Some indigenous beasts (Tasmanian
tigers and devils, native cats, dingoes, eaglehawks
and iguanas) were even paraded as saviours; but
lions, tigers and wolves were overlooked. Billjim
the cocky and Jimbill the rabbiter may alike bless
the caution of the Commission that kept this
menagerie at bay.
“Bareoo”: Baix-oo rot and Belyando spew
received their names from the two rivers on which,
in the early days of settlement, the troubles were
most common. When the rot was baptised, 35 years
ago, fever and ague were very prevalent also, but
the stocking of the country eradicated those plagues,
and it is doing the same thing now in the Territory
and Nor’-West Westralia. There is 85 to 90 per cent,
less rot in Queensland than there was 25 years ago.
Curiously', dark-skinned people are practically im-
mune, Ringhi being wholly so. The spew is always
with us, more particularly in prolific years when
the herbage and grass are luxuriant, and consequently
the common fly is numerous. It is raging now.
All hands in the camp where this is written have
sudden attacks, except three to whom sugar is an
abomination. They are not troubled.

THE BRIDGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gimme a bridge with mosses grown,
Of good red-gum or old blue-stone.
Some blokes prefers a ’oiler log
Accommodatin’ man ’n’ dog;
Some camp fer choice behind a fence;
Some flings their drum with little sense
Among the scrub along a ridge.
Gimme a bridge.
A bridge is best in rain or sun,
’N’ well do I remember one—
A bush-built bridge, a single span
Across a creek. There’s where a man
Could get his bath when night had fled
By turnin’ over in his bed ;
Fruit rip’nin’ east, ’n’ onions west—
A bridge is best.
A frien’ly cow, whose sloppy nose
Disturbed me innereent repose,
Gave milk to me for turnips white
I pinched from Carter over-night.
I took whey with the spuds I got
From Hogan’s forty-acre plot.
Fond reckerlection circles now
That frien’ly cow.
’Ad 1 the wish for breakfast fine
Of nice fresh fish, I took me line,
’N’ while I lay, ’n’ smoked, ’n’ read
Hooked plump, long blackfish to me bed.
’Ad I for tender duck a whim,
A swarm of ducklin’s came to swim.
Grilled ducklin’ was me evenin’ dish
’Ad I the wish.
Here, as a rule, by day I’d dream,
Tucked comfor’ble along a beam.
Once Carter, drunk ’n’ sleepin’ there,
Dripped fifty shillin’s in me hair.
Them blokes what picnicked ’long the creek
The bridge’s barmy shade would seek,
And put fat bottles in to cool
Here, as a rule.
Gords envied me me paradise,
Me blest existence free from vice.
One night came floods, ’n’ I awoke
On distant hills, wet, ’omeless, broke.
Me bridge was gorn, ’n’ all in vain
I seek the perfect bridge again.
Now, lookin’ back, it’s plain to see
Gords envied me!
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

FARMERS
SYDNEY
Performance Without Parallel.
Shearing Machinery in the Shearing Sheds
throughout Australia has been a marvel even to those who knew the
superior merits of the Cooper Machine.
Both in the work it has done in shearing unusually hard sheep, and in the record it has established for continuous use without
requiring duplicates—it is a performance without parallel in Shearing history.
Hundreds of letters are reaching us from every part of Australia, from the owner of a small 2-stand plant, to the large Contiactoi
who shear hundreds of thousands of sheep annually—testifying to the wonderful performance of Cooper Shearing machinery.
Messrs. It. A. ROONEY & CO., Contractors, of Stamford (Q’land) write: “We have just cut out at our shearing depot at Stamford,
and Mr. Goodwin advises me that the machines are a great success—he did not open one of the handpieces during the entire shearing.
Messrs. ERNEST AUSBURN & CO., Contractors, of Walgett (N.S.W.), write: “At the conclusion of another season we are more firmly
convinced that the upkeep of the Cooper machines are 75% below others.” Mr. GEORGE JAMES, of Victoria, writes : On two occasions
I shore 75 sheep, and on another 79, without changing my comb and cutter; any machine that runs as smooth and light as that will do
me.” -Mr. WM. GREEN writes from Woodstock: “Five of the machines shore 8500 sheep here without changing a single cutter pm At
Sandy Camp we shore 55.000 sheep, and at one time the expert sat on a kerosene-tin for eight days without a machine being brought to
him for attention. The Cooper is certainly a wonderful, efficient, and economical machine.”

This sectional view of the Cooper Air-cooled Handpiece illustrates its wonderfully practical and simple construction.
THE FORK is balanced on a centre bearing at point B, and driven from behind the bearing at point E, with an easy, free,
light-running motion. The Cooper Air-cooled LID and TENSION PIN is one of the greatest improvements ever made in a
handpiece. The top of the Tension Pin rests on a fixed bearing at point A, immediately over the fork bearing at point B, and
both fork and pin at point C. move back and forth in an easy sweeping motion on the crane principle, ensuring constant
pressure on the cutter in all positions. No tension-nnt bushings are used; tension -nut expanders and adjusters are never required.
The short, well-balanced driving spindle (F) runs through a short, one-piece bush (H), reducing both wear and heat to a minimum never found possible
with old-style machines carrying a spindle and bushing twice the size of that used in the Cooper.
The above are only a few of the sterling features of construction of the Cooper machines, which ar e influencing a great multitude of wool-growers and
contractors throughout Australia to fit their sheds with Cooper product.
If you have sheep to shear, and not the machines, you will require them in 1918. Now is the time to order! If immediate shipment is not desired, the
plant can be reserved until required. Let us send you our catalogue and full particulars, by return mail.

“E.S.S.”: Our old friend the gohanna, or
lace lizard, now being turned to commercial use, is
a hard doer. I don’t think there is anj' lizard more
tenacious of life. The heart will beat for hours after
THE GEOMETRY OF LIFE.
“Ah, my dear, it’s money makes the world go
round."
“Yes, but some of it prefers to remain
square."
the reptile has been killed ; it will beat for a con-
siderable time after it has been taken out of the
body and cut in halves. At the beginning of winter
Kojurrie buries himself deep in the ground, where he
remains until spring, the thick folds of fat he
retires with keeping his strength up during the
period of hibernation. The female is a prolific egg-
layer. She digs a small hole in a sunny spot, covers
the eggs and leaves them to be incubated by the
warmth of the ground. The oil of the beast has long
been in use among bushmen, but it has only lately
been put on the market. Besides its curative pro-
perties it is excellent for machinery—for any delicate
mechanism requiring a clear, non-sticky lubricant.
As Mrs. Kojurrie lays abundantly in captivity and
is no trouble to look after, a gohanna farm ought to
be a paying proposition.
* * * *
“J.A.C.”: At Southport (Tas.) two years
ago each cow of dairyman Joe Hardy’s whole herd
unexplainably went dry within six weeks of calving.
Their health didn’t seem impaired, and the vets,
scratched in vain for a reason. The herbage was
searched and analysed without giving a clue. The
miracle was repeated the following Spring, and the
boss was about to sell up when discovery was made
that the beasts had acquired the chewing craze.
Instead of being satisfied with cocksfoot and daisies,
they spent much of their time at the beach-end of
the run chewing the leather-like stems of the bull-
kelp. They thus imbibed sufficient iodine to cause
their milk to dry off and a large percentage of them
did not prove in calf the following season. There
was a somewhat similar case in California. A trot-
ting mare had developed an allegedly incurable swell-
ing in the thyroid gland and was retired from the
track. Being turned out on a sea-coast pasture to
rear a foal she became addicted to kelp-chewing, and
this reduced the goitre sufficiently to allow of her
being again raced.
* * * *
“Try Fluke”: Mata-hari, the Jappy name
of the too-adventurous lady who was executed in
France for espionage, has, I see, been translated as
“Sunny Eyes” or something like that. It’s curious
that in Maori Mata-hari means pretty much the
same thing. Mata is eye, and hari is happy or
joyful. The coincidence is something for philologists
to brush their brains upon. There are lots of sur-
face likenesses between Jap and Maori talk, but
in the vast majority of cases I imagine the analogy
and resemblance end there. “Toko” and “maru,”
for example, are familiar words in both tongues, but
the meanings are totally different.
“D.F.”: Queer stories are told of the
origin of some of the names in North-eastern Vic-
toria. Katamatite, near Dookie, is said to have
been originally the noise made by a soused inhabi-
tant in trying to say to his wife: “Kate, am I
tight?” Peechelba, on the Ovens, where Morgan
the bushranger was killed, was originally “P.G. —
Bar,” after the first squatter, Peter Gordon, whose
brand was “P.G.” over a bar.
“Tropicus”: A quaint decoration of the
Upper Strickland River (Papua) natives is the coni-
cal hat worn only by
man-killers. It is the
badge of successful murder,
and is greatly prized. The
collection of skulls is the
whole aim of every pro-
perly-developed male, and
the sons add their little
lot to the family trophies,
until the pile becomes of
surprising proportions. The
meat made available is
utilised in the only rational
way (from the savage's
standpoint). The hat is
very like the dunce’s cap.
It is constructed of reeds
plaited on a foundation of
split rattan-cane, and is
glued to the dome with a
mixture of gums and
ochre. Once invested with
the cadey the gentle canni-
bal struts around under it
until it falls to pieces
from old age, when an-
other one is prepared and
sealed to the head with due
ceremony. The owner also
wraps himself in a curiass
to resist the pointed refer-
ences of his peevish breth-
ren. This is made of cane
studded with innumerable
seeds of the grass known
to true believers as
“Job’s Tears.” It serves
its purpose very well, and
helps to prolong the
wearer’s life, until some-
body catches him without
it, when he goes to the
oven in the natural course
of events. Another pleas-
ing custom of these people
is the wearing of dried
hands and other selected
bits of deceased humans, a
fashion item considered very saucy by the knuts.

215 ; Wipere, 210 ; Major, 206 ; Teepa, 205 ; Noanoa,
200; Temple, 181. Total, 2703 sheep for 12 men;
average, 225£ per man. This performance was put
up at Loisel’s Waihau station, Poverty Bay district
(M.L.), in December. The sheep shorn were 4-tooth
and full-mouth ewes, and the actual working time
was nine hours. The runs were from 5 a.m. to 7
a.m., 8 a.m. to 10 a.m., 10.30 a.m. to noon, 1 p.m.
to 3 p.m., 3.30 p.m. to 5 p.m. Te Maori rikes te
plenty tucker when he is shearing. Hawira is an
exceptionally clean and fast barber, and is usually
the ringer. Manahi is a youth of about 20, and this
is his second season. The shearing rate this season
is 275. 6d. per 100 and food.
* * * *
“J.G.”: Re the sudden death of a Gipps-
land kangaroo, as related by “G.J.H.” (B. 27/12/’l7).
I know of two other animals snuffing out apparently
from heart failure due to fright. A few months
back a big hare was pruning the young trees in my
orchard, but as puss was not doing the job accord-
ing to textbook 1 leaped for her. She bolted down
the wire-netted garden and attempted to jump the
fence, but failed. She thereupon made a frantic
and useless effort to scramble through the mesh.
Then I was amazed to see the beast fall over and
kick its last. In a few seconds it was as dead as
pork. Another time I set a rabbit-trap in a hole
in the same fence and one evening I heard bunny
squeal. From the time I heard the cry till I
reached the trap was less than a minute, nevertheless
the intruder was dead. Yet it was hardly marked
where the trap had caught it.
“Huffin-Guffin”: Shearing records, is it?
Charley Haylock, of Harrow (Western Vic.), has
recently cut out his final shed for the season. It
is the fifty-second time he has done it —and never
a break. Aged about 75, the father of about 12
Australians and grand-dad to—well, ask him your-
self. He’ll tell you the tally proudly.
* * * *
“Leo Lear”: Yes, mallee hens’ eggs are
A 1 when not too ripe and the hen is obliging
enough to point out the most unappetising units in
her collection. Every morning she opens up the
mound to keep the sand friable or air the blankets,
and in doing so scratches the soft outer shell of the
eggs, which are placed large end up in a circle.
Thus the fewer the scratches the fresher the eggs,
and vice versa. A sanctuary for this wonderful
mound-builder has been found in the thickets of
Kangaroo Island (S.A.), and according to latest
reports the birds liberated there are thriving
wonderfully. Gorblessem, for on the mainland they
are doomed to early extinction.
“W.A.C.”: Wouldn’t accept “Barcoo’s”
invitation to afternoon tea if he made it with the
water that eucalyptus-leaves have been floating on
(B. 17/1/’IS). Anyhow, two crossed pine battens
prevent the water in a kerosene-tin spilling, and
leave the liquid sweet.
“K.”: You can have the rest of the bush
opera co. if you give me one magpie carolling or
yodelling on a fence in the early morning, I heard
Maggie’s beautifully liquid call first when I was
seven years young, and it has meant youth and per-
fect happiness to me ever since. A N. S. Wales
country storekeeper’s tame mag. welcomed me at
the door last month with a chirp and a hop, and I
asked the bosS if the bird still made the matchless
wild music. “Only when there’s a stiff wind blow-
ing,” he replied. “Then he dodges round the corner
into shelter and performs.”

“A.G.” : I often wondered why Bluey never
joined us when we raided our khaki underwear. One
day I stalked him and saw him peel off his shirt and
hang it on a fence. Then he retired and in about
two minutes a flock of starlings swooped down and
lunched.
* * * *
“Katharos”: Charles Darwin in his
“Expressions of the Emotions in Man and Animals”
mentioned several interesting examples of expressions
of shame in monkeys. Some time ago I saw
something which very clearly showed how intense
the shame-feeling is in dogs. A number of them
were playing together, running at full speed along
the bank of a lake, when one slipped and fell into
the water. A good few people on the spot applauded
Ponto with a burst of laughter when he agiin
appeared on the bank. Disconcerted or something by
the derision, instead of joining his playmates near by
he stuck his tail between his legs and, with scowling
side glances, slunk away.
“D.F.”: This is a Riverina man’s cure
for mosquitoes. Shovel a kerosene-tin half-full of saw-
dust from the Murray pine, set it on fire and put it
near the bed. It will smoulder away all night and
scare the mosquitoes, while the smell is soothing
and sleep-provoking to the man on the bed.

don’t you take some hot water —do just

McKENZIE: “Thanks verra much. I’ll go
home and try it."
THE VOICES OF THE RAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last night, when under troubled skies
The storm went marching o'er the plain,
An elfin music seemed to rise,
A singing in the rain.
At first it seemed a prattling child
That played alone in young delight,
And then it seemed a joy gone wild
That sang unto the night.
The raindrops with their steady beat
That set the iron roof aflow
Were like a thousand little feet
That hurried to and fro.
And where the runnels gushed and streamed
And soaked the grass-roots, dry and brown,
A busy band of fairies seemed
To patter up and down.
The air was full of whisperings,
And all the teeming dark was rife
With stir and call that told of things
That woke anew to life.
And then across the darkened waste
A sudden shouting wind was hurled;
It seemed a messenger in haste
With tidings for the world.
And till across that streaming scene
The wind went rushing down the plain,
I did not guess what they might mean —
Those voices of the rain.
They said: “Farewell to drought and dearth,
To Famine, hollow-eyed and nude!”
They said; “We are the teeming Earth,
The gift of plenitude!”
They said, those voices of the rain:
“We are the flesh and blood and breath;
We are the meat, the fruit, the grain
That succor all from death l”
They said: “Wherever we may pass
The hour of plenty comes to birth ;
We spread the banquet of the grass
Around about the Earth!
“We call,” they said, “and 10, the seed
Within its mother-soil is stirred;
The seasons round ’tis ours to feed
The fruit, the tree, the bird.
“By us the petal is unfurled,
The rose in damask splendor blooms;
We fill the markets of the world,
And feed its hungry looms.”
The moon sent forth one silver ray.
The fairy voices ceased to sing;
Yet far away, and far away,
I heard thanks echoing.
For cattle lowed throughout the night
In deep content across the plain ;
And I, too, thanked, with meet delight,
The voices of the rain.
N.S.W.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Trav.”: There are bushrangers still in
the country. This Ned Kelly insured his
brood mare through the local agent at
Swamp Gum. Two days later the mare
foaled safely. Kelly had just killed another
neddy and burnt it, so he put in a claim
for the loss of the mare, which the agent
had never seen. His explanation of the
cremation was that the corpse was a stand-
ing invitation to all the flies of the district.
The claim went in; but the policy not hav-
ing yet been issued, the co. pleaded not
liable. But Kelly ends up with a satisfied
grin, “I got me three quid back again.”
* *
“0.K.” supplies some more evidence that
Australia can do things when it wants to: —
Material for an interesting study in industrial
development is supplied by the Dunlop Rubber Co.’s
business in Australia. There was a Dunlop factoiv
in Melbourne nearly 20 years ago, but the imported
materials were merely “assembled” on the premises.
Later, an organiser named Kearns came from
America, bringing three foremen with him, and
then a proper start was made with the works v. hich
to-day cover seven acres and find employment, for
1500 hands. Kearns, by the way, is said to have
been a warm admirer of the Australian workman.
He reckoned him the most intelligent and alert
type he ever had to do with. The problem in those
early days was to perfect the quality of the
material; and when, some five or six years ago, S.
F. Roberts brought his American and Scotch labora-
tory experience to bear on the business, the problem
was still how to make Australian-manuiactu'red
rubber more valuable for local use than any that
could ever be dumped here. Evidently lie solved
the problem, for the Dunlop conveyor-belting has
driven imported rivals from the field and ahead}
Australia is a pretty large field. The other day the
co. supplied the Broken Hill Prop, with two lengths
that weighed 4* and tons respectively. All done
by experimenting and persistent testing. If as
much thought, patience and scientific knowledge had
been put into the Australian woollen industry as is
put into rubber-making the manufacture of this
country’s staple product would not have progressed
so lamely all these years. Crude rubber has hardly
begun to be a Commonwealth product yet. But it is
coming along nicely. Meanwhile the Dunlop Co.
imports 70 tons a month from Ceylon and the
Amazon.
To the average citizen “Dunlop ’ suggests only
tyres; but more than 1000 different sorts of rubber
goods are produced. Things that were formerly
made of some other material are now manufactured
from the milky ooze of trees, or with its aid. A
new product whereof manager Roberts seems
parentally proud is the “Kromhyd” rubber sole for
boots —a complete sole of fibrous compound. Rubber
and rubberised materials are waging commercial war
against leather all the time. As for rubber boot-
heels, the output has already touched 3$ tons in a
week. Some of the highest-grade material' produced
in the factory is shaped into cups for milking
machines; the cups need sterilising after each milk-
ing, and only the finest grade will stand that.
Rollers for wringing-machines are mighty solid-
looking goods; likewise the rubber stair-treads
which are supplanting the old slippery metal bind-
ings that have caused many a man to break his
neck. Rubber floorings are being laid where lino-
leum once served. In the home, the warehouse, the
factory, the garden, the motor-car, the tennis-court,
the bowling green—rubber is everywhere: on the
bottle of soda man mixes with the whisky, and on
the umbrella he unfurls when the rain is falling on
his rubbered coat. Yet it is well within the
memory of very young men when none of these
things were manufactured in Australia, and when,
moreover, the worthy fathers of the young men
mostly declared that such things never would or
could be satisfactorily manufactured in Australia,
owing to the climate or the Labor party, or some
other old thing.

“Shopstrutter”: I am aware of a little
cashier girl who sits perched in a tangle of
overhead wires deftly receiving and despatch-
ing those wooden pigeons that whir to and
fro tilled with bills and cash. During one
day lately she safely handled £3OO from one
department alone. Her weekly wage is 195.,
and if she asked for another bob or two the
shade of departed Chiffon would fall on the
impalpable shirtfront of dead-and-gone Cre-
tonne, and their frockcoated successors would
retire into their private offices and shake
their heads sadly.
» * * *
The charge of a hungry shark is nothing
to the rush of a hovering police court soli-
citor. When a eullud lady appeared at “the
Central,” Sydney, on a charge of selling beer
without a license, one of the practised police-
courtiers popped up and said briskly, “I
appear for the defendant, your Worship, and
ask for a remand.” By this time the police
inspector was on his feet pointing out that
the lady had not yet pleaded. “She pleads
not guilty,” retorted the prompt Gent. One,
and, the magistrate remanding her till the
following Wednesday, she was taken out.
Five minutes later, Gent. One having mean-
while left the court, the lady was brought
back by the inspector, who explained that
she insisted on pleading guilty and would
have done so before but for the Gent. One
“butting in.” At this stage the Gent. One
arrived back breathless to cast a fog of
explanation over the incident. “I was
engaged outside to appear for this woman
and plead not guilty for her, and I asked
her here ‘How are you going to plead?’ and
she said ‘Not guilty.’ ” The inspector then
wounded his feelings by saying, “You didn’t
ask her!” and the weary beak closed a bitter
debate by ordering the Gent. One to sit
down. Perhaps it hurts that sort of Gent.
One to sit down.
# * * •
“Sr”: Eaglehawk (Vie.) is being taken _to
pieces and scattered over the map of Vic-
toria. If you have any excuse for living in
old Bendigo’s suburb you can hire a five-
roomed house for three bob a week, or buy
the whole outfit for 50. or 00 quid. The
homes of the “ducked” miners are being
purchased at bargain prices by the enter-
prising and carted all over the country
and re-erected. The best way to shift
a house is to call in the driver of a
traction engine and big hauling carriage at
a fiver a day. For longer trips the practice
is to take the house down in sections, dump
it in a truck and rail the lot. As
most of the homes of old Eaelehawk are
wooden, another 20 years ought to see the
mining township in the In Memoriam
column.
* * * *
“Melb”: The production of mineral wool
from bluestone at Yarraville (Vic.) goes on
merrily. It is found to be good stuff for
deadening sound in buildings, insulating cool
stores, clothing boilers, and so on. It was
discovered by chayice, like lots of other
things. At Cleveland Ironworks a smelting
furnace broke down. Steam came in con-
tact with molten slag, and there was pro-
duced a white hairy substance, not unlike
short-fibred wool. The sulphur in slag was,
however, a drawback, so granite and basalt
were substituted. In its natural state basalt
is worth about 3s. 6d. a ton ; make it wool
and it is worth £l4.

“O’Phimertv”: The Vic. train mentioned
by “D.S.” (B. 27/12/TT) as the Australian
champion for want of speed is easily beaten
in Tassy. The Tullah express, which branches?
from the Burnie-Zeehan line at Mount
Farrell, cuts out seven miles in two and a
half hours when it has good luck. When it
hasn’t and the only carriage hops off the
line, the timetable is lengthened as required.

“The Slug”: All the thieves are not in
gaol. A Melbourne market-gardener sent
to an agent a large crate of cabbages, in
sroud condition, and received one-and-tup-
penee in reply, although the lowest market
price at the time was 4s. a dozen. It never
seems to occur to the farmer to throw in a
bob or two with his neighbors, employ a
detective and pinch the thieving agent. There
is a law to protect even the cabbage-grower
if he cares to invoke it. The same sort of
sharking, if it were detected in Stock
Exchange dealings, for instance, would have
pretty serious results for the guilty share-
broker ; and it should be as easy for the
farmer to straighten the agent if he would
only get up and face his enemy like a man.
* * * *
“Peanut”: I visited Captain Kidd, the
bootmaker, again to-day. About 18 months
ago he sold me a pair of shoes for 20s.
Three months later the same line had risen
to 235. 6d., and to-day the things cost 355.!
Though pretty credulous, I refuse to believe
the cost of production for these Australian-
made goods has increased 75 per cent, in 18
months. Here’s the inner history of a
parcel of stockings. They were purchased
in England by our firm’s buyer, and were
sold before arrival to a wholesale Sydney
house at 100 ner cent, on oiir invoice cost.
They passed along the line to one of our
toniest frill shops at. another 100 per cent,
advance on our cost price. The stockings
are now on view, and you can buy them at
a price equal to three times the original
cost; and that included manufacturer’s pro-
fit. Yes, this is a terrible w-ar, and we must
all put our shoulders to the wheel!
