K.
The Inky Way.
“T. 0.1.”: Australia’s first little teetotal
rag was the Temperance Magazine, pub-
lished from the S. M. Herald office,
then in George-street. It was born on
July 1, 1837. I have the first 12 numbers.
No. 1 contains a list of subscribers. Amongst
them are some who dealt largely in liquors—
A. B. Spark (who also had a cork leg),
J, T. Smith (then in Sydney, later inn-
keeper and theatre-proprietor, and seven
times Mayor of Melbourne), T. W. Smart.
Sam Terry, the Lords, John Tawell (who
emptied a quantity of liquor into Sydney
Cove, and was later hanged in England for
murder). The motto of the magazine was
“Temperance is moderation in things inno-
cent and abstinence from things hurtful.”
It seems to have been backed by a society
whose members boosted the circulation to
4000 by mostly taking 10 copies each. Gov.
Ginns was one of its supporters. In the
issue for May, 1838, the stockholders of the
Southern District pledged themselves not to
pay wages in spirituous liquors to any person
belonging to their establishments. E. P. S.
Sturt headed the list—he was subsequently
Police Magistrate in Melbourne —and 20
others, chiefly of the Goulburn country, fol-
lowed him.
* * * *
“8.5.”: In reply to “B. J.” (B. 24/1/T8)
“The Psalm of Time” is included in “The
Song of the Stars and Other Poems,” by
Alpha Crucis, published by Cassells and
George Robertson in 1882. The author,
Robert Dudley Adams, was a Custom House
agent in Sydney and a friend of old Samuel
Bennett of the Evening News. Bennett
knew very little about poetry, but he
printed slabs of Tennysonian verse by
Adams, whom he liked. It is therefore
probable that Heaton’s father-in-law is
responsible for the absurd opinion of “The
Psalm of Time” quoted in Heaton’s olla
podrida, “The Australian Dictionary of
Dates.”
* * * *
“Kettledrum”: Ernestine Hammings, the
austere-looking young poetess from Banana-
land, has been spending a holiday in Mel-
bourne under mamma’s eye. A scribbler at
10, she began to write printable verse at
sixteen, and was discovered by that smart'
little Scot, McAr-r-r-dle, then of Brisbane
Advocate and now of Melbourne Herald,
a son-in-law of De Largie. He induced her
to bring out her verse in book-form, and it
yielded her £7O. Now 18, and with her hair
still down her back, Ernestine has been
admired by several press people to whom
her shyness and simplicity appeal. She has
made good in The Bulletin, a “Nocturne”
having headed the Queensland page, and her
work has been reviewed on the Red Page.
In work-a-day hours she is in charge of the
library in the Queensland Department of
Justice, so she is a northern parallel to our
own Barney O’Dowd.
Frank Parnall, for whom the black camel
called at Prospect (S.A.) the other day after
years of painful illness, originally came to this
country from the U.S.A., and was reckoned
one of the most scholarly of Anglican par-
sons in Australia. Incurable disease had
long kept him from active work, but
till the last week or two he hobbled pain-
fully along the inky track as leader-writer
and reviewer for the Anglican Church
Standard.

“Chevron”: Returning to Australia, in-
valided, after doing his bit for nearly three
years, The Bulletin’s old contributor
“Umfaan,” otherwise Lieut. George Lee,
who, leaving with the Light Horse, was
transferred to the Camel Corps in Egypt,
and was with the Artillery in some French
stunts which were tres montarde. Born in
England, he is a S’African war veteran, and,
after a mixed experience of freelance jour-
nalism, joined Melbourne Herald staff in
1014. His wife and two little sons will be
glad to have him back.
# * * *
A curious relic is to be kept henceforth
in the Register office in Adelaide. E. W.
Webb was best known as an Advertiser man
in later life, and he was editor of its weekly,
the Chronicle, when he died. But he
started in the Grenfell-street office, and a
silver watch was given him “by the Register
companionship, as a mark of their esteem.
25 December, 1858.” The part bearing the
inscription has just been dug up in a sub-
urban garden, and sent back to where it
started from GO years ago. An odd sequel
is a letter to the paper from a relative ot
Mr. Webb, who says that the watch was
left to her. and was lately stolen, though
she didn’t know it till she read about the
discovery. However, she is quite content
that what is left of it should find a home in
the Register “chapel.”
MY PAPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My paper hates the little thief.
Its remedy too rash is—
It prints a most profound belief
In flve-and-flfty lashes.
I scan the sixth day’s supplement
With pompous writings swollen,
And find when half an hour is spent
That fully sixty-five per cent.
Of what is there is stolen.
Deceitful rascals who in town
Assail the folks bucolic
With subtle tricks to take them down,
In sermons apostolic
It whips with scorpions.. I turn,
Suffused with virtuous feeling,
Its thoughts on politics to learn—
And in the leaded lines discern
A specious kind of spieling.
More just than Solomon, more stern,
It deals out castigation
To men who tainted money earn
By vile adulteration.
Lies, prejudice, and all deceit
And spite that’s never ended
Alloy its policy effete.
My paper is a goodly sheet —
Its principles are splendid!
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

TENDERS.
For the Removal of Rock in the River
Tamar.
THE MARINE BOARD OF LAUNCES-
TON, TASMANIA, is prepared to
receive tenders for the Removal of portion
of the Porpoise Rock in the Estuary of the
River Tamar, Tasmania.
Specifications, Forms of Tender and Con-
ditions of Contract may be had from the
undersigned upon application on and after
the Ist day of February, such applications
to be accompanied by a cash deposit of
£3 35., which amount will be returned upon
receipt of a bona-fide tender.
Drawings may be inspected by intending
Contractors at the office of the MARINE
BOARD ENGINEER, and copies of same
may be obtained upon payment of one guinea
for the set.
Tenders, endorsed “PORPOISE ROCK”
and addressed to the Master Warden, Marine
Board, Launceston, Tasmania, must reach
this office not later than noon on the 3rd
day of June, 1918.
GEO. S. MEREDITH.
Secretary.
Marine Board Offices,
Launceston, Tas.

Anaemia
Our blood is composed of
red and white corpuscles
the red to nourish the body,
the white to fight disease. In
Anaemia—or bloodlessness—
the red corpuscles are more
or less deficient. Thus the
blood cannot provide suffi-
cient nourishment for the
body. Therefore the face
becomes white and “ pasty ”
—the eyes become dull and
“heavy”—and a feeling of
intense weariness pervades
the whole system. To over-
come Anaemia, the blood
supply needs recharging with
red corpuscles. And it is
here that
m

‘ Wincarnis.'

DYSPEPTICS SHOULD DRINK MORE
HOT WATER.
When food lies like lead in the stomach,
and you have that uncomfortable, distended
feeling, it is because of insufficient blood
supply to the stomach, combined with acid
and food fermentation. In such cases try
the plan now followed in many hospitals
and advised by eminent specialists of taking
half a teaspoonful of bisurated magnesia in
half a glass of water as hot as you can
comfortably drink it. The hot water draws
the blood to the stomach, and the bisurated
magnesia, as any physician or chemist can
tell you, instantly neutralises the acid and
stops the food fermentation. Try this
simple plan and you will be astonished at
the immediate feeling of relief and comfort
that always follows the restoration of
the normal process of digestion.. But be
sure you ask the chemist very distinctly for
bisurated magnesia, thus avoiding confusion
with the sulphates, oxides and citrates or
bismith and magnesia mixtures which are
often unsuitable. Soldiers at the Front and
travellers who are frequently obliged to take
hasty meals poorly prepared should always
take two or three five-grain tablets of
bisurated magnesia after meals to prevent
fermentation and neutralise the acid. — Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
How about calling it the Ministry of
Small Stinks?
TEE OPIUM-SMOKER.
“The Paris correspondent of HUMANITE, who
recently saw the German Emperor in Berlin, says
the military clique has convinced him that Ger-
many’s situation is excellent. The Emperor believes
he will still march into London. The acceptance
of peace pourparlers by the Bolsheviks has brought
back his old dreams. "— Cable.
N. S. Wales has millions of the loan
curse coming uome to roost later in the
year; and under advice that things will be
worse before they are better the Government
is already inviting holders, practically all
outside Australia, to take 5| per cent, in-
terest instead of the old per cent, and
call it square for the time. This will mean
an addition of nearly £300,000 a year to the
interest bill on this one transaction alone.
As the “permanent and reproductive works,”
including the Jenolan Caves House crockery
and sundries, won’t earn a farthing more
simply because the rate of interest has gone
up, this means £300,000 a year more State
taxation; and there is another £55,000.000
of debt carrying 31 per cent, or less coming
due mostly within a very few years roughly
£1,250,000 more interest to pay if it all has
to be “renewed” on the present terms. Maybe
it won't all have to be renewed on those
terms; but interest isn't going to be low for
many years to come, and the State's advisers
in London are probably right in assuming
that it will soar up to 6 per cent, and past
that before it takes a turn the other way.
Still the Government goes on cheerfully pil-
ing the debt higher on the very edge of the
precipice.
Sample of the official explanation of the
last referendum as conveyed to the men in
the trenches and in training: —
Repatriation.
£22,000,000 is to be devoted to settling soldiers
on the land and another £10,000,000 on other forms
of repatriation. Now it’s up to you to see that the
slackers in Australia do come, so that you, for whom
these huge sums are to be expended, may get back
to Australia in due course and participate in the
benefits.
No wonder an indignant officer wrote asking
whether they were not being mistaken for a
gang of bushrangers!
It is 1!) years since a censure or no-confi-
dence motion was carried in the N. S. W ales
Assembly. In the circumstances Parlia-
ments of 80 or 100 members are an expen-
sive absurdity. They don’t bring 80 or 100
brains to the business of the country. They
only bring 80 or 100 recortang machines;
and the country can’t stand tne ridiculous
expense.
Recent semi-official announcement: —
The disconcerting discovery has now been made
that many of the cargoes which have left Australia,
particularly for the East, have been filtering through
to Germany, and in consequence of this leakage the
Commonwealth Government is now considering the
whole question of the exportation of shipments which
these shores. It is probable that an important
decision will be arrived at shortly.
But what could you expect '! The public has
persistently been denied any revelations that
would awaken it to consciousness of German
and pro-German agencies and influences.
Australia hears nothing, sees nothing, sus-
pects nothing.

Argus still believes
that payment for Par-
liamentary services is
undesirable. Because
“some members who
have lost their seats
have not been able to
save anything, and
have immediately
fallen into poverty,”
gran’ma sees in that

This is the babble of

Not only were things so managed that
nothing came of the Broken Hill Prop.’s
offer, made last July, to at once build four
5000 tons ships; but, according to N.S.W.
Premier Holman, speaking at the end of
January, this also happened :
On July 27 we received a letter from the Prime
Minister, asking that arrangements be made to pre-
pare slips for as many vessels as possible, the
dimensions of the vessels being 300 ft. x 46ft. x 26ft.,
and requesting information as to how many slips
could be so prepared. We forwarded on July 31 the
information thus requested, and from that day to
this we have never heard another word about it.
This, added to the failure of the Government
to do anything with the Broken Hill Prop.’s
offer, is too serious a matter to be dealt
with in a party spirit. The National Par-
liament is above Ministers, and it is Parlia-
ment’s duty, if ever it had one, to sift this
matter to the bottom and find out who was
responsible.
The Federal Prime Minister has invited
all the State Premiers except one to meet
him to consider “the whole question of coal
supply and distribution.” The exception is
Ryan, of Queensland, who represents the
greatest coal-producing State bar N. S.
Wales. Why is Ryan left out? Is that egg
of Warwick going to burst up the Federa-
tion?
All parties in South Aus.
have decided to put behind
them the temptation to re-
tain their jobs without ask-
ing the country whether it is
satisfied or not. Thus the
elections will take place at
the proper date, which is
next April. The result
must depend very much on
whether the Labor party
will have to take on its
opponents separately or
together. Liberals and
“Nationals” are holding fre-
quent meetings and have
come to the conclusion, in
most cases, that they are
prepared to swallow one
another rather than be
swallowed. Some few, how-
ever, refuse to be anybody
but themselves. Among these
is Clarence Goode, lately of
the Vaughan Ministry, who
intends to play a lone hand
for the present. Another
complication has been
brought in by the supposed
promise of a safe seat to
young Captain Blackburn,
V.C., as a direct representa-
tive of returned soldiers.
Smeaton has found his con-
science requiring him to give
up his own fairly safe seat
in favor of Blackburn, but
he will probably be accom-
modated elsewhere.
S. Australia is busy trying
to draft itself into two
political mobs: those who
are not Labor are all trying
to get into one yard, and
people who think they are
quite honest are doing their
best to jamb them in. Yet
there would be revolution in S. Australia
for certain if somebody tried to compel the
whole population to be, say, either Baptist
or Roman Carth’lic.

The promised, or threatened, Judges Re-
tirement Bill is not included in the business
paper of the present N. S. Wales State
session. Perhaps its most pressing work was
done even before Parliament gave it birth.
A. H. Simpson having at last stood down, the
N. S. Wales Supreme Court Bench is now
almost youthful. Three of the District Court
Bench--Docker, Fitzhardinge and Industrial
Judge Heydon—-are in the 70’s, but none of
them shows any particular signs of decrepi-
tude. Murray and Rogers, who retired last
year, were both well over 70.
PIONEERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
America has constructed docks and built 600 miles
of railway in France. Foresters were sent and saw-
mills erected and even corn planted to feed the troops.
—Statement of U.S. War Minister.
With plough and hammer and axe and saw
The pioneers cross the sea,
Where the legions of liberty slowly draw
To the place of victory.
Oh, make the railways and sow the corn,
For thus is the strength of an army born!
Lightnings shall leap from the rending guns
And the bayonets redly gleam ;
And the world shall reel to the fire that runs
And quake to the bolts that scream ;
But engines and trucks and a port to land
Are the first great need of the iron hand.
The axes that ring on the storied oak,
The furrows in brown earth free
Are part of the furbished battle-stroke
That leaps from the western sea ;
And tl|e harvest white of an August morn
Shall laugh with plenty the foe to scorn.
Westward ho! when the seas were young
Their fathers pressed their sail;
But eastward ho 1 is their war-strength flung
Lest the shames of the years prevail;
And the pioneers of the old advance
Swing back in their splendid strength to France.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
WE SHALL SEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Before long we shall see, not one or two or four
ships, but, in all probability, 10 or a dozen ships
actually laid down in various parts of Australia.”
—Joe Cook.
Before long we shall see —Ah, what shall we not?
The hammers at work upon rivet and bolt:
For there conies a swift end to our present dry-rot
And we’re leaping ahead with a cheer and a jolt.
We shall see, we shall see, and it won’t be too long
Ere there’s more than the wag of a jaw in the song!
Before long we shall see! It is rumored abroad
That the win-the-war party will shortly begin
To do something that plain honest folk can applaud
Beyond screaming their angry intention to win.
We shall see, we shall see, that Joe Cook and Bill
Hughes
Can contrive something better than censoring news.
Before long we shall see! There is hope in that
word,
For seeing’s believing, as all the world knows;
And hearing in cases like this is absurd,
However the hot wind of- rhetoric blows.
We shall see, we shall see! It is faith’s final test;
But we watch, and our eyes are as good as the best.
Before long we shall see! Well, it’s cheering to know
That the curtain at last on the drama will rise;
And to have the emphatic assurance of Joe
That the size of the job will delight and surprise.
We shall see, we shall see! And we linger in hope
On an island of doubt in an ocean of dope.
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
In the weights for the Newmarket and
Australian Cup each list, begins with an imported
nag—Desert Gold with 9st. 131 b. in the sprint and
Camus 9st. 81b. in the long way. Speaking gener-
ally, the lady from Maoriland seems to have been
treated a bit better than the Englishman. He
would certainly have to be a considerably better
horse than he has at any time shown himself in
Australia to be to have much chance with that
weight in the Cup, whereas Desert Gold, big burden
though she lias been allotted, is left with a visible
chance. Whether the mare is to be a starter in
this race is another thing, though this page takes it
that if owner Lowry leaves her in for any time he
means business. She is a big, long-striding animal
and a quick beginner, and if she is given a run this
page would expect to hear of her being hard to beat.
Of the rest Panacre (9st. 21b.), Briseis (Bst. lib.),
Dame Acre (7st. 111 b.) and Princess Winifred (7st.)
read as well at this stage as anything- else. One
queer feature of the Newmarket Handicap is the
circumstance of Blue Bolt being called on to give
weight to Eulacre and Dame Acre. Either of them
could probably give him a good helping and a lead
home. Condition on the day, happenings at the
start, and riding are so much more important in a
race like the Newmarket than weight, that looking-
for the winner a month before on the basis ot
avoirdupois is little more than an exercise in
futility. But running down the list the writer's
eve stopped at Lord Marco, a four-year-old import
with 7st. 81b., and he wondered how the animal may
get on if everything" else is favorable.
The Australian Cup lot include a few
that are infirm and a few others who might be worth
considering in a suburban handicap if the rest were
ordinary. One astonishing thing about the weights
for this event is the continued persistence of the
handicapper in rating old Cisco as if he were still
one of the best. This time he is put within Gib. of
Green Cap, 31b. of Ulva’s Isle, and lib. of Colbert.
In addition he is counted 41b. better than Prince
Bardolph, all of which is pretty rough on Cisco.
Statistics are against the chances of top-
weights in the Australian Cup. Three nags only
since the race’s inception, 55 years ago, have carried
9st. or over to victory. These were Malua (1880).
9st. 91b.; Highborn (1892), 9st. 4lb. ; and Tartan
(1906), 9st. Gib. But seven winners have humped
more than Bst. Gib., and none of the last 15 (bar
Tartan) have carried over Bst. lib.
* * * *
Of 111 nags weighted for this year’s New-
market a couple of dozen are pommies and eight are
Maorilanders. #
On the pony courses round Sydney there
are weeks at •i stretch when the number of times the
good thing’ misses can hardly be mentioned in Israel
without provoking tears. But last Saturday was not
one of the occasions. The fixture at Victoria Park
being the only meeting in the metropolitan area
there was a big crowd on tap, and in nine races the
favorite arrived only twice. In the other seven Mo
hung* to most of what reached him. Many of him
were able to do with it, too.
# * * *
“Argus-Eyed”: At Sandown Park (Vic.)
Blue Toorie, an any-price-you-like winner, beat the
well-backed Sugelo very unexpectedly by a half-head.
Though returned at fifties the nag (a daughter of
Alavva and Belle Blue) was quoted by several ring-
men at double those odds. What was described by
the Argtjs as an “interesting incident” happened to
one of the generous layers. Ho had hooked a sport
a hundred pounds to one, when a well-known pro-
fessional punter (the names of both bookie and
backer were given) inquired the name of the long-
priced outsider. “Blue Toorie, and you’d bettei
have £3OO to £l,” was the reply. The bet was
accepted, the well-known punter drew his money,
and —nerhaps the ad. was worth it.
* * * *
All along this page has insisted that, run
together, the book will heat the tote, and that having
the two institutions on the premises is without even
plausible justification. There are two reasons why
the machine must come out second-best. The first
is that Ike stands to lose something and the machine
can lose nothing. The next is that to the majority
of punters with money the attraction of odds they
can understand is a long way more powerful than the
pull of odds which they cannot even guess at until
everybody else has finished helping themselves. How
it works out can be seen by having a look at the
Anniversary Handicap, run at Randwiek. An invest-
ment of 10s. meant standing to collect this way from
the two places:—
Bookmaker. Machine.
Kennaquhair . . .. £1 12 6 £1 7 G
Hitachi . . . . 2 15 0 2 0 0
Mehi King . . 5 10 0 4 3 0
The Christian Brother 6 10 0 318 G
Cisco .. .. . . G 10 0 40G
Ulva’s Isle . . . . 6 10 0 6 0 6
Chantamerle. . 7 10 0 3 18 0
Lady Liege . . . . 10 10 0 6 6 0
Wedding Day . . 10 10 0 7 G 0
Norbury . . . . 10 10 0 9 2 G
Scoutmaster . . . . 25 10 0 10 4 6
Phast Boy . . ..25 10 0 12 12 0
Dollar Dictator . . 25 10 0 30 18 G
There was a bit over on the machine for payment on
the second horse, but if all the machine money had
gone to backers of the winner it would have worked
out like this: — ~ , .
Bookmaker. Machine.
liennaquhair. . .. £1 12 0 £1 16 6
Hitachi . . .. 215 0 213 6
Mehi King . . . . 5 10 0 511 0
The Christian Brother 6 10 0 5 4 0
Cisco 6 10 0 576
Ulva’s Isle .. .. 6 10 0 8 1 0
Chantamerle.. 7 10 0 5 4 0
Ladv Liege .. . . 10 10 0 8 8 0
Wedding Day . . 10 10 0 9 15 0
Norbury . . ..10 10 0 12 2 6
Scoutmaster . . ..25 10 0 13 13 0
Phast Boy . . ..25 10 0 16 16 0
Dollar Dictator . . 25 10 0 41 4 0
Of course these-figures have no influence upon the
case for the totalisator or against the book. Upon
that controversy the arithmetic of particular events
has little if aiiy bearing. What the calculations on
the Anniversary and contemplation of the kind of
betting done on the machines at Randwick and subur-
ban courses do show is that the sooner the system
now in vogue is changed the better. If Ike is to
remain where he is the case for running the machine
on a 1-2-3 basis, with a single dividend on the three
placed horses, is overwhelming. If he is to go, then
the ease for continuing to do this and running
another tote for dividends on winners only would be
hard to controvert. If the State continues to take
the view that its interest in the matter begins and
ends with getting 7*per cent, from one place and a
ticket tax from the other, thing's will go from bad
to worse.
“Spreader”: In Maoriland they show the
horses’ names on the face of the machine as well as
their corresponding numbers in the racebook. Inci-
dentally they sell proportionately quite as many
racebooks at a meeting over there as are sold at
Randwick, so the fact of withholding the names and
showing only the figures—but what’s the good ! If
the Sydney clubs won’t put an end to the farce it
is time someone else did. Since the 1-2-3 business
started at the ponies half the crowd at the “pay-in”
window gets shut out every race, and only the front
rank of the mob at the “pay out” side get settled
with in time to have a punt on the next.
* * * «
A totalisator coup might have come off
with a little luck at Gosford (N.S.W.) last week.
When the animals had rushed past the judge in the
Welter Handicap, somebody informed the tote that
old Plavinius (an outsider) had won. Whereupon a
div. of £9 19s. 6d. for five bob was hoisted. The
truth was that two prads which paid less than £1
had run a dead-beat, and the outsider wasn’t in a
place. Someone noticed the error, and the machine
hauled down the div. notice just in time. It takes
many eyes to keep a look-out for sharks at some of
these country fixtures.
* * * *
“Dumps”: Ballarat Miners’ racetrack is
to say good-bye to its best-known mark. The mul-
lock-lieap which has monopolised the middle-distance
of the course ever since racing has been known in
the district is to be carted away and used for muni-
cipal purposes. The locals will benefit: they’ll be
able to watch a race from start to finish. Also the
strong-armed jockey, riding to his own “orders,” will
be in full view of the stewards all the way.
* * * * *
Jolm L. Sullivan was only in his sixtieth
year when he took the last count the other day; but
he had been an established institution so long that
some people might have been prepared to hear that
he was almost a hundred. John came into the
business when, quite young. He was a hefty youth
with a paralysing wallop, and by the time he reached
22 was up in the front rank, going through the oppo-
sition like a cyclone. By discovery of the k.o.
punch, he was for nearly 15 years invincible, and he
earned a fame that was world-wide. His two
greatest battles, in the sense of being the longest,
were fought under the brutal old p.r. rules, one with
Charlie Mitchell at Chantilly (France) going 39
rounds, and the other with Jake Kilrain, at New
Orleans, 75 rounds. The last of any importance was
with Jim Corbett, when Sullivan was 34. James
won on the 21st collision. Twenty-odd years ago
John was brought to Australia by the MaeMahons
and appeared in drama. The trip was a fiasco,
mainly because of the impossibility of inducing Sul-
livan not to keep himself full of ink. The stories
of his excesses would fill a book. For many years
he earned an enormous income and fooled it practi-
cally all away. Of late the old man had toiled on
his farm and taken a front seat on the water-
waggon. As a • denunciator of booze and earnest
advocate of sobriety in all things John addressed
himself to the rising generation as one who knew
of what he spoke. He was an Irish-American, born
at Boston, stood sft. lOJin., and was a man of
exceptional vitality and not wholly without character.
There were two men whom John L. dodged getting
into the ring with—Peter Jackson and Frank Slavin.
It is not likely the big fellow was afraid of either,
but he knew enough about J;hem to be certain that
he would have to be at his best, and just then train-
ing was something he had come to think of with
horror. Towards the end he never trained at all, and"
was only induced to meet Corbett through being made
to believe that Janies couldn’t hit hard enough to
hurt. Wherefore he went down—and out. It is a
moral certainty that had he been anywhere near
form Sullivan could have eaten Corbett; but the
younger man’s left stabbed him to pieces, and in
the later rounds John was just a lump of demoralised
blubber. But he took the lacing wonderfully well.
He. turned himself into a hog; but really John L.
was a bigger man than most of them who make a
profession of bash.
Sydney Stadium, like the Coliseum at
Rome, is up against it, and to meet the situa-
tion will in future only carry on business when
the proceedings seem worth while. The slide has
lasted a year or more, and as it is very obviously
going to continue, the promoters have acted with
great wisdom. Continuation of the struggle would
merely have meant crabbing the business beyond
redemption. When the travelling pug can arrive
with an extensive entourage and a loud noise and
excitement once more prevails as to whether Mick
can trim Joe, then the moment will be ripe for the
Stadium to again receive the multitude. Heaven
knows when that will be, though.
“0.K.”: Jonel Sullivan was a lucky
bounder. Any average citizen, with half the thirst
of John L. and equal means of satisfying it, has to
get buried at a much earlier age. He was the first
of the spoilt darlings of the stoush cranks who have
money to burn in the cause of the Ignoble Art, and
he remained an idol even after he had started the
fashion of drawing the color line by funking a
meeting with Peter Jackson.
Bill Beach will be 08 next time he has a
birthday, and he says he never felt better. The yarn
about athletics being a quick method of exhaustion gets
a bad ad. from Bill, for he did things in a hurry.
When he went to England he met and defeated five of
the best scullers in the world in three weeks. Three of
the contests were decided within one week. He rowed
seven times for the cnampionship of the world, and
was never beaten. The first wager he ever made was
at Kelso, near Bathurst, when he cued a wild bullock
for a barrel of beer. The old champion attributes
his success as a sculler firstly to his strong physique,
secondly to the muscular training he received at his
father’s anvil when a lad, and thirdly to perfect
training by Tommy Kelly. He has been living in
quiet retirement at Dapto for about 14 years.
The Hobart Regatta has been “wet”
afloat and ashore for 79 years; but the land part of
the 80th anniversary the other day was “dry.” When
the five booths were put up to auction the secretary
of the Temperance Alliance bought the lot for £5O,
and the thirsty souls without a private stock could
not get beyond ginger-beer and lemonade.
“Iverani”; It happened somewhere in
London. A burly Anzac had fallen foul of a crowd
of Tommies and was giving them his terrible opinion
of them. 1 thought he had conveyed it very lucidly,
but he seemed to think it needed illustration. “Look
’ere,” he said, with that withering contempt which
only beer can supply, “a man ’d feel a bit of a
to take on only a couple of you. What about three
of you ’avin’ a go?”
* * * #
The once-famous racing yacht Casco, in
which R. L. Stevenson left ’Frisco about 30 years
ago for the Southern Seas, has been rehabilitated for
trading in the Pacific. She was built in Cali-
fornia, is a top-sail schooner, 95ft. long and 70 tons
burden. About 10 years ago she was laid up at
Victoria, 8.C., and three years later was pressed
into service long enough to transport a cargo of
junk to Vancouver. Latterly she has been used
as a training ship.
* * * *
“Try Fluke” : Tom Jackson, the last living
of the old-time whalers of Kaikoura, who pulled his
first oar in a six-thwart whaleboat just 60 years
ago, is the doyen of M.L. authorities on sharks and
shark-lore. When he was a-whaling at Te Awaiti—
“old Tarwhite” —-in Queen Charlotte Sound, where
his father settled nearly 90 years ago, he slaughtered
all the sharks he could lay lance to, for the sake of
the liver oil. “Me noble shark,” he says, “gave us
all the light we needed in Tarwhite in the old times
—no kerosene then. We hung the livers up in a tree
to ripen and then squeezed them out and they gave a
first-rate oil. And there were sharks in those (lays—
-18-foot taniwhas, savage brutes wlio’d take a boat
between their jaws and shake it. That’s what one
big fellpw did to my old boat the Swiftsure one day
when I harpooned him —-turned on his belly and took
the bow of the boat in his mouth. I heard some-
thing crunching and smashing, and, thinks I, it’s all
up with the old Swiftsure, but the bows were too
wide for him to close his jaw on, and the breaking
sound that I heard was the crackling of the scoun-
drel’s teeth. He left three of them in the planks as
a memento, and we had to saw ’em off smooth. But
somehow these days the sharks don’t seem to grow l
so big—same as the peaches, nothing like the boomers
of the old days, me son!”
In a recent match it was not the fault of
Georgeson, of South Brisbane, that the Toowong team
accumulated 64 runs, lie took nine wickets for 36
and caught the other.
* * # *
G. .1. Neill (Sydney), who took six for 28
against Petersham on Saturday, is a new all-rounder
in premiership cricket. His best in three matches is
top-score (41) against Western Suburbs. Another
to put in good work in the mud was R. B. Minnett
(Middle Harbor), who piled up 37 (out of 82)
and took five wickets for 40 against Western Suburbs.
R.B. is one of three brothers (R.V. and L.A. are
the others) who have plaved for Ma in inter-State
cricket. After R. B. Minnett had taken “most
wickets” for ’Varsity in ’O7-8, he dropped out; but
in ’l4-15, he averaged 38.50 and took 32 wickets
worth 18.00 each. This season his 23 wickets have
averaged 13.52.
* * * *
Of 22 batsmen sent to the pavilion in the
’Varsity-North Sydney match on Saturday, not one
was bowled. Twenty were caught, one was stumped
and the remainder had thoughtfully put a leg in the
wrong place.
* * * *
Back for a spell in Australia, Private Billy
Kelly, the greatest of Sydney Rugby League five-
eighths. Kelly picked up the game in Maoriland,
where he played with Wellington in Union rep. games.
Afterwards he pulled on the League jersey for Wel-
lington and Maoriland. In Sydney he captained
Balmain to victory in the Rugby League premier-
ship, and also helped the Ma State in rep. matches.
Kelly is one of the buttresses of Northern Union
Rugby at this end of the globe.
* *• * *
“Arthur R.”: Alf Baud, the popular
skipper of the Carltons, is probably finished for
active service. A few months ago he was reported
slightly wounded; but his skull proved to be more
seriously damaged than was thought, necessitating
an operation and the young footballer’s return to
Australia.
* * * *
“S.T.S.”: Australian authors may not be
specially popular in London, but at any rate they
don’t have to disguise their work to get it printed.
Almost every cheap English magazine nowadays
contains a story written in America, but carefully
transplanted on to English soil. You can always
tell the American touch, somehow. Sometimes an
oversight gives it away fatally, as in one in a
recent Strand, where “United States” has been
carefully translated into “United Kingdom,” stars-
and-stripes into Union Jack, and so on, and the
truth only leaks out in a casual reference to foot-
ball. The hero had been a Cambridge University
crack, and there, “playing right-forward” in an
“eleven,” he had “bucked his way right through the
whole line, 60 yards for a touchdown”—which is
neither Rugby nor Soccer, but just the American
college game.
Sydney High School athletics owe much
to Cyril P. Scnrader, whose broad shoulders never
weary of work which will help -Young Australia.
Five days of the week he keeps an eye on the
cricket of Technical High School lads; on Saturday
with Frank Iredale, M. A. Noble and other enthu-
siasts he points the way to schoolboys practising on
Svdney Cricket Ground. Tennis, baseball and soccer
football take up other spare moments through the
year; and at the High School athletic championships
the programme runs smoothly under his care.
Schrader, an M.A. of Sydney ’Varsity, is one of the
masters of Sydnev Technical College.
* * * *
“Montalex”: In my part of the world,
when a badger was caught, lie was generally
“piped”; the pipe being a narrow trench about
7yds. long, roofed over with boards and soil.
Liberated into this, the “brock” betook himself to
the end, and there dug himself in by raising a
barrier of earth that left above it an opening for
only his biting department. Then dog after dog
would be sent in to try to draw him. I have never
known a fox-terrier succeed. _ Game enough certainly
the dogs were, and wouldn’t cry-off till they were
seriously mauled. But the job was beyond them.
Finally an experienced bull-terrier would be put on
the scent. Even then, it would be sometimes 15 or 20
minutes before —fearfully cut-up himself —he could
drag forth the prisoner. If he came out alone, and
sat down to lick his wounds, it meant that the badger
was dead meat.
“G.L.H.”: “Ranger” (B. 17/1/’18) says
that using a rifle-sling on mounds does not spoil a
man for shooting in the field. His experience does
not tally with that of the bush club I am a member
of. We have several first-class wallaby-shooters as
members, and they are all blobs on the mounds, using
a sling. On the 'other hand, in the scrub none of
the good target-shooters have a hope against them.
What else could be expected? A rifleclubman is
taught to tie himself down in a position impossible
in the bush and given two sighter shots. Hut
make him do without his sigliters and where is he?
His first shot will be a miss every time. The wal-
laby-shooter has to know his rifle and get his first
shot home, either kneeling or standing. If he misses
with his first, he is out of it. I have done well
myself at the target; but, like all rifleclub-
men, I must have sigliters and know where
each individual shot strikes, or I am lost. If club-
men were forced to do without that farce, sigliters.
and their shots were not signalled until firing finished,
we should have some exalted rifle shots. When
“Ranger” talks about the mound expenses that his
crowd paid out of the foxes they shot, I come at
him. When next in Sydney, if “Ranger” likes, [
will lay him £1 to 10s. for 50 shots, or anv num-
ber, against his hitting the bull at 500yds. kneeling
or standing without sling. Every bull, I will pay
out £1 ; every other shot he pays me 10s. The
500yds. target is the easiest of all the ranges, so
that, on his talk, he ought to do well.

* * * *
It looks as if the much-boomed synthetic
rubber has failed as a substitute for the real article
in Germany. Fritz was forced to attempt the imita-
tion by his failure to replenish his stock of natural
rubber, though for some months after the beginning
of the war he was getting heaps of the stuff through
holes in the blockade. At present he is keeping his
real maekay for staff ears and is fitting many essen-
tial war vehicles w r ith iron and wood tyres, which
means a serious loss in speed and efficiency. Motor
trucks formerly capable of 12 miles an hour now get
an Iron Cross if they can manage ten.

But when the eye has run down the “Profits”
column, switch it over to the “Written Oil.”
Then it will he seen that during the past
three years, though there is no reason to
suspect the management of having suddenly
gone gay, no visible appropriation has been
made to cover depreciation. Instead, nowa-
days, profit is shown only after provision
has been made “for repairs, renewals, taxa-
tion and sundry contingencies.” The mod-
esty of some of these patriots is surprising.-
Before the war this co., for instance, was
able to put by 15 or 20 thousand a year
towards renewals. Since the war it has been
so eager to help the Allies that it has been
barely making ends meet; but it is suppress-
ing all mention of its own privations in order
not to make the Allies feel uncomfortable.
However, though the Maoriland Government
was very slow in getting off the mark, it has
a nice, serviceable little income tax in opera-
tion now, which apparently will bite Ts. (Id.
in the £ out of tile bulk of the profit of this
co.
The look of the accounts has been much
altered by the shortage of shipping: meat
and wool.' wanted by the British Government
whenever they can be got, have been piling
up in Maoriland, and extra store accommo-
dation has been in urgent demand. “Build-
ing operations at Islington, Smithfield,
Pukeuri and Burnside are being undertaken
to cope with the requirements of the season
now commencing.” To finance the building
campaign further capital has been got in. the
unissued shares being sent out at a 50 p.c.
premium ; while special advances have been
made by the bank against stocks of wool
and meat held on account of the British
Government ( £603,0051. The following table
shows the more important changes: —

Capital is now £300.000 in 20,000 shares of
£lO (of which 90S were issued during the
; ear) and 25,000 of £4 (of which 13,280
were lately issued) ; but there are still some
calls to be paid. In 10 years, though the
dividend has averaged slightly over 10 p.c..
approximately only one-third of the profit
has been divided, and approximately equal
amounts have been put to reserves and laid
by to provide for renewals, etc. On the 10
years, therefore, the net profit has averaged
approximately 20 p.c., and the provision for
renlacement. etc., appears to have been ex-
ceedingly liberal. It seems safe to say that
for the past three years of war the figures
are vastly better even than that.

you don’t include the £5OO added during the
past year to Contingent Securities Depreci-
ation Fund, the profit left over after paying
the dividend was only £124. The gradual
shrinkage of profits since 1913 has bee_n
mainly due to increasing taxation —1915,
£900; 1916, £1433; 1917, £2968. And for
the current year the income tax, at any
rate, will no doubt be still more. In the
circumstances the directors might have been
expected to be a bit chary about making
advances; but apparently they don’t regard
the future as uncertain. At any rate, as
the next table shows, they are still, adding
to their advances :
1905. 1910. 1915. 1917.
Capital an d
reserves ..£94,193 £124,656 £137.968 £140,317
Liabilities .. 260,781 240.924 262,427 294,433
Assets i *
Advances . . 339,235 344,960 369,391 382,673
Freeholds.. 11,776 10,237 10,001 9,500
Liquid . . 3,963 10,383 21,003 42,577
Liabilities consist principally—almost wholly
—of deposits; and unless that money is
secured over a long period the management
is liable to have some worrying times in the
future. Incidentally the co. has the unusual
habit of changing its chairman every year
and making him managing director —
Harold Beauchamp in 1915, Balcombe Brown
in 1916. G. M. Kebbell in the past year; all
experienced men, certainly, and with a good
financial pull. In one respect, no doubt,
Maoriland can look ahead with more confi-
dence than Australia. The Dominion farmer
doesn’t depend upon wheat to a great ex-
tent, and it doesn’t take a fleet of ships to
carry away a million tons worth of wheat
and wool. All the same, there is a tremen-
dous bill to pay; and Maoriland will be
paying it long after it has ceased to get war
prices for its products. And if that doesn't
affect the value of such securities as this co.

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character ) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Helen Blazes; Couldn’t you put it all in
a page ? Haven’t got time for jokes that run to
eight or 10 sheets S.: Very nearly ran into a
place.... Noek: No use. Hasn’t a thousand to one
chance of getting past the Censor S.W.P.: Yarn
hasn’t much color and goes rather conventionally.
W.H.: Must go back A.H.: “Diary is
heavy fooling... .R.K.: Not up... .W.H.M.: There s
sense in that: —
Stop your clamor, stop your jaw,
Take a hammer, take a saw,
Get some boards and some nails,
I’ll find the wind to fill the sails.
... .E.G.; No interest... .Drummer: Not convincing,
though smart in spots.... Pitta: Too long for in-
terest. .. .R.G.B.: Not our style. .. .M.L.: Nothing in
it G.W.: “Somme Sport” doesn’t go Red
Lodge: Not up.... Padre: You don’t protest against
prudery; you emphasise it E.M.: One, may do.
....Blank: “Revolution in Germany” says nothing
new. Put your name on your copy. A covering
letter is liable to be detached Iphigenia: Yes,
there’s hope ; but blank verse is not a good medium.
.. . . J.IT.: Sonnets are a bit stiff. “Ship of Dawn
held for inquest E.G.: “Johnson” doesn't move
us. .. . J.D.S.: Wants more skill J.E.: Don’t care
for them M.M.: The “Flapper” won't do; the
other is on the border... .N.T.: This isn’t a nursery
paper.... Iiranskopf: No special thrill in it; but.
good feeling is good feeling whatever the merits of
the writing.... M. B.: “Guilty” of a very crude
attempt.... G.lt.M.: Not good enough... .Scotia: No.
....BAY.: None of them; though an “ ’Ollow Cry”
is very nearly.... Condorrie: Looks like a first
attempt. No reason why there shouldn't be a second.
....No name: That “Wright-Wrong” juggle of words
takes first prize for stolid stupidity.... Derwentside:
Well, it’s a rather dismal slab.... W.T.; The “Re-
cluse” says nothing at all....H.E.; The “Hughesiad’
is lame stuff making elephantine efforts after mirth.
... .E.V.M.lv.: Weak commonplace... .A. C.: No....
V.M.: “Boys of A.I.F.” doesn’t contain a new idea
nor an old one freshly stated. .. .L.G.: Try something
that gives you scope for feeling'. “Memory” ought
to play with something better than grass and flowers.
. ...G.L.: “Singing Sands” must go back.... Black
Hawk: The 21st fails like the rest. You’ll have to
make it something better than “slop” before you
score... . V.L.S.: Might have made a small paragraph.
We’re always looking for small paragraphs....
J.L.R.: Seems the same thin noise in longer lines.
. ...E.M.E.: The suggestion of lines somebody else
wrote to a “Queensland Opal” spoils it....B.C.:
“The Parson” goes back. .. .B.C. (M.L.) : There’s
too much scream, but the yarn has some power. . . .
F.R.: Not nearly up.... C.J.II.: The person who
begins a yarn, “The exigencies of life have at times
rather exacting tendencies” doesn’t really want it
published. He is only getting some of the inherent
vice out of his nature....No Name: “Denunciation”
is a mere pen of words... .Fernleaf: A good enough
subject, but the verse is not... .D.W.: Nothing there.
. ...Kridd: Too long for its interest... .H.T.: “The
Seer” looks a little hysterical.... Rufus: Verse too
rugged. .. .J.H.C.: Weak spot in the “bagpipes”
narrative is that there wasn’t any Turkey in exist-
ence 3000 years ago. ...L.I.: No freshness or
novelty about those “cries”... .N.C.: “Sonnet” no
good.... Digger: Nice feeling, but a bit too rugged.
... .C.P.M.: Moral tale in loose verse. If one or the
other has to be loose, make it the morals. . . .L.D.M.:
Rather too mad.... A.G.M.: No use...-M.F.: No joy
in it. ... I.O.G.: You won't make gjood verse by
choosing a simple theme and then stringing every
commonplace phrase and idea you can upon it....
E.R.: Not in this paper... .L.D.: “Memory” hasn’t
enough in it....N.J.; Not up. .. .S.J.B.: It won’t
do....F.J.; Coming all the way from Peshawar,
“The Empire’s Sons” should get a hearing:—
England, how glorious is thy name,
Whose fight for honor is Freedom’s gain;
From times' immemorial thou has reigned,
For Liberty’s cause; to-day mankind’s saviour
again.
Thy sons and daughters in every sphere
Fight amidst pain, for all they hold dear;
The Bull-dog breed still exists ;
Restoration must be made ere you desist ;
In Duty’s call thy clarion ring
Glory to our glorious Empire they bring.
. ...A.G.J.: “France” verses are good; but you don’t
keep the sonnet form. Why not revise and avoid such
shocking rhymes as “conqueror” and “war” ?....
J.M.: Not quite up... ./Willoughby: Not our style.
N.C.: “Sonnet” no value A.W.: No, it’s
“Scarcely Probable” ; but you had a dainty bit of
insanity there if you had handled it. Try again.
P.L.B.: None quite up L.L.D.: Poor kind of
idea... .M.S.: What exactly do you mean by saying
we are “worse than dead”? In fact, what exactly
do you mean by any of it?... .F.C.F.: “Mem’ries”
don’t rise to sufficient heights Yannatherra:
Rather crude Gertrude E.: If you can get those
figures it will make a good par.; write again....
W.H.C. (France) : No use sending it to us if you
have already sent it to others... .1. Say: Quite cor-
rect ; other was first in Austin D.: Can’t read
the faint pencil... .Cobber: There ought to be a good
story in that gun; get on to it Mac: Think you
misread the proud father. He merely pointed "out
the disparity between the country medico’s mileage
charge (10s. 6d.) and what the garage people find
profitable (Is.). There is no suggestion in his par.
that he would have jibbed at a moderate professional
fee Son of the Marsh: Thanks. But what is
there to reply to? On that matter this paper stands
where it has always stood—for universal service. It
stands where the Labor party used to stand and where
it will stand again before it is many years older.
Either that or the Labor party will see one of its
most-treasured planks go overboard and the white
laborer will bow his neck to a competition that will
make wreck of most of his social ideals.

Look to Your Teetk.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-

Heart of Empire Series—No. 9.
The Houses of Parliament,
on the site of the old Palace of
Westminster, were opened in 1847.
Designed by Sir Charles Barry,
R.A., in the Gothic Perpendicular
Style and noted throughout the
world for their symmetry and
beauty. The Royal or Victoria
Tower, 340 feet high, is the royal
entra ce to the House of Lords,
a magnificently decorated chamber.
The House of Commons, 70 feet
long, is also very handsome. Big
Ben was set in place in 1860.
Westminster Hall was built by
William Rufus and has been skil-
fully incorporated in the design.

“The Immature Male”: In a current show
which critics agree is made by its chorus,
120 dear things (more or less) are sweated
nightly. The girl who desired to shine in
this particular frivolity had first to put in
four weeks’ strenuous work for nothing,
rehearsing mornings, afternoons and some-
times evenings, week days and Sundays, fre
quently through meal hours, and at all times
in a thick atmosphere. Then, if she passed
the weeding-out process as regards legs,
voice and general appearance, she was
engaged at £1 12s. 6d. per week—a few bob
more than Main Mary Annette gets with, her
keep. The girls’ week includes six evening
performances, two matinees and numerous
rehearsals —and a rehearsal is frequently
called between a matinde and the even-
ing show. Extra matindes are not
paid _ for, and the producer and his
satellites have the almost magisterial
power to further reduce the wretched 325. 6d.
by inflicting a, series of petty fines (minimum
55.) for such trivial “offences” as mistakes
made through nervousness. Imagine how
fatally easy it is for a jaded girl to get mixed
in the complicated and frequently idiotic
“business” invented by ballet mistresses and
others! Heaven only knows how they
memorise all the rot they have to do.
“Fax”: What a master of unconscious
humor is James, the N.S.W. Minister for
The Panto., Melbourne
King's.
Zola Terrell, Principal
Danseuse.
Education! He
gravely announces
that the panto,
kiddies “seem to
enjoy their stage
exp e r i ence.” Of
course they do.
They love it all the
time. But their
enthusiasm for the
footlights, or what
they do on the
stage, doesn’t touch
the question.
Kiddies enjoy lots
of things which do
them no good.
They love choco-
late creams, and
woffld die of a sur-
feit if given the
chance. On the
other hand they
hate arithmetic, but
the Minister for
Education forces it
on them.
Melbourne lovers of the literary drama
will not starve this year if the Repertory
Theatre can help it. Beginning in April,
Gregan McMahon hopes to provide perform-
ances every two months. Gabriele D’An-
nunzio’s “Gioconda,” Brieux’s “The Three
Daughters of M. Dupont,” and Anatole
France’s “The Man Who Married a Dumb
Wife” are among the unusual things pro-
mised. together with helpings of Galsworthy,
Arnold Bennett, Rudolph Besier, Pinero
(“The Amazons”), St. John Han kin, Gran-
ville Barker, Jones (“The Liars”), and
'f.-hekhov. and two new plays only recently
seen on the commercial stage in London
Githa Sowerby’s “Sheila” and II. A.
YaehclPs “Uumpty Dumpty.”
“B.” : N’Yark has not taken Allen Doone
to its bosom in “Ilappy-Go-Lucky O’Shea.”
lie made a bad start in a theatre he had
taken on a four weeks’ lease, and, apparently,
could not recover.
“A.” : Tenor Walter Kirby passes around
a letter from II. D. McIntosh offering him
£100 a week to lift his fruity voice and
doubled-barrelled chin on the Tivoli circuit.
Walter says his fee. is £25 a performance,
and publicly scorns Mac’s offer. Just as
well, perhaps. In the old days of dinkum
variety shows, at any rate, the gallery would
have made nasty noises if Walter had ap-
peared on the scene.
“H.O.”: Despite many episodes of the
“Willow Tree” sort, the curtain lias very
rarely been rung down before the finish of a
performance. It was done in Melbourne at
the old Opera House many years ago,
when Mrs. Bernard Beere was appear-
ing in “Fedora.” On this occasion,
though, the trouble came not from the
auditorium, but from the stage. The
gentleman with whom Mrs. Beere was
playing was well sozzled, and in the
middle of a big emotional scene lie flopped
down upon a couch. The actress for a moment
looked appealingly towards the audience. “I
can’t go on,” she exclaimed despairingly, and
burst into tears; so down came the rag.
Bram Stoker, in his biography of Irving,
“The Bing Boys," at
Sydney Majesty's.
The Keeper of Lions
(Clyde Cook).
relates that one
night, when “Ham-
let” was the bill,
Irving, as soon as
be faced Polonius
realised that the
mummer was
screwed. S’renry at
once gave the sign
to send down the
curtain, and made
a breif, dignified
speech to the
house, announcing
that the play would
be proceeded with
as soon as a slight
rearrangement of
the cast had been
effected. A “re-
arrangement of the
cast” after the
performance had
started took place
at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s on the open-
ing night of a
revival of an old
play about a year
ago. There was a
nice derangement
of epithets in the
earlier scenes, and
for the second half of the play the part was
read by a lady cast for a minor character.
It was at Sydney Cri. that George Rig-
nold was given a rough passage by a few
galleryites while playing in “The Three Mus-
keteers.” Annoyed bv one of the interrupt-
ers, somebody shouted: “Put him out!”
“Blanky good idea !” commented George from
the stage, and went on with his lines. Shortly
after the leading Sydney and Melbourne
theatres had reduced the price of gallery
seats to a “zaek,” George was guyed by a
sixpenny crowd at Sydney Majesty’s. Rig-
nold paused, advanced to the footlights and
announced briefly: ' To-morrow night and
thereafter, one shiling.”
“0.K.”: Ivathlene Macdonell’s outburst is
another reminder that mummers make an
egotistical practice of claiming consideration
for themselves quite apart from the charac-
ters they play. When the part is effective
the performer laps up all the applause and
sweet criticism on the strength of having
acted it. When it is silly, the hired expo-
nent bitterly resents the ridicule due to the
part. A mummeress in a silly character has
no right to immunity from the annoyance of
laughter. She takes a sporting risk of being
absurdly circumstanced when she takes a
star part in an untried piece. She can blame
the management, or the author, if she likes, for
the tittering of the gallery girls who are
ordinarily her perfervid adorers. She shouldn t
blame the titterers. But women mummers
are not much worse than males where guy-
ing is concerned. An actor is very apt to
make an object of himself in such cases as
soon as he thinks himself of sufficient import-
ance to be able to show temper on the stage.
An obscure mummer must suffer in nervous
silence, but a leading man may walk up to
the footlights and protest against the audi-
ence's sense of humor. Audiences have little
enough sense of humor, God knows! The
business of mummers is to bluff them into
the belief that bathos is pathos, cheap tricks
and mannerisms poetically emotional, and a
bit of ranting a masterpiece of dramatic
passion. Once in a way the bluff doesn’t
come off’, and then the bluffer curses the
public’s lack of gullibility. What educated
theatre-goers complain of is that the public
doesn’t greet all silly plays with derision, and
thus raise dramatic entertainment to a
respectable level.
“The Bing Boys," at
Sydney Majesty's.
Oliver Bing (Leslie
Holland).
“S.S.”: It re-
quires a lot of
nerve in a pro. to
stand up before a
guying audience
and pretend not to
notice it. Gus
Glover once got
“the bird” at the
old Alexandra, Mel-
bourne. Gus had a
tremendous voice.
He let it go. The
malcontents hissed
% little louder. Gus
went one better.
The hubbub in the
pit increased, and
G 1 o v e r’s roar
nearly lifted the
roof. Gus won,
silence fell. Then
entered the “poor
old man,” holding
a flickering candle
over his head, and
q u aver ing : “I
thought I heard a
voice.” Down came
the house. Another
old pro., Cathcart.
awakened objec-
tions in a section
of a Royal audience. It boo-hooed for
three minutes; then Cathcart, speaking
nicely across the footlights: “You who
object to me may say what you please.
I am less fortunately situated —my objec-
tion to you is unspeakable.” At the
Pinafore, Tasmania, a variety co. from
Launceston contained a basso profund
whose Mephistophelean uproar moved the
miners to contempt. Basso tried again
and again, but was silenced with yells of
derision. At last he ceased liis efforts to sing.
Stepping out on the roof of the piano, he
shook a fist like a ham at the audience.
“Perhaps I can sing grand opera,” he said.
“Perhaps I can’t. But I’ll tell you what —
I’ll fight any eight-stone man in this audi-
ence for £10!” Basso weighed 15 stone if
he scaled an ounce !
“75” : At the fourth time of offering, actor
Teddy Landor has been accepted for khaki.
Edward revealed a quaint vein of humor in
“The Glad Eye,” and scored several Shaks-
perian successes with Allan Wilkie, notably
as clown in “Twelfth Night.”
“First Old Man”: The Demon King in
pantomime is a devilish infliction, as a rule.
The only exception I can recall is provided
by the King Rat of this season’s “Dick Whit-
tington.” lie carries off the mumming honors
of the show —gives full weight to every word
he utters, and by real acting ability evolves
a character as interesting as any in the
cast. Ilis singing, too. is that of an artist,
and he goes through his job (not the most
thankful one in the world) to his own evi-
dent enjoyment. Any “Poverty Pointer”
seen a better?
“Frank J.,” from the footlights in France:
I can settle the Ko Ivo argument. Ko Ko was
intended by Gilbert to be a little, insignificant,
jrmped-up buffoon. My authority is Richard
Wethersby, producer of the G. and S. operas in
Australia a few years ago, and Charles Whalen, who
played the part, both men trained by Gilbert him-
self. The Howard Vernon type was quite wrong.
Tradition was a fetish with old Dick Wethersby.
Not a departure from what Gilbert originally pro-
duced in score, libretto and business would he per-
mit. I was a member of the co. and recall
having trouble because I one day so violated “tra-
dish” in “Pirates” as to chew the tassel of my
cap, which made some people in the audience laugh
and old Dick roar like a bull, “Hark at ’em!” His
idea was that they should have thrown the theatre at
me.
“Glen Huntly”: Watching Barry Lupino,
as Clown in the “Aladdin” harlequinade at
Melbourne King’s, tickle the policeman's
seat of humor with a red-hot. poker, re-
minded me that 60 years ago, at Edinburgh,
Lupino’s grandfather paid a similar delicate
attention to S’renry Irving, who on that
occasion was the bobby.
“Iv.” : Peter Dawson, the virile Australian
baritone who looks like a fighter and sings
like a seraph, is the most satisfying star
H. D. Mclntosh has rolled on to the Rickards
circuit. He has none of the airs or graces
of the big artist, isn’t foolishly lavish with
encores, and has a charmingly natural plat-
form manner which is almost big-brotherly.
The other night, at Sydney Tivoli, somebody
asked for “The Floral Dance.” “I’m sorry,
I don’t think I’ve got that one,” said Peter,
turning over his songs. “But I’ll tell you
what I'll do. I’ll send out for it and sing
you something else while we’re waiting.”
Then, when it arrived: “Don’t know how
I’ll get on with this. I haven’t sung it for
about six months.” But he got through
brilliantly.
Constance Buchan, an Ipswich girl who
won the good opinion of Melba and Fritz
Hart while she was at the Melbourne Con-
servatoriurn, will give Brisbaneites a chance
of hearing her soprano on February 16.
“Bob Ray”: There is a slight error in
“William Devine’s” interesting account of
Jenny Watt Tanner’s career (B. 33/1/T8).
“The Village Priest” was originally produced
by a fine co. headed by Charles Cartwright
and Olgo Nethersole, Olga being the Coun-
tess. Jenny Watt Tanner played the part
of the blind woman. Brough and Bouci-
cault revived the play in ’93, and several
times subsequently. With them Mrs. Brough
was always the Countess. The title rule
has been acted by Cartwright, Titheradn'b
Brough and Flemming, all dead.
THE STAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VICTORIA.
Maud has a pianola,
And Mabel’s learnt to play
Profound duets with Lola
(They practise half the day) ;
And Jean with curl a-bobbing
Sets her violin a-throbbing,
A-sighing and a-sobbing,
To steal my heart away.
The music’s very pretty,
And yet it fails to please,
For daily in the city,
Upon the lettered keys,
Another’s fingers, flicking,
Set the old typewriter clicking;
To me its jerky ticking
Weaves sweeter tunes than these.
YouTl think my car is lacking
In nieeness to prefer
A mere typewriter’s clacking,
But, ah! it’s played by her!
Her charms like magic mingle
Rich music with the jingle;
She’s twenty, sweet and single,
And Love’s interpreter.
And Love's a great composer,
While, coaching her in art,
’Tis Maestro Cupid shows her
Just how to play her part;
So trim, in white attired,
She sits, my blood is fired.
What instrument's required ?—•
She plays upon my heart.
SYD. C.
THE USES OF ADVERTISEMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They saw Eve when she first appeared
Upon the local stage. “In fact,
She is extremely plain,” they sneered,
“And certainly she cannot act.”
Then for a year they read the smart
Advertisements which said her aft
And beauty had no counterpart.
They saw her once again, and had
Their doubts about her looks, but owned
Her acting was not wholly bad.
The third inspection was postponed
For six months. Posters everywhere
Declared Miss Fortesque was fair
And blessed with talents past compare.
Again they see her in a part.
Her talents now they all admit;
Her beauty captures every heart.
And yet Eve has not changed a bit.
(Advertisement has wondrous skill
To hypnotise the human will
And make it swallow any pill!)
VICTORIA.

ESTABLISHED 1852


London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Amsdkll, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
There are plenty of reasons why grey hair
is not desirable, and plenty of reasons why
hair dyes should not be used. But, on the
other hand, there is no reason why you should
have grey hair if you do not want it. To turn
the hair back to a natural color is really a
very simple matter. One has only to get from
the chemist two ounces of concentrate of tam-
malite and mix it with three ounces of bay
rum. Apply to the hair with a small sponge
for a few nights and the greyness will gradu-
ally disappear. This liquid is not sticky or
greasy, and does not injure the hair in any
way. It has been used for generations with
most satisfactory results by those who have
known the formula.
Blackheads Fly Away.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into gen-
eral use in the boudoir. It is very simple,
harmless and pleasant. Drop a stymol tablet
obtained at the chemist’s, in a tumbler full of
hot water. After the effervescence has suit-
sided bathe the face in the liquid, using a
small sponge or soft cloth. In a few minutes
dry the face and the offensive blackheads will
come right off on the towel. Also the large
oily pores immediately close up and efface
themselves naturally. The greasiness dis-
appears and the skin is left smooth, soft and
cool. This simple treatment is then repeated
a few times at intervals of four or five days
to ensure the permanence of the result.
Emergency Complexions.
Even a complexion which is hopelessly .dull
or greasy can be made beautiful in a moment
by a perfectly harmless home recipe. If you
have no cleminite in the house, get about an
ounce from your chemist, and add only
enough water to dissolve it. The process de-
fies detection, and the result is an immediate
appearance of velvety, youthful “bloom”
which every woman desires. The effect will
last all day under the most trying conditions,
indoors or out. and renders powdering quite
unnecessary. This simple substance is also
very good for the skin, and no possible harm
can come from its regular use.
Effectually Killing Superfluous Hair.
Many women know how to remove ugly
growths of superfluous hair temporarily, but
few know how to remove it permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered plieminol may be
used. Get about an ounce from your chemist
and apply a little directly to the objection-
able hair. The purpose of the recommended
treatment is not merely to remove the super-
fluous hair instantly, but also to kill the hair
roots completely in a comparatively short
time.
How Nature Makes New Complexions.
It is well known that the human skin is
constantly undergoing a tearing down and
building up process. With advancing years or
waning vitality this tissue-change lags: the
lifeless, soiled surface skin stays on so long
that its owner gets a “poor complexion.”
Commonsense tells us this dead skin cannot
be enlivened or beautified b.v any cosmetic,
lotion or powder. The natural thing to do is
to actually remove the offensive skin—remove
the bad complexion It has been found that
ordinary mercolized wax completely absorbs
the devitalised skin, in minute particles, so
gently, gradually, as to cause no inconveni-
ence. Mercolized wax, which any chemist can
supply, is put on at night like cold cream,
and washed off in the morning. If you would
have a brilliantly beautiful and youthful com-
plexion, just use this simple treatment.
How to Have Thick and Pretty Hair.
Soaps and artificial shampoos ruin many
beautiful heads of hair. Few people know
that a teaspoonful of good stallax dissolved
in a cup of hot water has a natural affinity
for the hair and makes the most delightful
shampoo imaginable. It leaves the hair bril-
liant, soft and wavy, cleanses the scalp com-
pletely, and greatly stimulates the hnir-
growtli. The only drawback is that stallax
seems rather expensive. It comes to the*
chemist only in sealed quarter-pound pack-
ages, which retail at about four shillings.
However, as this is sufficient for 25 or 30
shampoos, it really works out very cheaply
in the end. [4

THE thing that the people should have
been allowed to realise from the outset
is that WE MAY GET A LICKING. We
still may get a licking. Are you going to
let the Ravisher of Belgium win without
lifting a hand?
For enlistments during 1917 Gippsland
tops the list of Victorian country electorates
with exactly 500 volunteers. The fact has
caused vice-chairman Gillespie, of the Re-
cruiting Committee, to hand an unsolicited
testimonial to McLachlan as the. most enei-
getic Parliamentarian in the business.
’Struth, after all, is more than an exclama-
tion. The other day Private Struth came
back to Grasmere, a Vic. AA estern District
village, after a series of strafing jobs at
Pozieres, Mouquet Farm and Ypres, followed
bv a spell in hospital. Years ago, by the
way, the name used to be St. Ruth; but
time modified it.
“T. 0.1.” : Now being demolished, stone by
stone, a quaint edifice of the old convict
days, near Gosford (N.S.AV.). It is a guaid-
house, erected by F. A. Hely, Superintendent
of Convicts, who died September J, 18ou, and
whose homestead and neglected grave are
close by. The homestead was a large bun-
galow ; from the outside the guard-house
had much the appearance of a church—a
building of two stories in the centre, with a
lean-to on either side. There were two cells
underground, where, ’tis said, were
confined by chain and padlock. Certainl\
there are rings let into the stones in these
cells, which would indicate that prisoners
were supplied with inducements to stay. By
the way, near by are some pear trees which
must be close-on 90 years old.
Of the 180,000 square miles of this con-
tinent where it rains more than 40 inches
per annum, 95,000 square miles are in
Premier Ryan’s garden. There are -00
recorded cases between 1890 and 1916- when
over 10 inches of rain sloshed down on the
official rain-gauge in one day at some point
or another; in fact, there must be .more,
for along the coast nothing below 12 inches
in 24 hours is reckoned worthy of inclusion
among the big falls. And what may happen
over the enormous areas where .the official
gauge doesn’t hang up its hat in the halls
of human habitation no man knows exactly
save the oldest inhabitant, and he is
capable of knowing anything. Croham-
hurst. a green and pleasant spot in the
Blackall Ranges, once registered practically
a yard of rain (2ft. 11.71 in.) in one day
and a night. Port Douglas did itself
proud with 2ft. 7-Ain. and Yarrabah with
2ft. 6-Jin. As for the records between -tt.
and 2sft. of moisture, they are too many
to enumerate. Goondi, which is always
well up in the batting average, recorded
6yd. 2ft. llin. of downfall for the year
1894 —did it without an effort. As for
such items as over 5 yards, places like
Goondi, Innisfail and Harvey’s Creek take
them in their stride, so to speak.
“AA'art” sees an old horror off the
premises : . ■ .
When Governor Macquarie visited Tasmania in
1821 he ordered the removal of the northern estab-
lishment from Georgetown to Launceston, and made
plans for several buildings, including, of course, a
gaol. These were completed in 1824.
The old gaol is now being demolished. Successive
Ministries threatened to do the job, but it was left
to Lee to set the contractors to work. The site is
to be added to the High School grounds. The tread-
mill was done away with as far back as 1854 ; that
part of the premises is used as a stable for the
mounted police.
When Judge Barron Field made his first trip to
Tasmania to hold a court the gaol was so full that
the prisoners had to take turns to lie down. Seventy
prisoners were called up for sentence in one day,
and 18 were sentenced to death. Of late years, how-
ever, only short-sentence men have been kept there,
and it has taken the police all their time to run in
enough casuals to keep the walls decently white-
washed.
The last man to be hanged there was Tony Brax-
ton. He murdered a lady-friend. This was about oo
years ago. My only personal recollection of the
matter is that the murderer had steak-and-kidney
pie for his last breakfast.
The first part the contractors pulled down was the
chapel. It was built with a brick wall about 12ft.
high running down the centre. This .was to prevent
the male and female prisoners from seeing or com-
municating with each other. The privileged parson,
however, could see down both sides.
A good many bushrangers attended the service in
old days —Brown, Hunt, Holland, Brady, Jeffries,
Daulton, Kelly and others of less note. Daulton and
Kelly were hanged here; Brown and Hunt arrived
wounded and died in the gaol. The others were sent
to Hobart to receive the professional attentions of
Dogerty. Brady was undoubtedy the most famous of
all the prisoners. He was the polite ruffian who
would entertain the ladies on the piano while his
followers were robbing the house. On one occasion he
knocked down a member of the gang for attempting
to kiss a servant-girl. All this made him a great
favorite with the ladies, and while he was confined
in the gaol they kept his cell furnished with
flowers, fruit, wine, etc. Once, between jobs, Brady
decided to come up to Launceston and see his old
friend Jeffries, who was in the gaol there. .Jeffries
had been hangman in Edinburgh. Transported to
Tasmania, he escaped and put 13 murders to his
credit. He had been a member of Brady's gang, and
on being captured “peached,” so Brady wanted to
talk to him. Always the gentleman, he wrote to
Colonel Balfor, Commandant at Launceston, to say
that he would be up that night to stick-up the house
of Richard Dry (father of the Tasmanian Speaker)
and also take Jeffries out of gaol and shoot him. The
first part of the programme was carried out; the rest
went wrong. The Government then raised the reward
for the gang to £4OO per head. Soon after Brady
was wounded in the ankle. Batman, later on
founder of Victoria, thought he knew where the
leader was hiding in the Western Tiers, and went out
to look for him. Walking along, he was suddenly con-
fronted by Brady propped up with a wattle-crutch.
Brady covered him and called out to know if he was
a soldier. Batman replied: “I’m no soldier, Brady ;
I’m John Batman. You’d better give yourself up.
There’s no chance.” Brady put down his gun.
“You’re right, Batman,” he said. “And as you’re
a brave man, I give myself up to you and cheat the
soldiers of the reward.” Batman had quite a
triumphal procession into Launceston. AVhile in the
gaol Brady was made, a popular hero; but to his
disgust he was sent to Hobart in company with
Jeffries, hangman and traitor.
Dogerty was easily Van Diemen’s Land’s most
practised hangman. Solomon Blay could never have
reached within hundreds of Dogerty’s total, which
is reckoned to be somewhere about 500. He had
adjusted the noose for as many as nine in one morn-
ing. He took his profession very seriously, and
used to boast that nowhere could you get hanged so
decently as in Hobart. To do him justice, he always
drank himself into a stupor after a job.

AS GOOD AS A HOLIDAY.
The main object of a holiday is to gain
renewed energy for another year’s work, so
that one may return with sound nerves and
a clear brain, full of vigor and ready for
work again. Unfortunately that object is
not always attained.
It all depends on the state of your blood.
If your blood has become impoverished or
impure the holiday is probably too brief for
you to obtain proper benefit: and very few
people can claim to be free from the dregs
of some ailment which has drained the blood
of its natural strength. That is why they
feel fagged out, brain-weary and incapable
of much exertion.
If you cannot take a holiday—begin now
to build. up your blood with a true tonic, and
so invigorate and refresh your system.
Nothing is more valuable for this purpose
than Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Over and
over again they .have been proved to be as
good as a holiday to jaded men and women.
Their value to both sexes is made evident by
the fact that these pills make good the waste
caused by illness and the wear and tear of
life.
Any reader of this paper may have a use-
ful booklet on “Building Up the Blood” by
writing to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Box 594 G.P.0., Sydney.—-Advt.

What is Talcum Powder?
TALCUM is an inorganic substance
—neither dampness, heat or perspi-
ration affect it. Rice, starch, and other
vegetable powders are organic— they
are affected by temperature and
moisture—perspiration sours them—
.when sour they become a source of
irritation and a breeding place for
infectious germs.
rrerirrerfs
Borated Talcum Powder
will not sour or ferment; and, medicated in the proper proportion,
covers baby’s skin with a protective coating against
threatening germs. You use Talcum Powder m
such unlimited quantities on the sensitive skin oi
your baby, that the selection of a pure,
safe powder is of great importance.
