M.L.A.—Must Live, Anyway.
H. G. Baylis, a draftsman of the N. S.
Wales Lands Office, who lately died sud-
denly at Eastwood, aged 58, was a son of
the one-time P.M. at Wagga who was
wounded in a fight with Morgan, the most
infamous of Australian bushrangers. The
brute was killed at last by a station-hand in
April, 1865.
Among the very few still-living Australian
lawyers admitted in the early ’sixties there
is one, J. E. Bowden, who is not only native-
born himself, but is the son of a Cornstalk.
His grandfather was that Thomas Bowden
who came to Australia in the earliest days
of last century as a school-teacher. The
veteran Six-and-Eightpence of this pioneer
family is now 82.
“Fatherdad”: The wife and I and our
seven children had to go out for half-an-hour
to look at our next home. The wife expected
her friend Maud. So she wrote a note, put
it in an envelope, and harpooned it to the
door with a hatpin. “Go in, Maud dear,” she
told her pal, “the door is not locked. We’ll
be home in less than an hour.” “Look here,”
I protested, “any burglar could read that,
you know.” “Oh, yes,” said the writer
sweetly, “but I've adaressed it to Maud!”
The Victorian Itailway bosses have just
put up a good record in Bed Tape stupidity.
A Melbourne mother, receiving news of the
death in action of her boy, was hurrying to
lur sister’s for comfort, when she heard the
demand, “Show tickets, please!” She then
found she was in a first-class carriage with
a second-class ticket, and, apparently, it did
no good explaining to the checker who dis-
covered the mistake that she wasn’t think-
ing about the sort of carriage she was in.
Then the dunderheaded Department hauled
her to the Footscray Court, and the best offer
its hired Gent, one could make was that
the case would be withdrawn on payment of
7s. Gd. costs. The mother paid the three
halfcrowns and left the court, while the
chairman of the bench made the Depart-
ment’s Gent. One feel miserable. But not
half so miserable as the bowelless clod who
was responsible for the prosecution ought to
be feeling.
“S.S.”: Re the alleged lamentable failure
of a picture management in Melbourne to
secure a live giant over 7ft., I snigger at
the assumption that this implies some, fall-
ing away in the Australian stock. The
giant is a sign of decay just as the dwarf
is Both are the product of degener-
ates. I know an undersized couple, both
afflicted, who have produced six children,
all approaching the gigantic, and all non-
producers in the racial sense. A giant is
the product of disease: certain nerve-centres
have failed to perform their functions; they
have lost control, and a freak is the result.
The defect that produces the giant is only
slightly different from that which produces
the dwarf, and in most cases nature has no
use for either. Giants have been conspicu-
ous failures in this war. An official opinion
from Flanders on some of the prodigies sent
there runs: “Each consumes enough for
four bantams, and is not worth one!” Aus-
tralia wants no giants—not even Billy
Hughes.
“Plumber” : This price-fixing is a fool of
a business unless the price-fixer owns or con-
trols the whole supply. Recently I wanted a
few sheets of plain galvanised iron to re-
bottom some 1000-gallon tanks. I went to
three iron-merchants from whom I buy, but
they said they had none. Getting wise I went
to another—to the iron yard, not the office.
Strolling in I spoke with the foreman about
the weather and the crops and other things,
and we went and liquidated. Finally he
poked me in the ribs. “How - much do you
want?” “Six sheets, 36 x 24.” “Can’t be
did.” “But you’ve got it. You're not keep-
ing it to look at.” “Straight, old man. -We
haven’t got it. We’ve only got 24 x 24.”
“Well, give me nine of them. I’m not
asking the price. Tell the boss I must have
it • I’ve promised to do the man’s tanks and
he’s a good customer.” Then we had “just
one more,” and I whispered (with a wink),
“Tell the boss I want ‘special’-—not the regu-
lation iron.” I got it, and paid cash, without
any invoice. It was, of course, the ordinary,
“fixed” at £56 a ton, but it cost the firm £75
to land here, and I paid a trifle more for my
little lot. Moreover, I could have sold it—
and 10 times as much—five minutes after
at 10 per cent, profit, but it wouldn’t have
paid me. I wanted it.
“A. N. Opheles” buzzes angrily:—
I rise from the stagnant water to jab a prong into
“J. Len” (B. 4/10/’l7) for his effort to contradict
the superior knowledge of “Te Babarantiko” regard-
ing the unhealthy climate of (late) German New
Guinea and New Britain. Malaria is the curse of
this rich and pleasant land, for if it could be eradi-
cated, along with its kindred diseases, filaria, hook
worm, dysentery and beriberi, the New Guineas,
British and Dutch, would be a very pleasant land
indeed. The diseases of civilisation, enteric, typhus,
diphtheria, plague, smallpox, etc., are practically
unknown. Malaria and other preventable tropical
diseases abound; yet with proper medical attention
and the introduction of sanitation and fresh food the
islands would rapidly become a safe place of settle-
ment for the white race. Under present conditions
they are unhealthy, and will remain so until the
various Governments accept the aid of science to
make them otherwise.
In my 20-odd years of tropical experience I have
seen some ghastly effects of disease, bad food and
imperfect hygiene. Take the founding of Finsch
Haven (late German New Guinea): 31 Huns arrived
there to make a settlement; at the end of the first
year only two of them were alive, and they bolted to
another site. Another instance: During the first five
years of the Mambare Goldfield (British New Guinea)
six magistrates died in harness, and the death-rate
of Europeans and their native boys reached 32 per
cent.!
Health in the tropics is a matter of food, housing
and hygiene. Given proper living, the white man
can toil hard in the shade ; but outdoor work under
the blazing sun in an atmosphere with 70 to 80
points of saturation is quite out of the question.
Statistics show that the man who retains good health
is the fellow whose occupation keeps him under cover
from the damaging light rays of the sun; the
missionary, wireless operator, clerk and other easy-
living folk escape most of the serious disease. The
ordinary bloke, the pioneer planter, trader, miner
and the administrative officers of the Governments
all go down to the climate and its diseases.

To My Son.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LITTLE son, when you are grown
Strong enougn to stand alone,
All vour mother’s love and tears
Cannot help you down the years.
All the sin your father knew
ides in wait to trap you, too.
Learn this lesson while you’re young—
Shut your ears and guard your tongue.
I have seen an idle jest
Strike a woman in the breast,
Kill the laughter in her eyes,
Twist the tone of her replies.
Bitter words, recalled too late,
Turn the tide of love to hate.
Guard your lips, my little son,
Lest you weep as I have done.
I have seen a wanton word.
Lightly uttered, lightly heard,
Garbled, twisted out of truth,
Rob a man of all his youth.
Broken faith and blinded eyes
Follow hard on senseless lies.
Son of mine, go shut your ears,
Lest you, too, should know my tears.
When you make a man a friend
Trust him blindly to the end.
Let no word or woman’s whim
Break the faith you have in him.
Sound the depth of feeble sneers—
Guard your lips and shut your ears.
Little son, learn Charity,
Lest you weep.. . .alone. . . .like me.
LONDON.
DION TITHERADGE.
Deirdre.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[Connor—his chroniclers spelt his name Conclni-
hor because they had lots of time —was the King of
L ister. Deirdre was the most beautiful woman in the
world, doomed under a curse to bring great sorrow.
She was to have been Connor’s wife, but ran awav
with the young Naisi. The legend has many end-
ings. This is what really happened.]
Act I.
A glen in the Antrim hills. Brown fir-
trees are on the hillside and a bright stream
tumbles down over the mossy stones. Far
up among the trees are the white walls of a
house. It is full summer-time, and the
ground is covered with yellow light on which
the shadows of the branches weave a pattern.
J 1 ar away is a field white with corn, and
beyoiid it a narrow glimpse of blue sea.
C onnor, a tall, bearded man in the prime of
life conies in.
Connor: It is here that they told me at
the house I would find her, but where is
she at all ?
He moves slowly among the tree-stems, and
then puts his hands to his mouth and gives
a hunting call. From the head of the glen
comes a light cry. Deirdre’s white dress
flutters among the rocksj and she comes
bounding down the steep place, her dark hair
floating around her and her white arms out-
spread. She is a slight girl with beautiful
dark eyes and an exquisite flower face, and
the richness of her dress, which is soiled and
torn with brambles, shows her to be a woman
of high rank.
Connor {going towards her): Little play-
mate, it is the whole summer you bring to
me in one sweet look.
Deirdre : Och, Connor, and me not dressed
for company! You big man, walking
always across the world, and me not
knowing when you will come.
She throws herself into liis arms and
laughingly kisses him on the mouth and eyes.
Connor ( holding her away from him and
looking into her face) : Sweetheart, it is
the wonder of all the stars in the night
sky that you have in your face surely.
Deirdre : It is the wonder of a woman’s
love I have in my heart for you, Connor;
and me thinking of you all the hours of
the fine day.
Connor ( sighing ) : Och, well, it is you that
are to make the sorrow of sharp swords;
but I do not care if they were all together
in my own heart when I have your kiss
on my mouth.
Deirdre : Och, Connor, is it always a
curse must come true?
Connor: How would a man know that till
the days have done playing with him?
But maybe a true love in a strong heart
will make a curse a little thing with only
a small blackness in it.
Deirdre : It is not surely for me that have
a man’s love to fear for myself what will
happen; but it is you that are a king and
must be walking among the swords
always.
Connor : Heart, I have a sharp sword
among sharp swords and a king’s word to
speak that is more than the swords.
Deirdre : It is strong you are and very
bold, Connor; but, och, a curse is a thin,
hard thing that might come when a strong
man was sleeping and a bold man too
tired to keep watch.
Connor: Sweetheart, what ails you? Is it
thinking of the curse of sorrow you are,
with the summer-time making laughter in
every tree?
Deirdre ; Och, well, I will forget, for you
are with me.
Connor : Little playmate, we will run up
together to the pool where the brown
trout are lying and talk about the good
days when, the kingdom is made sure, and
you are with me every white hour of the
sweet day.
A horn is heard in the ivoods.
Connor: What horn is that? Who is it
that would be hunting here?
Deirdre : There was a man from the back
hills that saw them in the first of the
morning. There were three tall boys and
four great hounds, and they had a deer
taken in the heather.
Connor : It would, maybe, be Naisi and his
two brothers that are come from Alban.
Och, well, he is a fine boy; and if he has
too much talk he is very young.
A magpie flutters on the ground in front
of them.
Deirdre : Och, Connor, look if there is
another. It is one alone that is no good
at all. There is another surely. One is
for sorrow. Och, surely there is a fellow
to it in the trees.
Connor (gazing among the trees ) : There is
another surely, but I cannot see it for the
sun that is laughing at our foolishness.
He tales her in his arms and hisses her.
The horn is heard again.
Act 11.
The same place later in the day. The horn
has blown three times. Deirdre comes down
the hill from the house. She is in evident
distress. Connor has gone, and the fear of
the curse is on her.
Deirdre : Och, Connor, Connor, what will I
do? Surely it is from me that the sorrow
is to come; and me loving you too much.
Och, my heart, if I could die and go away
and you would forget. But you would not
forget, my soul. Och, it would be sorrow
on sorrow, and you grieving for me
always and forgetting nothing. Is there
no way I could make you forget? Och/.
Connor, I love you too well to be smiling
at you and setting kisses on your mouth
to destroy you.
Naisi comes in. He is very young and boy-
ish, and he is striding up the hill past
Deirdre without seeing her ylien she speaks.
Deirdre: It is a fine evening to you, tall
huntsman.
Naisi (suddenly smitten with her amazing
beauty) : It is a fine—och, what are you
and where did you come from? Are you a
woman at all, or
Deirdre: I am Deirdre.
Naisi : Deirdre! The high gods be kind to
you, princess of the world. I am Naisi,
and my brothers are somewhere in the
woods above.
Deirdre: It is you are the fine man surely
and the great hunter among the trees.
Naisi : It is more than a hunter I am, bright
lady of this glen. I have fought with
swords in a king’s wars, and there is
nothing at all that I ain afraid of.
Deirdre: Och, maybe there is nothing you
are afraid of. (Lamghing.) But maybe
a very bold man would be afraid to put
his two arms about Deirdre that is to
bring the great sorrow and set the love-
kiss on her mouth.
Naisi : It is not courting I am, but hunting
the deer under the high trees and in the
great heather.
Deirdre : Och, you are too young. How
would you be bold enough, and you with
no beard?
Naisi : There is nothing at all in the green
world that can make me afraid.
Deirdre : There is nothing at all surely
except Deirdre, and her with a curse on
her that an old man spoke when he had,
maybe, drunk too much wine.
Naisi : I am not afraid.
Deirdre ( coming very close to him) : Kiss
me, bold man.
Naisi: Well, I will kiss you.
Deirdre: Och, tall man.
She throws her white arms about him.
Naisi kisses her and then staggers drunkenly
ate ay.
Deirdre : Och, tall man, come back to me.
Naisi (turning among the trees) : I will
come back very soon.
He walks on slowly with head bent, turn-
ing twice to look at her. Then he passes out
of sight.
Deirdre : Och, Connor, Connor, man of my
soul. It will be no curse of sorrow for
you, but for me only. Surely you will
think a hard thought of me and forget.
Och, man of my soul, forget!
Naisi comes running back.
Naisi : Deirdre!
Deirdre : It is not far you went nor very
fast.
Naisi : It is for a kiss I came back. I could
not go away.
Deirdre : Och, tall man, you are very bold.
Act 111.
A place in a rath overlooking the sea. It
is a long low room with a table. There are
sounds of storm without, and the room itself
is wrapped in deep gloom. Connor is pacing
to and fro. Fergus comes in.
Fergus : It is true the ivord that was
brought. She is gone to Alban with Naisi
and his two brothers, Ainnle and Ardan,
in a small boat.
Connor: Have you blown the horn to call
the fighting men together? Have you sent
word to Meath and Connaught? It is war
surely. Will a king be robbed in his own
land and him sitting with a clean sword?
Fergus : It is but three there are, and them
young boys. What war would there be to
fight?
Connor: Och, Fergus, it is the soul of me
is broken. What use would a sw r ord be
but to kill me only?
Fergus : It is a poor thing that a king would
break his own heart for a young girl that
is light in the head and foolish.
Connor: Was it a little boat. Fergus?
There is no small boat can go to Alban in
this wind. Look out on the sea. Look out
and tell me is there a boat.
Fergus goes out. Connor walks up and
down thrusting his hands through his lona
hair.
Connor : Och, Deirdre, Deirdre! And me
giving up the soul of me to the taste of
your red lips!
Fergus comes back.
Fergus : They are seen surely. There is a
boat coming in beyond the carrick. It will
be them safe from the sea.
Connor : Blow on the horns, Fergus. Bid a
thousand men with their small bows and
their sharp arrows come now to the shore.
Let every man have his sword ready in
his hand. It is the king’s war.
Fergus (laying a hand on his shoulder) :
Is it for three beardless boys you would
bring an army with calling of horns?
Connor (shaking him off) : Is it my word
that men obey? Blow on thte horns! It
is the kin'g’s war!

Connor goes out. Fergus looks after him
with a great sorrow in his eyes. Then he
sighs and goes out the other wag. Horns
are heard blowing.
Act IV.
The seashore beyond the carrick. A great
sea is rolling in, and Deirdre stands with
Naisi and his tivo brothers by the boat that
they have just dragged ashore. The wind is
loud and the slanting rain lashes them.
Deirdre : There is a great fear on me.
Connor is near this day ; and us between
his great anger and the green sea.
Naisi : There is no man I fear at all but
the sea only. I will speak the good word
to CoS-or; and if he will not hear me, let
us two fight for you with our swords.
Dierdre : Oeh, why did you come to this
place? It is the sea that is kind and only
on the land that there is sorrow.
She spreads out her hands and runs
towards the loaves. Naisi puts his arm
round her and draws her back.
Naisi : Och, woman, I have a sword; and
the most that the man can do is to kill
me here on the wet shore. Look; they
are coming.
Round the rocky point Connor and Fergus
are seen approaching. Naisi hides Dierdre
behind the boat, and with his two brothers
strides forward to meet the king. Fergus is
seen as if restraining Connor. Bowmen arid
warriors swarm over the rocks.
Naisi ( lifting his voice into a great shout) :
Is it Connor himself, and us blown in by
the great wind on his shore?
Connor: It is Connor himself, and him with
his hand on his sword. But would he
touch with a sword the thief that has a
neck to be hanged on a high tree?
Naisi : It is for Deirdre that is worth the
stroke of a man’s sword when she has
given him the love kiss.
He advances menacingly with his sword
drawn.
Connor : Men of the bows, draw your arrows
to the head. Would a king set dirt upon
his sword blade?
Fergus : Let no man draw his bow. Och,
Connor, surely it is mad you are with the
love of a woman that was light-headed for
a young boy. There is no man in Ire-
land will forget what you are doing this
day. .
Connor : It is the arrows that must speak
a king’s word. Men of the bows, let
them talk in the wind.
There is great confusion among the
crowded bowmen. Some obey Fergus, but
a hundred arrows fly at the king's word.
Deirdre springs up from her place of shelter.
At sight of her Connor utters a loud cry
and runs forwurd. The three brothers fall.
Deirdre, her dark hair streaming in the wind,
meets the shower of small arrows and falls
bleeding into Connor's arms.
Connor : Och, Deirdre, Deirdre!
Deirdre : Man of my soul, it was to lift
the curse of the sorrow. ( Panting for
breath.) And I have sent a young man
with no fault to his death.
Connor: Och, my woman, it is mad I am
with the thing that is done.
Naisi ( raising himself on one arm) : It is
dark and there are no stars....but I
have no fear.... I have kissed the beauty
of the world that had death in it.... I
have.... It is dark.... I am not afraid....
The bowmen and the armed men cluster
in trembling and horror about Fergus.
Fergus : It is the sorrow of the world.
Deirdre {in a failing voice) : Kiss me, Con-
nor.
DAVID McKEE WEIGHT.
How it Happened.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE had to find Black Beauty and her
calf....and there were three of us
to do it. That’s how the trouble
started.
The day was hot: which accounted for our
taking the track by the river, though, inci-
dentally, there were two good blood-sucker
logs and a new snake-hole along .that way.
But any design on or thought of the possi-
bilities of the big log-pile on the headland
never entered our minds; to that extent we
were innocent.
Of course, if you put us to the question,
we never really knew of a blood-sucker
sucking one’s blood. But then we never
really knew that one had never done it —
or mightn’t do it some time. There were
lots of things one couldn’t be sure of. They
used to say that Topsy was a quiet cow, the
quietest in the yard, and we believed it till
one day Frank and Jack Hunniford tried to
ride her at once while I drove her by the
tail, and she kicked me over and tossed them
both after she had bucked them off. So as
we couldn’t be sure about the blood-suckers,
and as we had heard no good of them, we
just went on calling them blood-suckers
because it sounded a good sort of name.
Several heroic attempts on the part of
one or other of us to pump enthusiasm into
the quest for Black Beauty did bring spas-
modic responses; but the lady was no ordi-
nary cow when it came to planting her
young calf. Anyway, we were sick of cows.
The trouble began with Lena. It always
did. But that comes later.
The first blood-sucker . log was a disap-
pointment. Mrs. Blood-sucker was not at
home to callers, and Mr. Blood-sucker was
evidently in poor spirits, for after flicking
his blue tongue at us in a half-hearted man-
ner once or twice he ran up the hole in the
log, and our fiercest taunts and insults,
that no self-respecting lizard could ignore,
brought no reappearance of the recreant.
With a final rasp of our sticks along his
wooden roof and the frank expression of
our opinion that they were cocktails, we
were going on peaceably; but Lena took
exception in her usual way to my manner
of speech towards lizards in general.
Reminding her that she was afraid of
them wasn’t mending matters ; but the snake-
hole took our attention just then. Mr. Snake
was sumiing himself outside liis new resi-
dence. He didn’t hoist any flags of defiance
and he wasn’t worth much as far as size
went; but snakes were snakes, and we
killed him on principle and went on, argu-
ing as we went over the possibility that
Beauty and her calf might have been over-
looked in a shady gully-bed some way back.
Opinions were still vexed when the second
blood-sucker log hove in sight. That couldn’t
be resisted. Frank and I promptly chal-
lenged the keepers of the castle in the recog-
nised fashion, and our challenge was as
promptly accepted.
Out came the fierce little black creatures,
bristling and darting their blue tongues with
as much assurance as if they were flaming
swords that could annihilate us. Excite-
ment, for two of us, at any rate, ran high.
Lena stood at a respectful distance
haranguing me on the old question of pro-
priety, and telling me that it wasn’t ladylike
to tease lizards, and she would tell mother.
That was too much ; “ladylike” was forever
in my ears in those days, when, if they had
only known it, my dreams ran to bushrang-
ing and such like, and mere recreations such
as lizard-teasing were only the result of the
sternest self-denial.
We chased her back to the bend with the
threat of a green frog down her back. We
were rid of her, and had almost decided to
look hard for Black Beauty, when alas! we
saw the logs. Why hadn’t we seen them
before? Or, rather, why hadn’t we seen the
possibilities of them?
There they lay, a little mountain of logs
on a high headland. Beneath, the river ran
lazy and deep, with scarce a curl to mark
the menace of the undertow. We looked
at one another, we looked at the logs and
the river. “Just the top one,” we said, and
meant it, I think, when we said it.
The weather-worn handspikes lay where
the men had thrown them. We seized them,
and, heavy as they were, we clambered up
the pile and fixed them in place for a good
purchase. Then the tussle began between
our determination and the weight of that
top log. We panted and Strained; at first
vainly, then hopefully, then triumphantly.
The great log heaved and paused while we
held our breath ; then with a grinding rush
it raced down the heap, struck the ground
with a hollow bump and went leaping down
the cliff. We watched it, fascinated.
Spl—a—sh ! and it swirled around head-
on to the current and shot downstream with
only its back showing, like a fat sea-serpent.
Ic was great —greater and more desirable
than anything in the whole world of tame
happenings! The quest for Black Beauty
and her calf was far off and long ago. Noth-
ing mattered only this stolen hour, this riot
of whirling logs and foaming water. For
us was neither heat nor weariness nor any
withdrawing—nothing but the roll and
bump, the grind and the long leap as we
sent monster after monster, like Leviathan
back to his Deep.
But the hour passed. We stood on the
headland very silent. Round our feet a few
beetles and things scuttled for cover from
where the last log had lain. The angry river
was creamed with foam-clots still, and past
the bend there came to us the faint palonka
palonk where some log jostled its fellows in
the stream.
It was far too late to find Black Beauty
now. It would be pitch dark before we
could make home. And then
Doggedly we faced about; some things
have to be done somehow. The scrub was
full of black shadows now, black, sneaking
things that crawled —or was it our guilty
consciences? A spotted native cat swore
openly at us as he sprang across our track
and vanished in his log. “Kagga gooka,” he
said in good native cat, which having no
equivalent in polite English I can only tact-
fully interpret as' “Gerout —who cares for
you f” But we went on sadly. We had done
with native cats.
We passed the blood-sucker’s log with
scarcely a glance. What did lizards matter,
after all? The little dead snake hung limply
on the stump where we had hung him ; but
somehow we felt no pride of victory. Per-
haps even he had the best of things when
all was said and done, for he was dead and
we were alive. And Black Beauty wasn’t
found. And.... round the furthest bend,
where the logs were driving seaward, was
the new pumping-plant that was to supply
the township with water!
MARIE E. J. PITT.
Finis.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE days and nights are fled, my dear.
The noons all droop into the mid-
night’s heart;
A pallid dawn comes whispering near —
Whispering that these, who lately loved,
now part.
Let us have done, my heart, have done!
In lavender and tears we’ll hide all this.
Our Paradise is lost and gone—
Lost even as this last, long, lonely kiss.
Time was I watched your face in fear,
And long and ardently; and well I know
The hair curls featly by your ear,
And how your fingertips are white as
snow.
Your eyes of moon-frail light are made;
All this I know, and well. The lattice, too,
Your fingers made for eyes that prayed
And called your angels down your dawn
for you.
Let be, belov&d, oh, let be!
The radiant nothings that were ours are
dead ;
Your eventides and noons for me
Shine nevermore. For now the sun dips
red;
And now the night falls, drear and old.
Listen! The far call cries adown the
sky
How cold these shadows grow—how cold!
Good-night, my heart! O Heart o’ me,
good-bye!
N.S.W.

SOMETHING
If Engineers and Steam-plant Owners would only
realise the importance of EFFICIENTLY insulating
tlieir Boilers, Steam Pipes, etc., .more attention might
he paid to this particular matter. Great economies
can be effected in this direction, in some cases the coal
consumption being reduced as much as 50%. But the
insulating material must he of the very best.
Carbonate of Magnesia is to-day recognised as the best
non-conductor of heat. Newall s Coverings are com-
posed of 85% of this material, and 1570 Asbestos—the
Asbestos being used for binding purposes only—and in
addition possess innumerable advantages over all other
forms of lagging. Newall’s Coverings are used, practi-
cally exclusively, by the British Admiralty, N'.S.W.
and Victorian Governments, and have been used
throughout the Ultimo. White Bay, Melbourne, Perth
and Canberra Power Stations.

The Outfit that “ Swept the Poll ” in the
Season.
F. D. McMASTER, Esq., Dalkeith, Cassilis, writes, under date 34th January:
“I have pleasure in stating that I have given both of the 3 Stand Portable
Crutching Plants I purchased from you, an exhaustive trial, and with the
utmost satisfaction. They are handy to move about, economical to run, and
the Fairbanks Morse Engine is simplicity itself. Crutching at the different
sheep-yards is, in my opinion, a far better method than doing the work at
the woolshed, as- the saving for the lambing ewes in driving alone is very
considerable.”
